Defining al-Qadim: The Eternal
Dardeer defines al-Qadim and negates origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of al-Qadim (the Eternal) in Sunni kalam and why it entails that Allah cannot be originated or a locus of originated things.

Core Idea
Al-Qadim means Allah is eternal without beginning, and nothing else coexists with Him.

In this passage, Imam Dardeer begins to define the essential attribute of qidam (eternity). He starts by negating what Allah must not be—originated or a locus of originated things—before affirming His positive attribute as the Eternal. This approach of negation then affirmation is typical of kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first a negation (what Allah is not), then an affirmation (what He is).
The parenthetical is likely a gloss explaining the negation.
2
Identify the two parts
Identify the negative part ('أن يكون حادثاً ...') and the positive part ('القديم ...'). Understand that al-Qadim is defined by both negating temporal attributes and affirming eternal ones.
3
Contrast with everyday usage
Reflect on how 'qadim' in Arabic often means 'old' or 'ancient'. In kalam, it means 'eternal with no beginning'. This is a key technical term.
The passage says 'الأول بلا بداية' – the First without beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يكون حادثاً أو محلا حادثاً أو محلا للحوادث (أي - تنزيه الباري جل جلاله عن الأمور الكائنة بعد عدم كالحركة والسكون).
القديم
- تنزيه الباري عن أن يكون معه غيره، حيث كان ولا شيء معه، هو الدائم الحي الباقي الأول بلا بداية الآخر بلا نهاية ليس قبله قبل ولا بعده بعد.
That He be originated, or a locus of an originated thing, or a locus for originated things (i.e., transcendence of the Creator, glory be to Him, from things that come into being after non-existence, such as motion and stillness). Al-Qadim: Transcendence of the Creator from there being any other with Him, when He was and nothing was with Him. He is the Eternal, the Living, the Everlasting, the First without beginning, the Last without end. There is no before Him 'before' and no after Him 'after'.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the attribute al-Qadim (the Eternal) as understood in Sunni kalam. The first line sets up the negative: it is impossible for Allah to be originated (hadith) or to be a locus of originated things (mahall li al-hawadith). The parenthetical clarifies that this means Allah transcends all temporal events like motion and stillness. Then the term 'al-Qadim' is introduced, and its meaning is given both negatively—that nothing else existed with Him—and positively—that He is the Eternal, Living, Everlasting, First without beginning, Last without end, beyond temporal categories of before and after. This is a standard kalam encapsulation of the divine attribute of qidam.

Key Terms
القديم (al-Qadim)
حادث (hadith)
محل للحوادث (mahall li al-hawadith)
تنزيه (tanzih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'al-Qadim' merely means 'old' or 'ancient'.
Correction: In kalam, al-Qadim means eternal with no beginning. The passage emphasizes 'first without beginning' and 'no before Him before'.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'al-Qadim' mean?
A) He is old in years
B) He is eternal, with no beginning and no end
C) He is the creator of time
D) He is the first to exist

Key Takeaway
Al-Qadim means Allah is eternal, without beginning or end, and nothing existed with Him before creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you worry about the future, do you remember that Allah is the Last without end and your time is in His hands? How does this truth reshape your anxiety today?
?
If you truly internalized that Allah was when nothing was with Him, how would that affect your attachment to created things like wealth, status, or relationships?
?
Do you live as if something exists alongside Allah, such as fear of people's opinions or love of worldly approval? What concrete step can you take today to negate that association in your heart?

Existence as a Self-Attribute (Ṣifa Nafsiyya)
Proof that existence is a necessary attribute of God and is called ṣifa nafsiyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence is considered a necessary self-attribute of God and how the argument from effect to cause supports this.

Core Idea
Every effect points to a cause; thus existence is inseparable from God's essence.

In this passage, Imam Dardeer begins his discussion of divine attributes by establishing the first necessary attribute: existence. He provides a simple yet profound proof based on the principle that every effect requires an efficient cause. This not only affirms God's existence but also introduces the concept of a 'self-attribute' (ṣifa nafsiyya), an attribute that is not separate from the essence. The passage then prepares us for the upcoming negative attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English versions to grasp the basic claim and its proof.
Notice the logical flow: claim → proof → naming → preview.
2
Understand the proof
Focus on the phrase 'every effect points to an efficient cause' and reflect on how this shows that existence is necessary for God.
Think of any created thing as an effect that points back to its Creator.
3
Learn the terminology
Note the terms 'self-attribute' (ṣifa nafsiyya) and the preview of five negative attributes, understanding what 'nafsiyya' means.
Remember: self-attribute = not superadded, but identical to essence in reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاعلم بأن الوصف بالوجود من واجبات الواحد المعبود إذ ظاهر بأن كل أثر يهدي إلى مؤثر فاعتبر وذي تسمى صفة نفسية ثم تليها خمسة سلبية
Know that the description with existence is among the necessary attributes (wājibāt) of the One, the Worshipped, for it is apparent that every effect (athar) points to an efficient cause (mu'aththir), so contemplate. And this is called a self‑attribute (ṣifa nafsiyya). Then after it come five negative attributes.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage argues that existence (wujūd) is a necessary attribute of God. The reasoning is that every effect (athar) in the world requires an efficient cause (mu'aththir). Since God is the cause of all effects, existence must be attributed to Him. This attribute is called ṣifa nafsiyya (self-attribute) because it is not a separate meaning added to the essence but is identical to the essence in terms of reality, though conceptually distinct. The passage then announces that after this self-attribute come five negative attributes (sifāt salbiyya) which will be enumerated next.

Key Terms
وصف بالوجود
واجبات
أثر
مؤثر
صفة نفسية
سلبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A self-attribute is separate from God's essence; it is an added quality like power or knowledge.
Correction: The term 'nafsiyya' means the attribute is not added to the essence but is identical to it in reality, though conceptually distinct. Existence is not a separate quality but the very reality of God.

Checkpoint
What is the first attribute mentioned in this passage?
A) Power (qudra)
B) Existence (wujūd)
C) Eternity (qidam)
D) Oneness (waḥdāniyya)

Key Takeaway
Existence is a necessary self-attribute of God, proven by the principle that every effect requires an efficient cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You see countless effects around you—your heartbeat, the rising sun, the order of the universe. Do you actually respond to these as signs pointing to a Necessary Cause, or do you take them for granted? What would change if you lived each moment as if every effect is a direct pointer to God's existence?
?
The passage says existence is a self-attribute, not separate from God's essence. Does your concept of God reflect this—that He doesn't merely 'have' existence but IS existence? If not, how might your worship change if you truly internalised that God's essence and existence are one?
?
If you truly believed that every effect necessitates an efficient cause, and that cause is God, would your sense of awe and dependence increase? Describe one specific situation today where you saw an effect and yet failed to lift your heart to the Cause. What will you do tomorrow to change that?

The Five Negative Attributes (Ṣifāt Salbiyya)
Enumeration of the five negative attributes (sifāt salbiyya) that negate imperfection from God

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five negative attributes that negate imperfection from Allah and how each one affirms divine perfection by denying its opposite.

Core Idea
Five negations that affirm Allah's perfection.

This passage from the Kharīda enumerates the five negative attributes that are essential for understanding what Allah is not. These attributes clear away any notion of imperfection, limitation, or similarity to creation. By negating what cannot apply to God, they indirectly affirm His absolute perfection. Let's explore each one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Let the author's own wording introduce the five attributes.
Notice the rhyming style (ʿajam) in the Arabic.
2
Identify each attribute and what it negates
List the five attributes: qidam, baqā', qiyām bi-nafsih, mukhālafah, waḥdāniyya. For each, state the imperfection it removes from Allah.
These are called 'negative' because they negate, not because they are passive.
3
Understand the phrase 'in essence or His high attributes'
The last attribute, oneness, is explicitly said to apply to both essence and attributes. Reflect on why this distinction matters.
It guards against assuming oneness only applies to the essence.
4
Reflect on the purpose of negative attributes
Consider why theology begins with negations. How do these attributes protect tawḥīd?
Negation clears the ground before affirming positive attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي القدم بالذات فاعلم والبقا قيامه بنفسه نلت التقى تخالف للغير وحدانية في الذات أو صفاته العلية
They are: qidam (pre‑eternity) in essence, so know, and baqā' (permanence), His self‑subsistence (qiyāmuhu bi‑nafsihī), you have attained piety, dissimilarity to others (mukhālafah li‑l-ghayr), and oneness (waḥdāniyya) in essence or His high attributes.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage lists the five negative attributes (al-ṣifāt al-salbiyya) which negate from God any imperfection or limitation. They are: (1) qidam (pre‑eternity) – God has no beginning; (2) baqā' (permanence) – God has no end; (3) qiyām bi‑nafsih (self‑subsistence) – God does not need a place or sustainer; (4) mukhālafah li‑l-ghayr (dissimilarity to others) – God is not like any created thing; (5) waḥdāniyya (oneness) – God is one in essence, attributes, and actions, with no partner or equal. The phrase 'in essence or His high attributes' indicates that oneness applies both to His essence and to His attributes.

Key Terms
قدم (qidam)
بقا (baqā')
قيامه بنفسه (qiyāmuhu bi-nafsihī)
تخالف للغير (mukhālafah li-l-ghayr)
وحدانية (waḥdāniyya)
صفات سلبية (sifāt salbiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'negative attributes' mean God lacks positive attributes.
Correction: Actually they affirm perfection by negating their opposites.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the five negative attributes mentioned?
A) Qidam (pre‑eternity)
B) Baqā' (permanence)
C) Qudra (power)
D) Waḥdāniyya (oneness)

Key Takeaway
The five negative attributes protect Allah's perfection by denying any imperfection or limitation in His essence, attributes, and actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah in your prayers, do you catch yourself assigning Him any form or location? How does knowing that He is self-subsistent (qiyāmuhu bi-nafsihī) and dissimilar to creation challenge your mental image? What concrete steps can you take to purify your tawhid?
?
Your life is filled with things that come and go—people, wealth, health. How does the fact that Allah is qidam (pre-eternal) and baqā' (permanent) confront your tendency to rely on temporary things? In what specific area do you need to shift your trust to the One who never ends?
?
You affirm the oneness of Allah's essence and attributes, but do your actions reflect that? Are there any 'partners' in your attention or devotion—status, comforts, relationships—that you place alongside Him? How can you align your heart's dependence with the waḥdāniyya you proclaim?

Why God Must Have Attributes
Proof of divine attributes via reductio, then praise and transcendence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that God must possess attributes and the description of His transcendence beyond all imperfections.

Core Idea
Denying attributes to God leads to absurdity; affirming them leads to glorification.

This passage from al-Kharidat al-Bahiyyah presents a concise yet powerful argument: if God lacked attributes, He would be temporal, which is impossible. The argument then moves to a sublime description of God's names and His transcendence (tanzih). Read closely to see how logical necessity and devotional praise are woven together.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the flow of argument.
Notice the conditional 'لو لم يكن' at the beginning.
2
Understand the reductio argument
The passage argues that if God were not described with attributes, He would be temporal. Since temporal origination is impossible for God, the antecedent must be false. The impossibility is further supported by leading to infinite regress and circularity.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum: denying attributes leads to an impossibility.
3
Analyze the positive and negative descriptions
The passage lists beautiful names (e.g., al-Jalīl, al-Jamīl) that affirm perfection, then negates imperfections like indwelling, direction, connection, separation, and foolishness. Reflect on how both affirmation and negation are needed to describe God properly.
The names are not arbitrary; they follow from the logical necessity of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو لم يكن متصفا بها لزم حدوثه وهو محال فاستقم لأنه يفضي إلى التسلسل والدور وهو المستحيل المنجلي فهو الجليل والجميل والولي والطاهر القدوس والرب العلي عن الحلول والجهة والاتصال الانفصال والسفة
If He were not described with them, His temporal origination would be necessary, which is impossible, so be upright. For it leads to infinite regress (al-tasalsul) and circular reasoning (al-dawr), which is the clear impossibility. So He is the Majestic (al-Jalīl), the Beautiful (al-Jamīl), the Guardian (al-Walīy), the Pure (al-Ṭāhir), the Holy (al-Quddūs), the Lord (al-Rabb), the Most High (al-'Alīy), free from indwelling (al-ḥulūl), direction (al-jiha), connection (al-ittiṣāl), separation (al-infiṣāl), and foolishness (al-safah).
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage provides a logical proof that God must be described with attributes (specifically the positive attributes of existence, eternity, etc.). If He were devoid of attributes, He would be non‑existent or temporal, which is impossible because that would require an infinite regress or circular reasoning (tasalsul or dawr). Both are absurd. The conclusion is that God is described with beautiful names (al‑Jalīl, al‑Jamīl, etc.) and is absolutely transcendent (tanzīh) beyond any imperfections such as indwelling (ḥulūl) in creation, direction (jiha), connection or separation, and even foolishness (safah). This affirms both positive attributes and negative transcendence.

Key Terms
متصف
حدوث
تسلسل
دور
الجليل
الجميل
الولي
الطاهر
القدوس
الرب
العلي
حلول
جهة
اتصال
انفصال
سفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is itself circular or invalid.
Correction: This is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming the opposite (no attributes) leads to an impossibility, so the original claim is proven. It is valid logic.

Checkpoint
What logical problem does the passage say would result if God were not described with attributes?
A) Anthropomorphism
B) Infinite regress (tasalsul) and circularity (dawr)
C) Existence of multiple gods
D) Denial of prophethood

Key Takeaway
God must be described with attributes of perfection and is absolutely transcendent beyond all imperfections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on God's attributes, do your thoughts match the affirmation and negation in this passage, or do you unknowingly strip God of His perfection by neglecting His beautiful names? Will you commit to learning and using these names in your du'ā?
?
The proof shows that denying attributes leads to impossibility. Have you ever felt uneasy about affirming that God has attributes because it seemed to limit Him? How does this passage correct that unease, and what step will you take to internalize this truth?
?
God is described as al-Jamīl (the Beautiful). Do you truly see His beauty in your life, even in trials? If not, what does that say about your understanding of His attributes? Pledge to look for His beauty today.

The Primary Attribute and the Five Negative Attributes
Author defines existence as necessary attribute and lists five negative attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that existence is the primary necessary attribute of God and be able to list the five negative attributes that remove imperfection.

Core Idea
Existence is the foundation; negatives perfect our understanding of God.

This lesson begins the study of divine attributes. The author first establishes that existence (wujūd) is a necessary attribute of God, proven by the causal argument. Then he introduces the five negative attributes (ṣifāt salbiyya) that negate any imperfection from God. This passage lays the groundwork for the entire discussion of God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the content.
2
Identify the proof for existence
Find the argument that every effect points to an agent. This establishes existence as a necessary attribute.
The author calls this a 'primary attribute' (ṣifa nafsiyya).
3
Understand 'primary attribute'
Note that the attribute of existence is positive, not negative. It is the basic attribute from which others derive.
The term 'nafsiyya' means it is inherent, not acquired.
4
List the five negative attributes
Read through the list: pre-eternity, everlastingness, self-subsistence, dissimilarity, and oneness. Understand what each negates.
These attributes remove imperfection from God.
5
Reflect on the overall purpose
Ask why these five are mentioned right after existence. They complement the positive attribute by clearing away all imperfections.
The phrase 'for the influence is only for the One' emphasizes God's exclusive action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاعلم بأن الوصف بالوجود من واجبات الواحد المعبود إذ ظاهر بأن كل أثر يهدي إلى مؤثر فاعتبر وذي تسمى صفة نفسية ثم تليها خمسة سلبية وهي القدم بالذات فاعلم والبقا قيامه بنفسه نلت التقى تخالف للغير وخدانية في الذات أو صفاته العلية والفعل فالتأثير ليس إلا للواحد القهار جل وعلا
Know that the description of existence is from the necessities of the One worshipped, for it is apparent that every effect guides to an agent, so consider. And this is called a primary attribute, then after it come five negative attributes: pre-eternity of essence, and everlastingness, and self-subsistence, and dissimilarity to others, and oneness in essence or His exalted attributes and action, for the influence is only for the One, the All-Dominant, exalted and high.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage defines the first attribute of God, which is existence (al-wujūd). The author states that the description of existence is among the necessities (wājibāt) of God. He provides a proof: every effect points to an agent. Then he mentions that this attribute is called 'primary' (nafsiyya), meaning it is a positive attribute. After that, he lists the five negative attributes (ṣifāt salbiyya) that remove imperfection: (1) pre-eternity of essence (qidam al-dhāt), (2) everlastingness (baqā'), (3) self-subsistence (qiyām bi al-nafs), (4) dissimilarity to creation (mukhālafa li al-ghayr), and (5) oneness (waḥdāniyya) in essence and attributes. He also includes action (al-fi'l) as part of the negation, emphasizing that only God truly acts.

Key Terms
صفة نفسية
الصفات السلبية
القدم
البقاء
القيام بالنفس
مخالفة للحوادث
الوحدانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the five negative attributes contradict the positive attributes of God.
Correction: The negative attributes do not contradict the positive ones; they complement them by removing any imperfection, thus perfecting our understanding of God's nature.

Checkpoint
What does the phrase 'الصفة النفسية' refer to in this passage?
A) A negative attribute
B) The attribute of existence
C) The attribute of power
D) The attribute of will

Key Takeaway
Existence is the foundational positive attribute, and the five negative attributes protect God from any imperfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does realizing that every effect points to an agent challenge you to see God's active role in every detail of your life today? Will you consciously look for His hand in the events of your day?
?
The five negative attributes strip away any imperfection from God. Which of those attributes (pre-eternity, everlastingness, self-subsistence, dissimilarity, oneness) confronts you most with your own limitations? How will you humble yourself before Him in response?
?
The passage ends by stating that influence (التأثير) belongs only to God. In what areas of your life are you still acting as if your own influence is independent of Him? What can you do to surrender that illusion today?

The Seven Attributes of Meaning
Enumerates and explains the seven attributes of meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven attributes of meaning in Sunni theology, their attachment to all things, and their eternity by essence.

Core Idea
God's seven essential attributes are eternal and inseparable from His essence.

Imam al-Dardīr now turns to the attributes that are not merely negative or relational but positive meanings subsisting in God's essence. These seven attributes—knowledge, power, will, life, speech, hearing, and sight—are the core of Sunni divine attribute theology. In this passage, he enumerates them and clarifies key points about their scope and eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with 'ثم المعاني سبعة' indicating a list.
2
Identify the seven attributes
List out the seven attributes mentioned: knowledge, power, will, life, speech, hearing, sight. See how each is described.
Pay attention to the distinction between intention and command.
3
Understand the attachment rules
The passage specifies which attributes attach to which categories: knowledge and speech to all divisions, power and will to possible things, hearing and sight to existing perceived things. Note that life is excepted from perpetual attachment in a certain sense.
Life is eternal but its 'attachment' is unique; it is the basis for the other attributes.
4
Grasp the eternity by essence
The passage concludes that all these attributes are eternal by essence because they are not other than the essence. Reflect on the meaning of 'qadīma bi al-dhāt'.
This is the key Sunni stance against those who say attributes are created or separate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم المعاني سبعة للرائي أي علمه المحيط بالأشياء وقدرته إرادة وكل شيء كائن أراده وإن يكن بضده قد أمرا فالقصد غير الأمر فاطرح المرا حياته فقد علمت أربع أقساما في الكائنات فاحفظ المقاما كلامه والسمع والإبصار فهو الإله الفاعل المختار وواجب تعليق ذي الصفات حتما دواما ما عدا الحياة فالعلم جزمًا والكلام السامي تعلقا بسائر الأقسام تعلقا بالممكنات كلها أخا التقى وقدرة إرادة واجزم بأن سمعه والبصرا تعلقا بكل موجود يرى وكلها قديمة بالذات لأنها ليست بغير الذات
Then the attributes of meaning are seven for the seeing one: His science encompassing all things, and His power, will. And every thing that occurs He willed, and if He may have commanded its opposite, then the intention is different from the command, so discard the controversy. His life. For you have known four divisions among contingent things, so preserve the station. His speech and hearing and sight. So He is the God, the Agent, the Chooser. And it is necessary that these attributes be attached perpetually always except life. Then science definitely and lofty speech are attached to all other divisions. Attachment to all possible things, O brother of piety, and power and will. And be certain that His hearing and sight are attached to every existent that is seen. And all of them are eternal by essence because they are not other than the essence.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents the seven attributes of meaning (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) which are distinct from the negative attributes. The author lists them: knowledge (ʿilm), power (qudra), will (irāda), life (ḥayāt), speech (kalām), hearing (samʿ), sight (baṣar). He explains that knowledge encompasses all things, and everything that happens is willed by God. He distinguishes between God's intention and His command, noting that they can differ. Life is mentioned separately. Then he mentions that knowledge and speech are attached to all divisions (i.e., all categories of beings), and power and will are attached to all possible things. Hearing and sight are attached to all existents that are perceived. Finally, he states that all these attributes are eternal by essence (qadīma bi al-dhāt) because they are not other than His essence.

Key Terms
صفات المعاني
العلم
القدرة
الإرادة
الحياة
الكلام
السمع
البصر
قديمة بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that life is excluded from being eternal or attached perpetually.
Correction: Life is eternal and essential, but it is not 'attached' in the same manner as other attributes because it is the substratum for them; the passage says 'except life' meaning it is not attached in the sense of being added separately, but it is the basis.

Checkpoint
How many attributes of meaning are mentioned in this passage?
A) Five
B) Seven
C) Four
D) Six

Key Takeaway
The seven attributes of meaning are eternal by essence, attached to all beings appropriately, and inseparable from God's nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this distinction expose the condition of your heart? What will you do today to align your inner will with His?
?
The attributes are eternal and inseparable from God, meaning His knowledge and power are constantly with you. Do you truly live with the awareness that God's knowledge encompasses your every thought and deed? What specific habit can you change this week to act in the light of His all-encompassing presence?
?
Life (ḥayāt) is the foundational attribute among the seven. In what areas of your spiritual life are you neglecting this foundation—your connection to the Living God? Commit to one concrete action today to revitalize your spiritual life so that your knowledge, will, and actions reflect His eternal attributes.

Divine Speech and the Impossibility of Opposite Attributes
Clarifies that divine speech is not letters/arrangement and opposite attributes impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine speech is not composed of letters or arrangement like human speech, and that opposite attributes are impossible for Allah.

Core Idea
Divine speech is not letters; opposite attributes are impossible.

This passage from Imam Dardeer's commentary clarifies two fundamental points about Allah's attributes. First, His speech (al-kalām) is unlike human speech—it is not made of letters or sequential sounds. Second, it explains why opposite attributes cannot be ascribed to Allah. Understanding these points deepens our grasp of tawḥīd and the uniqueness of Allah's perfect attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's words come through clearly.
Notice the concise style of the original.
2
Identify the two claims
The passage makes two distinct points: about divine speech and about opposite attributes. Find each and note how they are connected.
The second claim builds on the first.
3
Understand the reason
The author gives a brief proof: if Allah lacked these attributes, He would be known by imperfection. Reflect on why that is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الكلام ليس بالحروف وليس بالترتيب كالمألوف ويستحيل ضد ما تقدما من الصفات الشامحات فاعلما لأنه لو لم يكن موصوفا بها لكان بالسوى معروفا
Then speech is not by letters and not by arrangement as is customary, and the opposite of what preceded from the sublime attributes is impossible, so know. Because if He were not described with them, He would be known by what is other than Him.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage addresses the nature of divine speech. The author states that God's speech (al-kalām) is not composed of letters (ḥurūf) nor arranged in sequence (tartīb) like human speech. Then he asserts that it is impossible for God to have attributes opposite to those already mentioned (i.e., the negative and positive attributes). The reason: if God were not described with these attributes, He would be known by something else (i.e., imperfection), which is not fitting. Therefore, these attributes are necessary.

Key Terms
الكلام
الحروف
الترتيب
ضد الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Divine speech is composed of letters and arranged like human speech.
Correction: Divine speech is not by letters nor arrangement; it is an eternal attribute unlike created speech.

B) As not letters nor arrangement

D) As silence

Key Takeaway
Divine speech is not composed of letters or arrangement, and it is impossible for Allah to have opposite attributes, as that would imply imperfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

When you pray to Him, do you truly believe that His hearing, seeing, and speech are unlike any creation? How does this truth change the way you whisper to Him in du'a? Decide now to speak to Him with the awe that comes from knowing His attributes are sublime and incomparable.

The Seven Divine Attributes and Their Eternality
Enumeration of seven divine attributes and affirmation of their eternality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven essential divine attributes in Ash'ari theology, how they relate to God's essence, and why they are eternal.

Core Idea
God's seven attributes are eternal and identical with His essence.

This passage from Imam Dardir's poem enumerates the seven divine attributes that are essential to God's nature. It explains how each attribute relates to its objects and concludes with the crucial doctrine that these attributes are eternal 'by essence' — that is, they are not separate from God but are His very reality. Let's work through the verse step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic verse slowly. Then read the full English translation. Let the poetic rhythm and key terms settle in your mind.
The poem uses a concise style typical of Islamic didactic poetry.
2
Identify the seven attributes
Notice the seven attributes mentioned: knowledge, power, will, life, speech, hearing, and sight. These are the 'meanings' (ma'ani) referred to in the first line.
In Ash'ari theology, these are considered essential attributes (sifat dhatiyya).
3
Understand the distinction between will and command
The passage distinguishes between God's will (irada) and His command (amr). God may will something to happen even if He commands its opposite, showing that will is independent of command.
This refutes the idea that God's will is merely His command.
4
Grasp the attachment of attributes
Each attribute attaches to its objects: knowledge and speech to all categories, power and will to all possibilities, hearing and sight to every existent. Life is eternal and attaches perpetually.
This explains how attributes relate to creation without change in God.
5
Absorb the conclusion: attributes are eternal by essence
The final lines assert that all seven attributes are 'eternal by essence' (qadima bi al-dhat) because they are not other than the essence itself. They are neither created nor separate.
This is a key Ash'ari doctrine against those who claim attributes are accidents or separate entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ المعاني سبعة للرائي * أي: عِلْمُهُ المحيط بالأشياء وقذرة، إرادة * وكُلّ شَيءٍ كائن أراده وإنْ يكُنْ بضده قدْ أمَرا * فالقَصْدُ غَيْرُ الأمرِ فاطرَح المرا * حياته، * وإن * فقد علمتَ أربعًا أقسامًا * في الكائنات، فاحفظ المقاما * كلامه والسمع والإبصار * فهوَ الإله الفاعل المختار * وواجب تعليقُ ذي الصِّفاتِ * حتما دواما ما عدا الحياة * فالعلم جزمًا والكَلامُ السامي * تعلقا بسائر الأقسام * تعلقاً * بالمُمْكِناتِ كُلِّها أخا التقى وقدرة إرادة * واجزِمْ بأَنَّ سَمْعَهُ والبصرا * تعلَّقاً بِكُلِّ مَوجُودٍ يُرى * وكُلُّها قديمة بالذات * لأنها ليستْ بغَيرِ الذات
Then the meanings are seven for the seer * i.e.: His knowledge encompassing things and power, will * And everything that occurs He willed, even if He commanded its opposite * So intention is not command, so abandon contention * His life, and * Indeed you have known four divisions * in contingent things, so preserve the station * His speech, hearing and sight * So He is the acting, choosing God * And obligation of attaching the attributes * necessarily perpetually except life * So knowledge decisively and the sublime speech * attach to the rest of the divisions * attachment * to all possibilities, O possessor of piety, and power and will * And be certain that His hearing and sight * attach to every existent that is seen * And all of them are eternal by essence * because they are not other than the essence.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage enumerates the seven divine attributes (knowledge, power, will, life, speech, hearing, sight) and establishes their eternality. It begins by stating that there are seven 'meanings' (attributes) for the seer (the one who reflects), then explains each: God's knowledge encompasses all things; His will applies to everything that occurs, even if He commands its opposite (distinguishing will from command); His life is eternal; and the attributes of speech, hearing, and sight are affirmed. The passage then notes that these attributes attach to their respective objects: knowledge and speech to all categories of existence, power and will to all possibilities, hearing and sight to every existent that is perceived. Finally, it asserts that all these attributes are eternal 'by essence' (qadīma bi al-dhāt) because they are not other than the essence itself, refuting any notion that they are accidental or created.

Key Terms

العلم
الإرادة
القدرة
الحياة
الكلام
السمع
البصر
قديمة بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the divine attributes are separate entities added to the essence, or that they are created.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the attributes are 'eternal by essence because they are not other than the essence', affirming their identity with God.

Checkpoint

A) Three attributes, and they are created.

D) Nine attributes, and they are temporal.

Key Takeaway
The seven divine attributes are eternal and identical with God's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Commit to one specific way you will align your heart with His will today.

Divine Speech: Not Letters, Not Sounds
Affirms that God's speech is not composed of letters or order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine speech (al-kalām) is an eternal attribute of Allah, not composed of letters or sounds like human speech.

Core Idea
God's speech is eternal; it is not made of letters or arranged in order.

In this passage, Imam Dardīr clarifies a crucial point in Islamic theology: the nature of Allah's attribute of speech. Many mistakenly conceive of God's speech as similar to human speech, consisting of words and sounds. The author firmly negates that and then explains why denying this attribute would lead to a logical contradiction. Read on to see the precise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the original Arabic lines slowly. Notice the negations 'laysa bi al-hurūf' and 'laysa bi al-tartīb'.
The rhyme scheme helps memorization.
2
Understand the negation
The passage first negates that divine speech consists of letters or sequence. This distinguishes it from created speech.
This is a key Ash'ari position.
3
Grasp the logical consequence
The second part argues that if God were not described with these attributes, He would be known by something other than Himself—which is impossible.
This shows the necessity of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ الكلامُ لَيسَ بالحروفِ * ولَيسَ بالترتيب كالمألوف ويستحيلُ ضِدُّ ما تقدما * مِنَ الصِّفاتِ الشامِحَاتِ فاعلَما * لأنه لو لم يكُنْ مَوصوفا * بها، لكان بالسوى معروفا
Then speech is not with letters * nor with order as is customary. And the opposite of what preceded is impossible * of the sublime attributes, so know. * Because if He were not described * with them, He would be known by other than (Himself).
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage clarifies the nature of divine speech (al-kalām). It states that God's speech is not composed of letters or arranged in a sequence like human speech. Then it asserts that it is impossible for the opposite of the previously mentioned attributes to exist in God—meaning that since He is described with these attributes (knowledge, life, hearing, sight, etc.), their opposites cannot be attributed to Him. The reason given is that if God were not described with these attributes, He would be known by something other than Him (i.e., His attributes define Him, so denying them would reduce Him to something else). This is a key argument in Ash'ari theology for the necessity of eternal attributes.

Key Terms
الكلام
الحروف
الترتيب
الصفات الشامحات
السوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that God's speech is made of letters and sounds, like the created Qur'an or human speech.
Correction: Divine speech is an eternal attribute, not composed of letters or order; the Qur'an is the expression of that speech in created letters.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is divine speech NOT?
A) Eternal and attribute of God.
B) Composed of letters and order.
C) Attached to God's essence.
D) Known through revelation.

Key Takeaway
Divine speech is an eternal attribute of Allah, not composed of letters or sequence, and its denial would lead to a logical absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you truly believed that Allah's speech is unlike any created speech, how would you listen to a verse that commands or prohibits something? Would your heart respond differently? Commit now to a specific action that shows you accept the divine authority of the Qur'an.

God's Absolute Independence
The matn asserts God's independence and the neediness of all else.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that only God is truly independent and self-sufficient, and that everything else is inherently needy.

Core Idea
Only God is worthy of being called Self-Sufficient.

This opening passage of the poem establishes the foundation of tawhid: God's absolute independence. Before discussing God's actions or attributes, we must first affirm that He alone is al-Ghani, the Self-Sufficient, and that all creation stands in need of Him. The lines use a logical argument to invert the common notion of independence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the contrast between 'independent' and 'needy'.
2
Understand the logical structure
The first line presents a paradox: anything that claims independence is actually at the utmost neediness. Only God is truly independent.
3
Reflect on the divine names
The passage ends with two names: al-Ghani (Self-Sufficient) and al-Muqtadir (Powerful). Contemplate how these attributes relate to God's independence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متن المنظومة
وكل من قام به سواها به سواها * فهو الذي في الفقر قد تناهى
والواحد المعبود لا يفتقر * لغيره، جل الغني المقتدر
وجائز في حقه الإيجاد * والترل والإشقاء والإسعاد
ومن يقل: فعل الصلاح وجبا * على الإله قد أساء الأدبا
واجزم أخي برؤية الإله * في جنة الخلد بلا تناهي
إذ الوقوع جائز بالعقل * وقد أتى فيه دليل النقل
Text of the poem:
And everyone who is independent of it by its own [؟] is the one who has reached the utmost in neediness.
And the One worshipped does not need anything other than Him; exalted is the Self-Sufficient, the Powerful.
And it is possible for Him to bring into existence, and to [descend?], and to cause misery, and to cause happiness.
And whoever says: 'Doing what is good is obligatory upon God' has indeed shown bad manners.
And be certain, my brother, of seeing God in the Garden of Eternity without end,
since its occurrence is possible by reason, and textual evidence has come concerning it.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a series of doctrinal statements from the matn of al-Kharida, covering foundational Ash'ari teachings on God's attributes. The first line declares that anything that is independent of God is actually at the utmost degree of neediness—only God is truly independent. The second line affirms that God, the One worshipped, does not need anything else; He is al-Ghani (the Self-Sufficient) and al-Muqtadir (the Powerful). The third line states that it is possible (ja'iz) for God to create, to cause misery and happiness, and also to 'descend' (if that is the correct reading) as an act of His will—this asserts that God's actions are not necessary but stem from His free choice. The fourth line refutes the Mu'tazilite position that God is obligated to do what is best (al-salah), calling it bad manners towards God. The final two lines affirm the vision of God (ru'yat Allah) in Paradise, saying it is possible rationally (bi al-'aql) and confirmed by transmitted evidence (dalil al-naql), thus countering those who deny it literally.

Key Terms
الغني
المقتدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that creation can be independent in some aspects.
Correction: The passage refutes this by asserting that any claimed independence is actually utter neediness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the state of anyone who claims independence from God?
A) They are truly free.
B) They are at the utmost neediness.
C) They are equal to God.
D) They are self-sufficient.

Key Takeaway
Only God is truly independent; all else is completely needy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which area of your life do you most often feel self-sufficient in, acting as if you don't need Allah? Will you honestly name that area and commit to a daily reminder of your need for Him?
?
The matn says that anyone independent of Allah is actually in extreme need. How does your pride react to that? Do you truly believe you are needy in every breath, or do you act as though you can manage without Him? What one change can you make today to live that belief?
?
If Allah removed His power from your life for just a second, you would cease to exist. Are you living with that daily awareness? What practical habit can you adopt to remind yourself of your absolute dependence on al-Ghani and al-Muqtadir?

God's Freedom in Action
The matn states God's possible actions and refutes the obligation to do what is best.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's actions are not necessitated; He freely creates, causes misery and happiness, and is not obligated to do what is best.

Core Idea
God's actions are possible, not obligatory.

Having affirmed God's independence, the poem now addresses the nature of God's actions. It asserts that it is possible (ja'iz) for God to bring things into existence, to cause misery and happiness, and it refutes the Mu'tazilite doctrine that God must do what is most beneficial (al-salah). This lesson clarifies that God's will is not constrained by any external obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic lines about possible actions and the refutation of obligation.
Note the word 'وجائز' – it is possible, not necessary.
2
Understand 'ja'iz' in divine acts
In kalam, 'ja'iz' means rationally possible. For God, this indicates that His actions are not necessitated by His essence; He acts by free choice.
3
Analyze the refutation
The line condemning those who say 'doing good is obligatory' teaches that we should not impose duties on God, as it shows bad manners.
This is a direct attack on the Mu'tazilite position.

The Vision of God in Paradise
The matn affirms the vision of God in Paradise as rationally possible and textually established.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that seeing God in Paradise is rationally possible and confirmed by revelation, establishing the Ash'ari position on ru'ya.

Core Idea
Seeing God in the afterlife is both possible and promised.

The final two lines of this passage address the controversial topic of ru'yat Allah (vision of God). The poem affirms that believers will see God in Paradise eternally, that this is rationally possible, and that revealed texts support it. This counters those who deny literal vision, such as the Mu'tazilites.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic lines about seeing God in Paradise.
Pay attention to the phrase 'بلا تناهي' – without end.
2
Distinguish rational and transmitted evidence
The passage says 'إذ الوقوع جائز بالعقل وقد أتى فيه دليل النقل' – it is possible rationally and confirmed by textual evidence.
This shows the Ash'ari approach of using both reason and revelation.
3
Connect to theological context
Understand that this affirms the beatific vision against those who deny it.

Source Text

ومن يقل: فعل الصلاح وجبا * على الإله قد أساء الأدبا
واجزم أخي برؤية الإله * في جنة الخلد بلا تناهي
إذ الوقوع جائز بالعقل * وقد أتى فيه دليل النقل
Text of the poem:
And everyone who is independent of it by its own [؟] is the one who has reached the utmost in neediness.
And the One worshipped does not need anything other than Him; exalted is the Self-Sufficient, the Powerful.
And it is possible for Him to bring into existence, and to [descend?], and to cause misery, and to cause happiness.
And whoever says: 'Doing what is good is obligatory upon God' has indeed shown bad manners.
And be certain, my brother, of seeing God in the Garden of Eternity without end,
since its occurrence is possible by reason, and textual evidence has come concerning it.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a series of doctrinal statements from the matn of al-Kharida, covering foundational Ash'ari teachings on God's attributes. The first line declares that anything that is independent of God is actually at the utmost degree of neediness—only God is truly independent. The second line affirms that God, the One worshipped, does not need anything else; He is al-Ghani (the Self-Sufficient) and al-Muqtadir (the Powerful). The third line states that it is possible (ja'iz) for God to create, to cause misery and happiness, and also to 'descend' (if that is the correct reading) as an act of His will—this asserts that God's actions are not necessary but stem from His free choice. The fourth line refutes the Mu'tazilite position that God is obligated to do what is best (al-salah), calling it bad manners towards God. The final two lines affirm the vision of God (ru'yat Allah) in Paradise, saying it is possible rationally (bi al-'aql) and confirmed by transmitted evidence (dalil al-naql), thus countering those who deny it literally.

Key Terms
رؤية الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God cannot be seen because He is not a body.
Correction: The passage asserts that it is rationally possible and confirmed by revelation, though we do not know the modality.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what evidence supports the vision of God?
A) Only reason
B) Only revelation
C) Both reason and revelation
D) Neither reason nor revelation

Key Takeaway
The vision of God in Paradise is a certain belief, supported by reason and revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine the greatest joy you could ever experience, does seeing Allah with your own eyes come to mind? If not, why does your heart not yearn for what the poem calls a certainty?
?
You intellectually affirm that ru'ya is possible—but does that belief make you restless for the afterlife, or have you grown comfortable with a faith that only lives in your head?
?
The poem commands: 'Be certain.' Are there hidden doubts in your heart that deny the reality of seeing Allah? What would need to change in your life for that certainty to take root?

Deriving 'ar-Rahman' and 'ar-Rahim' from a Transitive Verb
Explaining the derivation of 'ar-Rahman' and 'ar-Rahim' as sifa mushabbaha from a transitive verb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why 'ar-Rahman' and 'ar-Rahim' are classified as attributive adjectives (sifa mushabbaha) and how the transitive verb 'rahima' is treated as intransitive to form them.

Core Idea
Even transitive mercy must be grammatically treated as intrinsic to Allah.

Sanusi addresses a grammatical challenge: the names 'ar-Rahman' and 'ar-Rahim' are built for exaggeration from the verb 'rahima', but the verb is transitive while the required form imposes intransitivity. This lesson shows how transitivity is resolved so that mercy becomes an unqualified, permanent attribute of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammatical issue
Note that 'rahima' is a transitive verb, but 'sifa mushabbaha' is only derived from intransitive verbs. This creates a tension.
2
Understand the two solutions
The passage offers two ways to treat the verb as intransitive: either ignore the object or change the verb form to 'fa'ula'. Read each explanation carefully.
3
Connect to the definition of sifa mushabbaha
The footnote defines sifa mushabbaha as a permanent quality derived from intransitive verbs. See how 'karim' is an example.
4
Apply to divine attributes
Reflect that this grammatical treatment ensures that Allah's mercy is seen as an intrinsic, boundless attribute, not dependent on its objects.
This aligns with the doctrine that Allah's attributes are eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و الرحمن الرحيم»: صفتان مُشَبَّهتان، بُنيتا للمُبالغة من رحم بالكسر، إما بتنزيله منزلة اللازم بأنْ يُقْصَدَ إِثْباته للفاعل فقَطْ مِنْ غَير اعتبار تعلقه بمفعول، وإما بجعله لازما بأنْ يُنْقَلَ إلى «فعل» بالضم، وإنّما احتيج لأنَّ الصِّفةَ المَشَبَّهَةَ إِنَّمَا تُصَاغَ مِنَ اللازم. (الصفة المشبهة سميت بذلك تشبيها لها باسم الفاعل، وهي ما صيغ من فعل لازم لمن قام به الفعل على معنى الثبوت مثل «كريم»، ولما كان رَحِم فعلا متعديًا، والصفة المشبهة لا تصاغ إلا من فعل لازم، أنزلها منزلة اللازم بأن يقصد إثباته للفاعل فقط، من غير اعتبار تعلقه بمفعول أو بجعله لازما على صيغة «فَعل» بالضم.) (هذا مشكل الحصرهم صيغ المبالغة في خمسة أوزان، وهي: فعّال، ومفعال، وفعول، وفعيل، وفعل، وليس رحمن على واحد منها ؟! فالجواب أنه مفيد للمبالغة بمعناه لا بصيغته وليس المراد بالمبالغة أن ينسب للشيء أكثر مما هو له، فذلك منتف في حقه تعالى إذ قدرته لا تتناهى، وإنما المراد المبالغة النحوية، أي كثرة معانيها وكثرة متعلقاتهما مع بقاء المعنى الأول.)
And 'ar-Rahman' and 'ar-Rahim' are two attributive adjectives (sifatan mushabbaha-tan), built for exaggeration (mubalagha) from the verb 'rahima' (to have mercy) with kasra. Either by treating it as intransitive (lazim) such that only the affirmation of the attribute for the subject is intended, without consideration of its connection to an object; or by making it intransitive by changing it to the form 'fa'ula' (with damm). The reason for the need is that the attributive adjective (sifa mushabbaha) is only derived from an intransitive verb. (The attributive adjective is so called because it resembles the active participle, and it is what is derived from an intransitive verb for the one who performs the action, with the meaning of permanence, like 'karim'. Since 'rahima' is a transitive verb, and the attributive adjective is only derived from an intransitive verb, they treated it as intransitive either by intending its affirmation for the subject only, regardless of its connection to an object, or by making it intransitive by changing it to the pattern 'fa'ula' with damm.) (There is a problem that they restricted the patterns of exaggeration to five forms: fa''al, mif'al, fa'ul, fa'il, and fa'l, and 'Rahman' is not among them. The answer is that it conveys exaggeration by its meaning, not by its pattern. And what is meant by 'exaggeration' is not attributing more to something than it actually possesses, for that is impossible for Him, exalted, since His power is infinite; rather what is intended is grammatical exaggeration, i.e., the abundance of its meanings and the abundance of its related aspects while preserving the original meaning.)
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage discusses the grammatical form of the divine names 'ar-Rahman' and 'ar-Rahim'. They are classified as 'sifa mushabbaha' (attributive adjectives) built for 'mubalagha' (exaggeration) from the verb 'rahima'. The challenge is that 'sifa mushabbaha' is normally derived from intransitive verbs, but 'rahima' is transitive. The solution is either to treat the verb as if it were intransitive by focusing only on the subject's possession of the attribute, ignoring the object, or to change the verb to the intransitive pattern 'fa'ula'. The first footnote clarifies the definition of 'sifa mushabbaha': it resembles the active participle but indicates a permanent quality, like 'karim' (generous). Since 'rahima' is transitive, they adjust it. The second footnote addresses a secondary issue: 'Rahman' does not fit the five standard patterns of exaggeration, but it still conveys intensity through its meaning, not its morphological pattern. It also clarifies that 'mubalagha' in relation to Allah does not mean attributing more than deserved, because Allah's attributes are infinite; rather it is a grammatical tool to express abundance.

Key Terms
الرحمن
الرحيم
صفة مشبهة
مبالغة
لازم
متعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'ar-Rahman' and 'ar-Rahim' are simple active participles with no special grammatical consideration.
Correction: They are sifa mushabbaha, requiring the transitive verb to be treated as intransitive to convey permanence and intensity.

Checkpoint
Why must 'rahima' be transformed to form 'ar-Rahman' and 'ar-Rahim'?
A) Because Arabic avoids transitive verbs for divine attributes
B) Because sifa mushabbaha is only derived from intransitive verbs
C) Because 'rahima' is rarely used in Arabic
D) Because the names are active participles

Key Takeaway
The names 'ar-Rahman' and 'ar-Rahim' are built for exaggeration from a transitive verb by grammatically treating it as intransitive, emphasizing that Allah's mercy is an intrinsic, permanent attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's mercy is intrinsic and not contingent on your deeds, how does that change the way you approach Him when you have failed? Will you let this truth break your fear or deepen your hope?
?
The lesson shows that even the grammar of Allah's names is structured to protect His attributes. Have you ever considered that every detail of revelation is intentionally guarding your understanding of Allah? What does that demand from you when you study His names?
?
You learned that the object of mercy is not considered in the derivation. Does your heart truly rest in the fact that Allah's mercy is not a reaction to your worthiness but an eternal outpouring? What would it take for you to live from that reality today?

The Exaggeration Pattern of 'ar-Rahman'
Answering an objection about 'Rahman' not fitting standard exaggeration patterns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why 'ar-Rahman' does not fit the five standard patterns of exaggeration and how it still conveys mubalagha through meaning.

Core Idea
Exaggeration in divine names is about meaning, not morphological form.

A sharp student might notice that 'ar-Rahman' does not match the standard five patterns of exaggeration (fa''al, mif'al, etc.). This lesson shows how the author resolves the objection by distinguishing between formal and semantic exaggeration, and by clarifying that mubalagha does not imply attributing more than is true to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Read the objection: 'Rahman' is not among the five recognized patterns of mubalagha. Why is it still called mubalagha?
2
Understand the answer
The answer is that 'Rahman' conveys exaggeration by its meaning, not by its morphological pattern. This is a key distinction.
3
Clarify the meaning of mubalagha
The passage explains that mubalagha in relation to Allah does not mean attributing more than is real, because Allah's attributes are infinite. Instead, it means abundance of meanings and relations.
4
Apply the principle
Reflect on how this principle protects divine transcendence while still affirming intense mercy.

The Meaning of Mercy (Rahma) When Applied to Allah
Definition of rahma and the principle of interpreting by ultimate result.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the literal meaning of mercy cannot apply to Allah and how we interpret divine names by their ultimate result.

Core Idea
Allah's mercy is His generous bestowal, not an emotional feeling.

This lesson clarifies a fundamental principle in understanding Allah's attributes: when a divine name seems to imply a human quality, we take it according to its ultimate outcome. Using 'ar-Rahman' and 'ar-Rahim', Imam Dardeer explains how mercy truly means abundant favor and kindness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Imam Dardeer defines mercy lexically.
The passage begins with the lexical definition.
2
Understand the lexical meaning
Rahma linguistically means 'tenderness of heart' (riqqat al-qalb) and implies sympathy and bestowal.
3
Why it cannot apply literally to Allah
Tenderness of heart is a bodily quality, impossible for Allah. So we interpret mercy by its result: the actual bestowal of favors.
This is based on the principle that nothing is like Allah.
4
General rule for all similar names
Any divine name whose apparent meaning suggests imperfection is interpreted by its ultimate result. This preserves Allah's transcendence.
Example: al-Muntaqim (the Avenger) means His justice, not revenge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرحمة: رقةُ القَلْبِ، أي: رأفته، وهي تستلْزِمُ التَّفَضَّلَ والإحسان، فهو غايتها مبدؤه، فيُراد منها هنا الغايةُ لاستحالتها عليه تعالى، أي: الثابت له الفضل والإحسان كثيرًا، وكذا كلُّ اسْمِ مِنْ أسمائه تعالى يوهِمُ ظَاهِرُه خِلافَ المُرادِ، يُراد منه غايته. ثم إن أُريدُ مُريد ذلك كمُريد الإنعام فصفة ذاتٍ، وإنْ أُريدَ الفاعل كالنعم فصفة فعل. (لاستحالتها عليه تعالى أي بمعنى رقة القلب، لأن ذلك يستلزم الجسمية وتشبيهه تعالى بخلقه وهو ليس كمثله شيء.) (أي إن فسرت الرحمة بإرادة الإنعام فقد رجعت إلى صفة الإرادة فتكون صفة للذات، أما إن أريد بها الفعل فتكون من صفات الأفعال، والفرق بين صفات الذات وصفات الأفعال أن صفات الذات لا يصح ان يوصف تعالى بضدها، كالحى والعليم والسميع والبصير، أما صفات الأفعال فيصح أن يوصف بضدها كالمعز والمذل والخافض والرافع إلخ.)
And 'ar-rahma' (mercy): is tenderness of the heart (riqqat al-qalb), i.e., sympathy (ra'fa). And it necessarily entails bestowal of favor (tafaddul) and kindness (ihsan). So the bestowal is its ultimate result (ghaya) and its beginning (mabda'). Therefore what is intended here from it is the ultimate result, because its literal meaning is impossible for Him, exalted. So what is established for Him is the bestowal of favor and kindness abundantly. And similarly, every name from His names, Exalted, whose apparent meaning suggests something contrary to the intended meaning, what is intended from it is its ultimate result. Then if what is intended by 'Mureed' (the Willer) like the One Who wills bestowal (Mureed al-in'am), then it is an attribute of essence (sifat dhat). And if what is intended is the doer (al-fa'il) like the bestower of blessings (al-mun'im), then it is an attribute of action (sifat fi'l). (Because its literal meaning is impossible for Him, Exalted, i.e., the meaning of tenderness of heart, because that entails being a body and likening Him to His creation, and nothing is like Him.) (I.e., if mercy is interpreted as the will to bestow favor, then it reverts to the attribute of will (iradah) and thus is an attribute of essence. However, if by it the actual bestowal is intended, then it is from the attributes of action. The difference between attributes of essence and attributes of action is that it is not valid for Allah to be described by the opposite of attributes of essence, such as Living, Knowing, Hearing, Seeing; whereas for attributes of action, it is valid to describe Him by their opposites, such as the Exalter, the Abaser, the Lowerer, the Elevator, etc.)
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage explains how the word 'rahma' (mercy) applies to Allah. Lexically, mercy means tenderness of heart, which implies sympathy and leads to bestowing favors. Since tenderness is a bodily quality, it is impossible for Allah. Therefore, when we say 'ar-Rahman' and 'ar-Rahim', we intend the result or outcome of mercy, namely the actual bestowal of favors and kindness. This principle applies to any divine name whose apparent meaning suggests imperfection: we interpret it by its ultimate effect. The passage then introduces an important theological distinction: if we consider mercy as the will to bestow favors, it falls under the attribute of will (irada), which is an attribute of essence (sifat dhat) – eternal and unchanging. If we consider it as the act of bestowing itself, it is an attribute of action (sifat fi'l) – occurring in time and having opposites. The footnotes clarify: the literal meaning is impossible because it implies corporeality; the difference between the two types of attributes is that attributes of essence cannot have opposites (e.g., God cannot be dead), while attributes of action can (e.g., He can be both the Exalter and the Abaser).

Key Terms
الرحمة
رقة القلب
الغاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah's mercy is an emotion like human compassion.
Correction: Allah's mercy is an act of His will that results in bestowal of favors; it is not an emotion or feeling.

Checkpoint
Why is the literal meaning of mercy (tenderness of heart) not applicable to Allah?
A) Because it implies change in Allah
B) Because it implies bodily composition
C) Because it implies ignorance
D) Because it implies weakness

Key Takeaway
When a divine name seems to imply a human limitation, we understand it by its beneficial outcome.

?

The passage states that every divine name whose apparent meaning suggests imperfection must be understood by its ultimate result. ?

In what specific relationship do you fall short of granting favors and kindness without expecting emotional reciprocity? Attributes of Essence vs. Attributes of Action
Distinction between attributes of essence and action based on mercy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will distinguish between attributes of essence (eternal, without opposites) and attributes of action (temporal, with opposites) in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Some divine attributes are eternal and unchanging; others are actions that can have opposites.

Building on the discussion of mercy, Imam Dardeer introduces a key distinction: whether mercy is understood as the will to bestow (attribute of essence) or as the bestowing act itself (attribute of action). This distinction applies to many divine names and is crucial for a correct understanding of Allah's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two ways to interpret mercy and the resulting classification.
The passage includes footnotes that clarify the difference.
2
Understand the two interpretations
If mercy is interpreted as the will to bestow (irada), it is an attribute of essence. If interpreted as the act of bestowing, it is an attribute of action.
Attribute of essence is eternal; attribute of action occurs in time.
3
Grasp the key difference
Attributes of essence have no opposites (e.g., Allah is always Living, never dead). Attributes of action can have opposites (e.g., al-Mu'izz and al-Mudhill).
This distinction helps avoid confusion about Allah's names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرحمة: رقةُ القَلْبِ، أي: رأفته، وهي تستلْزِمُ التَّفَضَّلَ والإحسان، فهو غايتها مبدؤه، فيُراد منها هنا الغايةُ لاستحالتها عليه تعالى، أي: الثابت له الفضل والإحسان كثيرًا، وكذا كلُّ اسْمِ مِنْ أسمائه تعالى يوهِمُ ظَاهِرُه خِلافَ المُرادِ، يُراد منه غايته. ثم إن أُريدُ مُريد ذلك كمُريد الإنعام فصفة ذاتٍ، وإنْ أُريدَ الفاعل كالنعم فصفة فعل. (لاستحالتها عليه تعالى أي بمعنى رقة القلب، لأن ذلك يستلزم الجسمية وتشبيهه تعالى بخلقه وهو ليس كمثله شيء.) (أي إن فسرت الرحمة بإرادة الإنعام فقد رجعت إلى صفة الإرادة فتكون صفة للذات، أما إن أريد بها الفعل فتكون من صفات الأفعال، والفرق بين صفات الذات وصفات الأفعال أن صفات الذات لا يصح ان يوصف تعالى بضدها، كالحى والعليم والسميع والبصير، أما صفات الأفعال فيصح أن يوصف بضدها كالمعز والمذل والخافض والرافع إلخ.)
And 'ar-rahma' (mercy): is tenderness of the heart (riqqat al-qalb), i.e., sympathy (ra'fa). And it necessarily entails bestowal of favor (tafaddul) and kindness (ihsan). So the bestowal is its ultimate result (ghaya) and its beginning (mabda'). Therefore what is intended here from it is the ultimate result, because its literal meaning is impossible for Him, exalted. So what is established for Him is the bestowal of favor and kindness abundantly. And similarly, every name from His names, Exalted, whose apparent meaning suggests something contrary to the intended meaning, what is intended from it is its ultimate result. Then if what is intended by 'Mureed' (the Willer) like the One Who wills bestowal (Mureed al-in'am), then it is an attribute of essence (sifat dhat). And if what is intended is the doer (al-fa'il) like the bestower of blessings (al-mun'im), then it is an attribute of action (sifat fi'l). (Because its literal meaning is impossible for Him, Exalted, i.e., the meaning of tenderness of heart, because that entails being a body and likening Him to His creation, and nothing is like Him.) (I.e., if mercy is interpreted as the will to bestow favor, then it reverts to the attribute of will (iradah) and thus is an attribute of essence. However, if by it the actual bestowal is intended, then it is from the attributes of action. The difference between attributes of essence and attributes of action is that it is not valid for Allah to be described by the opposite of attributes of essence, such as Living, Knowing, Hearing, Seeing; whereas for attributes of action, it is valid to describe Him by their opposites, such as the Exalter, the Abaser, the Lowerer, the Elevator, etc.)
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage explains how the word 'rahma' (mercy) applies to Allah. Lexically, mercy means tenderness of heart, which implies sympathy and leads to bestowing favors. Since tenderness is a bodily quality, it is impossible for Allah. Therefore, when we say 'ar-Rahman' and 'ar-Rahim', we intend the result or outcome of mercy, namely the actual bestowal of favors and kindness. This principle applies to any divine name whose apparent meaning suggests imperfection: we interpret it by its ultimate effect. The passage then introduces an important theological distinction: if we consider mercy as the will to bestow favors, it falls under the attribute of will (irada), which is an attribute of essence (sifat dhat) – eternal and unchanging. If we consider it as the act of bestowing itself, it is an attribute of action (sifat fi'l) – occurring in time and having opposites. The footnotes clarify: the literal meaning is impossible because it implies corporeality; the difference between the two types of attributes is that attributes of essence cannot have opposites (e.g., God cannot be dead), while attributes of action can (e.g., He can be both the Exalter and the Abaser).

Key Terms
صفة الذات
صفة الفعل
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All divine names refer to eternal attributes of essence.
Correction: Some names refer to actions that occur in time and can have opposites, making them attributes of action.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an attribute of action?
A) al-Hayy (the Living)
B) al-‘Alim (the Knowing)
C) al-Mu‘izz (the Exalter)
D) as-Sami‘ (the Hearing)

Key Takeaway
Understanding whether a name refers to an essential attribute or an act helps avoid misconceptions about Allah's nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Bismillah ir-Rahman ir-Rahim,' do you feel that Allah's mercy is an emotional reaction like human tenderness, or do you now see it as His eternal will to bestow? How does this shift your trust in Him?
?
Think of a time you felt Allah had 'withheld' something from you. Does knowing that attributes of action can have opposites (e.g., the Abaser) help you accept His decree? What changes in your heart when you realize He is both the Elevator and the Lowerer?
?
If Allah's mercy as an attribute of essence means He always wills your good, yet you face hardship, how can you reconcile the two? Write down one concrete way you will rely on His eternal will today, regardless of your circumstances.
