The Derivation of Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim
Dardeer explains the grammatical derivation of Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim as descriptive adjectives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the divine names Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim are derived as descriptive adjectives (ṣifatān mushabbahatān) and the grammatical steps required to form them from the verb raḥima.

Core Idea
Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim are intensive adjectives, not simple participles.

Before we can appreciate the meanings of Allah's names, we must understand their linguistic roots. Imam Dardeer begins by explaining that Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim are not simple participles but descriptive adjectives built for intensification. To derive them from the transitive verb raḥima, the grammarians had to treat the verb as intransitive. In this lesson, we explore that process.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a sense of the grammatical discussion. Notice the two methods for making the verb intransitive.
The passage uses technical grammatical terms like 'لازم' and 'متعد'. Don't worry if you don't know them yet – the commentary will explain.
2
Identify the two methods
The passage presents two ways to treat the verb raḥima as intransitive: either by ignoring the object or by changing the verb form to faʿula. Understand the reasoning behind each.
The first method is conceptual, the second is morphological.
3
Understand the necessity
Reflect on why this treatment is needed: descriptive adjectives (ṣifat mushabbahah) can only be derived from intransitive verbs. Since raḥima is transitive, adjustments must be made.
This shows the precision of Arabic grammar in preserving theological meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرحمن الرحيم صفتان مشبهتان بنيتا للمبالغة من رحم بالكسر إما بتنزيله منزلة اللازم بأن يقصد إثباته للفاعل فقط من غير اعتبار تعلقه بمفعول وإما بجعله لازما بأن ينقل إلى فعل بالضم وإنما احتيج لأن الصفة المشبهة إنما تصاغ من اللازم. والرحمة رقة القلب أي رأفته وهي تستلزم التفضل والإحسان فهو غايتها مبدؤه فيراد منها هنا الغاية لاستحالتها عليه تعالى أي الثابت له الفضل والإحسان كثيرا وكذا كل اسم من أسمائه تعالى يوهم ظاهره خلاف المراد يراد منه غايته. ثم إن أريد مريد ذلك كمريد الإنعام فصفة ذات وإن أريد الفاعل كالنعم فصفة فعل. وقدم الرحمن لأنه خاص به تعالى إذ لا يطلق على غيره تعالى ولأنه أبلغ إذ معناه المنعم بجلائل النعم كما وكيفا بخلاف الرحيم فإن معناه المنعم بدقائقها كذلك. وجلائل النعم أصولها كالوجود والإيمان والعافية والرزق والعقل والسمع والبصر وغير ذلك. ودقائقها فروعها كالجمال وكثرة زيادة الإيمان ووفور العافية وسعة الرزق ودقة العقل وحدّة السمع والبصر وغير ذلك. والمعنى أنه تعالى من حيث إنه منعم بجلائل النعم يسمى الرحمن ومن حيث إنه منعم بدقائقها يسمى الرحيم.
And 'Ar-Rahman' and 'Ar-Rahim' are two descriptive adjectives (ṣifatān mushabbahatān) built for intensification from 'raḥima' (with kasra) either by treating it as intransitive by intending to affirm it for the agent only without considering its attachment to an object, or by making it intransitive by transferring it to 'faʿula' (with ḍamma). And there is need for this because the descriptive adjective is only derived from intransitive verbs. And 'mercy' (al-raḥma) is tenderness of heart, i.e., compassion, and it entails favor and beneficence, so He (may He be exalted) is the ultimate end and origin of it; so what is intended here is the ultimate end because of its impossibility upon Him (may He be exalted), i.e., that which is established for Him is abundant favor and beneficence. And likewise every name of His names (may He be exalted) whose apparent meaning suggests contrary to what is intended, its ultimate end is intended. Then if what is intended is the one who intends that, like the one who intends beneficence, it is an attribute of essence (ṣifat dhāt); and if the agent is intended, like the acts of bestowing, it is an attribute of action (ṣifat fiʿl). And He placed 'Ar-Rahman' before because it is specific to Him (may He be exalted), as it is not applied to other than Him (may He be exalted), and because it is more emphatic, for its meaning is the Bestower of great blessings (jalāʾil al-niʿam) in quantity and quality, unlike 'Ar-Rahim' for its meaning is the Bestower of subtle blessings (daqāʾiqihā) likewise. And great blessings are their foundations, such as existence, faith, well-being, provision, intellect, hearing, sight, and other than that. And subtle blessings are their branches, such as beauty, abundance of increase of faith, abundance of well-being, extensiveness of provision, sharpness of intellect, acuity of hearing and sight, and other than that. And the meaning is that He (may He be exalted) is called 'Ar-Rahman' in respect of His being the Bestower of great blessings, and is called 'Ar-Rahim' in respect of His being the Bestower of subtle blessings.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a combined definition and classification. Dardeer first explains the linguistic derivation of the two divine names 'Ar-Rahman' and 'Ar-Rahim'. He notes that they are descriptive adjectives (ṣifatān mushabbahatān) built for intensification from the verb 'raḥima' (to have mercy). Since this verb is transitive, but descriptive adjectives are only derived from intransitive verbs, the grammarians treat it as intransitive either by disregarding the object or by changing the verb form to 'faʿula'. He then addresses the meaning of 'rahma' (mercy): it is tenderness of heart, which is impossible for Allah. Therefore, when applied to Allah, it must refer to the result of mercy, which is abundant favor and beneficence. This principle applies to all divine names that might imply a meaning impossible for Allah. Next, he distinguishes between two types of divine attributes: attributes of essence (ṣifāt al-dhāt) and attributes of action (ṣifāt al-fiʿl). If the mercy is interpreted as the will to bestow, it is an attribute of essence; if as the act of bestowing itself, it is an attribute of action. Finally, he explains why 'Ar-Rahman' precedes 'Ar-Rahim' in the basmala: it is specific to Allah (not used for others) and more comprehensive, denoting the Bestower of major blessings, while 'Ar-Rahim' denotes the Bestower of minor blessings. He then enumerates examples of major blessings (e.g., existence, faith) and minor blessings (e.g., beauty, increased faith), concluding that Allah is called 'Ar-Rahman' in respect of major blessings and 'Ar-Rahim' in respect of minor blessings.

Key Terms
الرحمن
الرحيم
صفة مشبهة
مبالغة
لازم
متعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim are simple active participles from raḥima.
Correction: They are actually intensive descriptive adjectives (ṣifatān mushabbahatān), requiring grammatical adjustments to derive from a transitive verb.

Checkpoint
Why is it necessary to treat the verb raḥima as intransitive when deriving the names Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim?
A) Because descriptive adjectives can only be derived from intransitive verbs
B) Because the verb raḥima is weak
C) Because the names are foreign
D) Because the verb has multiple meanings

Key Takeaway
Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim are intensive descriptive adjectives derived from 'raḥima' by treating it as intransitive, because descriptive adjectives require intransitive verbs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim are not just actions but permanent, intensive attributes of Allah. Do you live as if His mercy is a fixed reality, or do you let your sins make you doubt it? What would change today if you fully believed in the intensity of His mercy?
?
The scholars went to great lengths to ensure the grammatical correctness of Allah's names. When it comes to understanding your Lord, do you put in similar effort, or do you settle for a surface-level understanding? What is one thing you can do today to learn a divine name more deeply?
?
If Allah's mercy is described by these intensive adjectives, how should that mercy affect the way you treat others? Is there someone you have not forgiven? Take a step today to reflect that mercy.

The Meaning of Mercy and Classification of Divine Attributes
Dardeer explains the theological meaning of mercy and the classification of divine attributes into essence and action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'mercy' (rahma) is applied to Allah, the principle of interpreting divine names by their ultimate end, and the distinction between attributes of essence and attributes of action.

Core Idea
Allah's mercy means abundant favor, not emotion; divine names with apparent shortcomings point to their ultimate end.

Understanding the linguistic derivation is only the first step. Now Dardeer tackles the theological meaning of mercy as an attribute of Allah. Since mercy in humans involves tenderness of heart, which is impossible for Allah, we must interpret it as the result: abundant favor and beneficence. This principle applies to all divine names that might imply imperfection. Moreover, Dardeer distinguishes between attributes that reside in Allah's essence (like the will to bestow) and those that are acts of bestowal. In this lesson, we unpack these important theological nuances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the meaning of mercy
Read the Arabic text focusing on the definition of rahma and the introduction of the principle of interpreting by ultimate end.
Notice how Dardeer contrasts the literal meaning (tenderness) with the intended meaning for Allah (favor).
2
Understand the 'ultimate end' principle
Reflect on why this principle is necessary: because attributing literal mercy (emotion) to Allah is impossible. So we refer to the result, which is beneficence.
This is a key hermeneutical tool in Islamic theology.
3
Distinguish attributes of essence vs. action
Read the short sentence about 'if what is intended is the one who intends...' and grasp the two categories: attributes of essence (like the attribute of will) and attributes of action (like the act of bestowing).
The classification depends on whether the focus is on the agent who wills or the act itself.

?

The Distinction Between Great and Subtle Blessings
Dardeer explains the difference between Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim in terms of great vs. subtle blessings and the reason for their order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the names Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim in terms of the types of blessings they denote, and the reason for the precedence of Ar-Rahman.

Core Idea
Ar-Rahman bestows great blessings; Ar-Rahim bestows subtle blessings; both are abundant.

Having established the linguistic and theological foundations, Dardeer now explains the specific nuance between the two names. Ar-Rahman denotes the Bestower of great, foundational blessings (like existence and faith), while Ar-Rahim denotes the Bestower of subtle, detailed blessings (like beauty and increase). Moreover, Ar-Rahman is placed first because it is specific to Allah and more emphatic. In this lesson, we explore these distinctions with concrete examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the order and meaning of the two names
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the reason given for the precedence and the definitions of great vs. subtle blessings.
Focus on the examples: great blessings include existence, faith; subtle blessings include beauty, sharp hearing.
2
Reflect on the precedence of Ar-Rahman
Consider the two reasons: (1) it is specific to Allah (not used for others), and (2) it is more emphatic (denotes greater comprehensiveness).
This explains why we say 'Bismillahir Rahmanir Rahim'.
3
Understand the types of blessings
Great blessings (jalāʾil al-niʿam) are foundational, like existence; subtle blessings (daqāʾiq al-niʿam) are refinements, like beauty. Both are from Allah.
The distinction is not about quantity but about the nature of the blessing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرحمن الرحيم صفتان مشبهتان بنيتا للمبالغة من رحم بالكسر إما بتنزيله منزلة اللازم بأن يقصد إثباته للفاعل فقط من غير اعتبار تعلقه بمفعول وإما بجعله لازما بأن ينقل إلى فعل بالضم وإنما احتيج لأن الصفة المشبهة إنما تصاغ من اللازم. والرحمة رقة القلب أي رأفته وهي تستلزم التفضل والإحسان فهو غايتها مبدؤه فيراد منها هنا الغاية لاستحالتها عليه تعالى أي الثابت له الفضل والإحسان كثيرا وكذا كل اسم من أسمائه تعالى يوهم ظاهره خلاف المراد يراد منه غايته. ثم إن أريد مريد ذلك كمريد الإنعام فصفة ذات وإن أريد الفاعل كالنعم فصفة فعل. وقدم الرحمن لأنه خاص به تعالى إذ لا يطلق على غيره تعالى ولأنه أبلغ إذ معناه المنعم بجلائل النعم كما وكيفا بخلاف الرحيم فإن معناه المنعم بدقائقها كذلك. وجلائل النعم أصولها كالوجود والإيمان والعافية والرزق والعقل والسمع والبصر وغير ذلك. ودقائقها فروعها كالجمال وكثرة زيادة الإيمان ووفور العافية وسعة الرزق ودقة العقل وحدّة السمع والبصر وغير ذلك. والمعنى أنه تعالى من حيث إنه منعم بجلائل النعم يسمى الرحمن ومن حيث إنه منعم بدقائقها يسمى الرحيم.
And 'Ar-Rahman' and 'Ar-Rahim' are two descriptive adjectives (ṣifatān mushabbahatān) built for intensification from 'raḥima' (with kasra) either by treating it as intransitive by intending to affirm it for the agent only without considering its attachment to an object, or by making it intransitive by transferring it to 'faʿula' (with ḍamma). And there is need for this because the descriptive adjective is only derived from intransitive verbs. And 'mercy' (al-raḥma) is tenderness of heart, i.e., compassion, and it entails favor and beneficence, so He (may He be exalted) is the ultimate end and origin of it; so what is intended here is the ultimate end because of its impossibility upon Him (may He be exalted), i.e., that which is established for Him is abundant favor and beneficence. And likewise every name of His names (may He be exalted) whose apparent meaning suggests contrary to what is intended, its ultimate end is intended. Then if what is intended is the one who intends that, like the one who intends beneficence, it is an attribute of essence (ṣifat dhāt); and if the agent is intended, like the acts of bestowing, it is an attribute of action (ṣifat fiʿl). And He placed 'Ar-Rahman' before because it is specific to Him (may He be exalted), as it is not applied to other than Him (may He be exalted), and because it is more emphatic, for its meaning is the Bestower of great blessings (jalāʾil al-niʿam) in quantity and quality, unlike 'Ar-Rahim' for its meaning is the Bestower of subtle blessings (daqāʾiqihā) likewise. And great blessings are their foundations, such as existence, faith, well-being, provision, intellect, hearing, sight, and other than that. And subtle blessings are their branches, such as beauty, abundance of increase of faith, abundance of well-being, extensiveness of provision, sharpness of intellect, acuity of hearing and sight, and other than that. And the meaning is that He (may He be exalted) is called 'Ar-Rahman' in respect of His being the Bestower of great blessings, and is called 'Ar-Rahim' in respect of His being the Bestower of subtle blessings.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a combined definition and classification. Dardeer first explains the linguistic derivation of the two divine names 'Ar-Rahman' and 'Ar-Rahim'. He notes that they are descriptive adjectives (ṣifatān mushabbahatān) built for intensification from the verb 'raḥima' (to have mercy). Since this verb is transitive, but descriptive adjectives are only derived from intransitive verbs, the grammarians treat it as intransitive either by disregarding the object or by changing the verb form to 'faʿula'. He then addresses the meaning of 'rahma' (mercy): it is tenderness of heart, which is impossible for Allah. Therefore, when applied to Allah, it must refer to the result of mercy, which is abundant favor and beneficence. This principle applies to all divine names that might imply a meaning impossible for Allah. Next, he distinguishes between two types of divine attributes: attributes of essence (ṣifāt al-dhāt) and attributes of action (ṣifāt al-fiʿl). If the mercy is interpreted as the will to bestow, it is an attribute of essence; if as the act of bestowing itself, it is an attribute of action. Finally, he explains why 'Ar-Rahman' precedes 'Ar-Rahim' in the basmala: it is specific to Allah (not used for others) and more comprehensive, denoting the Bestower of major blessings, while 'Ar-Rahim' denotes the Bestower of minor blessings. He then enumerates examples of major blessings (e.g., existence, faith) and minor blessings (e.g., beauty, increased faith), concluding that Allah is called 'Ar-Rahman' in respect of major blessings and 'Ar-Rahim' in respect of minor blessings.

Key Terms
جلائل النعم
دقائق النعم
المنعم
الوجود
الإيمان
العافية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim are synonyms or that Ar-Rahman denotes general mercy and Ar-Rahim specific mercy.
Correction: Dardeer clarifies: Ar-Rahman denotes bestowal of great blessings, Ar-Rahim denotes bestowal of subtle blessings, both are abundant.

Checkpoint
According to Dardeer, which type of blessing does the name 'Ar-Rahman' specifically denote?
A) Subtle blessings like beauty
B) Great blessings like existence and faith
C) All blessings equally
D) Mercy in the afterlife only

Key Takeaway
Ar-Rahman is the Bestower of great, foundational blessings; Ar-Rahim is the Bestower of subtle, detailed blessings; and Ar-Rahman precedes because it is exclusive to Allah and more comprehensive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a moment to list three great blessings and three subtle blessings from the passage that you experience daily. Which of these do you most often take for granted? Before you sleep tonight, deliberately thank Allah for one subtle blessing you normally overlook.
?
The passage teaches that subtle blessings (like beauty, sharp intellect) are branches of great blessings (like existence, faith). Are you using your intellect and senses for purposes that please Allah? If you find yourself neglecting them, what is one concrete step you can take tomorrow to align them with gratitude?
?
Ar-Rahman is a name exclusive to Allah and denotes the bestowal of the very foundations of your life. When you wake up each day, do you consciously acknowledge that your very existence and faith are gifts from Ar-Rahman? Write down a short dua or phrase to say each morning that reminds you of this.

The Distinction Between ar-Rahman and ar-Rahim
Imam Dardeer explains the distinction between ar-Rahman and ar-Rahim through the types of blessings they denote.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise distinction between the divine names ar-Rahman and ar-Rahim as explained by Imam Dardeer.

Core Idea
Ar-Rahman bestows major blessings; ar-Rahim bestows subtle blessings.

The names ar-Rahman and ar-Rahim are often mentioned together, but they are not synonyms. Imam Dardeer provides a clear distinction based on the types of blessings each name emphasizes. This lesson unpacks that distinction and why ar-Rahman is more comprehensive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm and key terms come through.
Pay attention to the terms 'جلال' and 'دقائق'.
2
Identify the two reasons for precedence
The passage gives two reasons why ar-Rahman is mentioned before ar-Rahim. Find them and note how they differ.
One reason is about exclusivity, the other about comprehensiveness.
3
Understand the classification of blessings
Notice that blessings are divided into 'جلائل' (great) and 'دقائق' (subtle). Ar-Rahman relates to the former, ar-Rahim to the latter.
The passage calls great blessings 'roots' and subtle blessings 'branches'.
4
Study the examples provided
Go through each example of great and subtle blessings. See how the subtle blessings are enhancements of the great ones.
For instance, existence vs. beauty, faith vs. increase of faith.
5
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself how this distinction helps you understand Allah's mercy more deeply. Why does it matter that these names are not interchangeable?
This connects to the broader theme of divine names and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقدَّمَ (الرحمن) لأنه خاص به تعالى، إذ لا يُطْلَقُ على غَيْرِه تعالى، ولأنه أبلغ، إِذْ معناه: المنعم بجلائلِ النِّعَم كما وكَيفًا، بخلاف الرحيم» فإنَّ معناه : المُنعِمُ بدقائقها كذلك. وجلائلُ النَّعَم أصولها، كالوُجودِ، والإيمان، والعافية، والرِّزْقِ، والعقل، والسَّمع، والبصرِ ، وغَير ذلك. ودقائقها فروعُها، كالجمال، وكثرة زيادة الإيمان، ووفورِ العافية، وسعةِ الرِّزْقِ، ودقة العقل، وحدّةِ السمع والبصر، وغير ذلك. والمعنى: أنه -تعالى- مِنْ حَيثُ إنّه مُنعِم بجلائل النِّعَمِ يُسَمّى «الرحمن»، ومِنْ حَيثُ إنه مُنعِمٌ بدقائقها يُسمّى «الرحيم».
And he began with 'ar-Rahman' because it is specific to Him, may He be exalted, as it is not applied to other than Him, may He be exalted, and because it is more comprehensive, for its meaning is: the Bestower of great blessings in quantity and quality, unlike 'ar-Rahim', for its meaning is: the Bestower of subtle blessings likewise. And great blessings are their roots, such as existence, faith, well-being, provision, intellect, hearing, sight, and other than that. And their subtle blessings are their branches, such as beauty, abundance of increase of faith, completeness of well-being, breadth of provision, sharpness of intellect, acuity of hearing and sight, and other than that. And the meaning: that He, exalted, inasmuch as He is a Bestower of great blessings is called 'ar-Rahman', and inasmuch as He is a Bestower of subtle blessings is called 'ar-Rahim'.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. It explains why the name 'ar-Rahman' was mentioned before 'ar-Rahim'. The author states two reasons: first, 'ar-Rahman' is specific to Allah and not used for others, while 'ar-Rahim' can be applied to humans; second, 'ar-Rahman' is more comprehensive because it denotes bestowal of major (jilal) blessings, while 'ar-Rahim' denotes bestowal of subtle (daqa'iq) blessings. He then defines major blessings as the foundations (usul) of blessings like existence, faith, well-being, provision, intellect, hearing, sight, etc. Subtle blessings are the branches (furu') of these, such as beauty, increase of faith, fullness of well-being, etc. The conclusion: Allah is called 'ar-Rahman' with respect to bestowing major blessings, and 'ar-Rahim' with respect to bestowing subtle blessings.

Key Terms

الرحيم
جلائل النعم
دقائق النعم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ar-Rahman and ar-Rahim are synonymous and interchangeable.
Correction: They denote different aspects of mercy: ar-Rahman focuses on bestowing major blessings, while ar-Rahim focuses on bestowing subtle, detailed blessings. They are complementary, not identical.

Checkpoint

C) Because it appears first in the Basmalah.
D) Because it refers to eternal mercy.

Key Takeaway
Ar-Rahman and ar-Rahim are distinct yet complementary names of Allah, referring to His bestowal of major and subtle blessings respectively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If ar-Rahman gave you the root of hearing, and ar-Rahim gave you the acuity to enjoy a loved one's voice, which of these gifts do you take for granted? Commit today to praising Allah specifically for one subtle blessing, and let that change how you see His mercy.

The Divine Name Al-Ali: Linguistic Derivation and Theological Depth
Dardeer explains the derivation and theological meaning of al-Ali

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic derivation of the divine name al-´Ali and how it encapsulates both divine transcendence (negating defects) and perfections (affirming attributes).

Core Idea
Al-´Ali means Allah is high above all deficiency and full of all perfection.

The name al-´Ali is more than a simple attribute of height. In this passage, Imam Dardeer takes us through the linguistic roots and morphological rule behind the name, then unveils its profound theological meaning: it simultaneously negates every imperfection and affirms every perfection in Allah. This makes al-´Ali a comprehensive divine name that sums up the entire framework of tanzih (transcendence) and ithbat (affirmation).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal English translation. Let the flow from linguistics to theology register before analyzing.
2
Understand the linguistic derivation
Notice that the name comes from 'alayu' and see how the waw turns into ya. Why did this happen? The author explains it's because ya is lighter. This morphological detail sets the stage.
The rule is that the assimilated letter changes, but here it's the other way around.
3
Grasp the theological meaning
Focus on the shift from physical elevation to metaphorical transcendence. The name al-´Ali means Allah is above every deficiency, which entails all negative attributes. But it can also include positive attributes, making it comprehensive.
The author gives two possible interpretations: only negation, or negation plus affirmation.
4
Identify the two sets of attributes
The passage lists the five attributes of negation (sifāt as-salūb) and the seven attributes of meanings (sifāt al-ma´ānī). Commit these to memory as the foundation of divine attributes in Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(العلي) من العلو، وهو الرفعة، فأصله: «عليو»، اجتمعت الياء والواو، وسبقت إحداهما بالسكون فقلبت الواو ياء، وأدغمت فيها الياء. وعلوه تعالى معنوي، عبارة عن تنزيهه تعالى عن كل نقص، فيتضمن اتصافه تعالى بجميع صفات السلب. ولك أن تقول: علوه تعالى عبارة عن تنزيهه عن كل نقص، واتصافه بكل كمال، فيشمل صفات المعاني أيضًا.
هذا على خلاف القاعدة، وهي أن المدغم هو الذي يقلب ويرد من جنس المدغم فيه، لكن لما كانت الياء أخف من الواو قلبت ياء وأدغمت في الياء مثل سيد.
فبهذا يكون من الأسماء الجامعة، أي لنفي النقائص وإثبات الكمالات. وصفات السلوب هي: القدم، والبقاء، والقيام بالنفس، والمخالفة للحوادث، والوحدانية، وصفات المعاني هي: العلم، والإرادة، والقدرة، والحياة، والسمع، والبصر، والكلام.
(al-´Ali) from al-´uluww, which is al-raf´a (elevation). Its original form is '´alayu'. The ya and waw met, and one of them preceded by sukun, so the waw was turned into ya and the ya was assimilated into it. And His elevation, may He be exalted, is metaphorical, an expression of His transcendence above every deficiency, so it entails His being described with all attributes of negation (ṣifāt as-salūb). And you may say: His elevation is an expression of His transcendence above every deficiency, and His being described with every perfection, so it includes the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-ma´ānī) as well. This is contrary to the rule, which is that the assimilated letter is the one that changes and merges with the same type as the assimilated letter, but because the ya is lighter than the waw, it was changed to ya and assimilated into the ya, like 'sayyid'. So by this it is one of the comprehensive names, i.e., for negating deficiencies and affirming perfections. The attributes of negation are: pre-eternity, everlastingness, self-subsistence, dissimilarity to contingent things, and oneness. The attributes of meanings are: knowledge, will, power, life, hearing, sight, and speech.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage explains the divine name al-´Ali (the Most High). The author begins with the linguistic derivation: it comes from al-´uluww (height, elevation), and the root is '´alayu'. He then describes a morphological rule where the waw is changed to ya and assimilated because the ya is lighter, contrary to the usual rule. Theologically, Allah's elevation is not physical but metaphorical (ma´nawī), meaning His transcendence (tanzīh) above all deficiency. This entails the attributes of negation (ṣifāt as-salūb) like pre-eternity, oneness, etc., which negate imperfection. Alternatively, one can say His elevation expresses both transcendence and being described with all perfections, thus also including the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-ma´ānī) like knowledge, power, etc. The author then lists the five negative attributes and the seven positive attributes of meanings. So al-´Ali is a comprehensive name that simultaneously negates all defects and affirms all perfections.

Key Terms
العلي
العلو
صفات السلوب
صفات المعاني
تنزيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Allah's elevation is spatial, like being physically above the heavens.
Correction: The author clarifies that Allah's elevation is metaphorical (ma´nawī), meaning His transcendence above all deficiency, not physical height.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the name al-´Ali primarily indicate about Allah?
A) Physical height above the heavens
B) Transcendence above all deficiencies and being described with all perfections
C) Only the attributes of negation
D) Only the attributes of meanings

Key Takeaway
The name al-´Ali is a comprehensive divine name that simultaneously negates all deficiencies and affirms all perfections, encompassing both sets of divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you invoke the name al-ʿAlī without pausing to consider what it demands of your belief? Does your heart truly affirm that Allah is free from every deficiency you witness in creation, or do you unconsciously attribute limitations to Him when life feels heavy? Today, commit to saying 'Ya ʿAlī' with the intention of negating your mental images of imperfection.

The passage teaches that al-ʿAlī is a comprehensive name encompassing both negation of flaws and affirmation of perfections. When you think of Allah's attributes, is your concept lopsided—either overemphasizing His transcendence (making Him distant) or His immanence (making Him too familiar)? How will you balance tanzīh and tashbīh in your worship starting now?
?
You read that Allah's elevation is 'maʿnawī'—not spatial. Yet, when you raise your hands in du'a, do you instinctively imagine Allah 'up there' in a physical sense? What would change in your salah if you truly internalized that His 'highness' is absolute perfection, not location? Write one concrete change you will make in your prayer posture or focus to remind yourself.

The Three Aspects of Divine Oneness (al-Wāḥid)
Dardeer defines al-Wāḥid and enumerates three aspects of oneness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three aspects of divine oneness (al-waḥdāniyya) as explained by Imam Dardeer, and why each negates a form of partnership with Allah.

Core Idea
Divine oneness means Allah is free from any partner in essence, attributes, and actions.

Imam Dardeer begins his explanation of the divine name al-Wāḥid by defining it as the One free from all forms of partnership. He then unpacks the meaning of oneness into three distinct aspects, each negating a different potential form of association. This passage establishes the comprehensive nature of tawḥīd.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the three-part enumeration introduced by 'ثلاثة أوجه'.
2
Identify the three aspects
Find each of the three aspects of oneness. Notice that each aspect negates something: multiplicity, a partner, or shared agency.
The first negates multiplicity in essence, the second negates any equal in essence or attributes, the third negates any partner in creation and regulation.
3
Understand the final conclusion
Read the concluding sentence: 'فهو منزه عن الضد والند'. Ask yourself what al-ḍidd and al-nidd mean, and how they relate to the three aspects.
Al-ḍidd means opposite or contrary; al-nidd means rival or equal. Both are negated by the three aspects.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Many think oneness only means 'there is one God'. This passage shows it also negates any partner in attributes and actions, and excludes composition in the essence.
Tawḥīd is not just numerical unity; it excludes all forms of partnership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الواحد) أي: المنزه عن الشريك في الذات والصفات والأفعال.
معنى الوحدانية في حقه تعالى يشتمل على ثلاثة أوجه: الأول: نفي الكثرة في ذاته تعالى، والثاني: نفي النظير أو الشريك له تعالى في ذاته أو صفة من صفاته، والثالث: انفراده تعالى بالإيجاد والاختراع والتدبير، فهو منزه عن الضد والند.
(al-Wāḥid) i.e., the one who is free from any partner in essence, attributes, and actions. The meaning of oneness (al-waḥdāniyya) with respect to Him, may He be exalted, comprises three aspects: the first: negation of multiplicity in His essence; the second: negation of any equal or partner to Him in His essence or any of His attributes; the third: His exclusivity in bringing into existence, invention, and regulation. So He is free from al-ḍidd (opposite) and al-nidd (rival).
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage explains the divine name al-Wāḥid (the One). The author defines it as the one who is free from any partner in essence, attributes, and actions. He then elaborates on the meaning of oneness (al-waḥdāniyya) through three aspects: first, negation of multiplicity in the divine essence (i.e., Allah is not composed of parts); second, negation of any equal or partner in essence or attributes (no one shares His nature or qualities); third, His exclusivity in creating, inventing, and managing all affairs. As a result, Allah is free from al-ḍidd (opponent or contrary) and al-nidd (rival or match).

Key Terms
الواحد
الوحدانية
الشريك
الضد
الند

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the explanation, what are the three aspects of divine oneness (al-waḥdāniyya)?

B) Negation of multiplicity, negation of partner in essence/attributes, and exclusivity in creation/management
C) Oneness of God, oneness of religion, and oneness of humanity
D) Negation of equal, negation of opposite, and negation of rival

Key Takeaway
Divine oneness (al-waḥdāniyya) means Allah has no partner in essence, attributes, or actions, and He alone creates and manages all things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if Allah alone is the exclusive creator, sustainer, and regulator of every detail of your life, or do you sometimes rely on created means as if they share His power? What would it mean to entrust your entire existence to Him alone?
?
Which of the three aspects of divine oneness is weakest in your heart: negating multiplicity in His essence (that He is beyond parts), negating any equal in His attributes (that no one shares His qualities), or affirming His exclusive control over all things? Reflect on one specific belief or action where you have failed to uphold that aspect.
?
If you were asked to explain the difference between 'opposite' (al-ḍidd) and 'rival' (al-nidd) to someone who might be committing shirk without realizing it, what would you say? Have you ever considered that by ascribing ultimate control to a system or person, you are effectively setting up a rival to Allah?

The All-Knowing: Allah's Comprehensive Knowledge
Dardeer explains al-Alim via categories of knowable things

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's knowledge as al-Alim encompasses all knowable things: necessary, contingent, and impossible; past, present, and future; existent and non-existent.

Core Idea
Allah knows everything — even impossibilities and non-existents.

Imam Dardeer unpacks the divine name al-Alim by describing the scope of Allah's knowledge. The passage clarifies that His knowledge is not limited to what exists or will exist, but extends to all categories of knowable things, including what does not exist and what is impossible. This lesson explores that comprehensive scope.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the concise style and the series of categories listed.
The passage includes parenthetical clarifications.
2
Identify the categories
List all the categories of knowable things mentioned: what is, what is not, what is happening, impossibilities, contingencies, necessities.
Some are mentioned in the main text, others in the footnotes.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why it matters that Allah knows impossibilities and non-existents. How does this deepen your understanding of omniscience?

The Divine Name al-'Aliyy: Moral Highness and Comprehensive Meaning
Defines al-'Aliyy with linguistic derivation and two theological interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic derivation of the name al-'Aliyy and how its theological meaning encompasses both negation of defects and affirmation of perfections.

Core Idea
Allah's highness is moral, not physical, and al-'Aliyy is a comprehensive name covering all attributes.

The name al-'Aliyy is one of Allah's beautiful names, but its meaning goes beyond mere height. In this passage, Imam Dardir explains the linguistic root and then unpacks two theological interpretations that show how this single name can encompass both the attributes of negation and the attributes of meaning. Understanding this will deepen your appreciation of how divine names can summarize entire categories of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage aloud, then read the literal translation. Notice the concise style and the two interpretative options introduced by 'wa laka an taqūla'.
2
Understand the linguistic derivation
The passage explains that the name comes from 'uluww (height/elevation), and its root is 'alayw, where the waw changes to ya. This shows the importance of Arabic morphology in understanding divine names.
The change from waw to ya is a phonetic rule to make the word lighter.
3
Grasp the first interpretation: moral highness as negation only
The first definition says Allah's highness is moral (ma'nawi), meaning He is declared free from all defects. This automatically includes all attributes of negation (sifat as-sulub).
The attributes of negation are: pre-eternity, everlastingness, self-subsistence, dissimilarity, and oneness.
4
Grasp the second interpretation: negation plus affirmation
The alternative definition adds that Allah's highness also means being described with every perfection, thus including the attributes of meaning (sifat al-ma'ani): knowledge, will, power, life, hearing, sight, speech.
This makes al-'Aliyy a comprehensive name (ism jāmi').
5
Reflect on the comprehensiveness
Ask yourself: Why does the author offer two interpretations? The second is more inclusive, showing that al-'Aliyy can summarize both negation and affirmation, making it one of the most encompassing names.
This helps in understanding the richness of divine names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(العلي) مِنَ العُلُو، وهو الرَّفْعَةُ، فأصلُه: «عَلَيْو»، اجتمعت الياء والواو، وسَبَقَتْ إحداهما بالسكون فقُلبت الواو ياء، وأدْعِمتْ فيها الياءُ (۲) (٢) وعلوه تعالى معنوي، عبارةٌ عن تنزيهه تعالى عَنْ كُل نقص، فيتضمَّنُ اتصافه تعالى بجميعِ صِفاتِ السُّلوبِ. ولكَ أنْ تقولَ: عُلُوُّه تعالى عِبارَةٌ عَنْ تنزيهِهِ عَنْ كُلِّ نَقْصٍ، وانصافه بكُلِّ كَمالِ، فيشْمَلُ صِفاتِ المعاني أيضًا (۳). (۳) فبهذا يكون من الأسماء الجامعة، أي لنفي النقائص وإثبات الكمالات. وصفات السلوب هي: القدم، والبقاء، والقيام بالنفس، والمخالفة للحوادث، والوحدانية، وصفات المعاني هي: العلم، والإرادة، والقدرة، والحياة، والسمع، والبصر، والكلام.
Al-'Aliyy: from al-'uluww, which is ar-raf'ah (elevation). Its origin is 'alayw, the ya and waw came together, one of them was preceded by sukun so the waw was turned into a ya and incorporated into the ya (2). And His Highness, exalted be He, is moral, an expression of declaring Him free from every defect, thus it includes His being described with all attributes of negation (sifat as-sulub). And you may say: His Highness is an expression of declaring Him free from every defect and His being described with every perfection, so it includes the attributes of meaning (sifat al-ma'ani) as well (3). (3) Thus it becomes one of the comprehensive names, i.e., for negating defects and affirming perfections. The attributes of negation are: pre-eternity, everlastingness, self-subsistence, dissimilarity to originated things, and oneness. The attributes of meaning are: knowledge, will, power, life, hearing, sight, and speech.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage defines the divine name al-'Aliyy. First, it gives the linguistic derivation from 'uluww (height) and raf'ah (elevation). It explains the etymology: the root is 'alayw, where the waw is changed to ya' and then merged because it is lighter. Then it clarifies that Allah's highness is not physical but moral (ma'nawi), meaning He is free from all imperfection, which entails His being described with all the attributes of negation (sifat as-sulub). It then offers an alternative expression: His highness is His being free from all imperfection and described with all perfection, which also includes the attributes of meaning (sifat al-ma'ani). The footnote (3) explains that this makes al-'Aliyy one of the comprehensive names (al-asma' al-jami'ah), as it combines negation of defects and affirmation of perfections. It then lists the attributes of negation (pre-eternity, everlastingness, self-subsistence, dissimilarity, oneness) and the attributes of meaning (knowledge, will, power, life, hearing, sight, speech). The passage thus shows how a single name can encompass both types of attributes.

صفات السلوب
صفات المعاني
الأسماء الجامعة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Allah's highness ('uluww) is physical, like being above the heavens in a spatial sense.
Correction: The passage clarifies that Allah's highness is moral (ma'nawi), meaning it is about transcendence and perfection, not physical elevation.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Al-'Aliyy is a comprehensive divine name that, through its moral meaning, encompasses both the negation of all defects and the affirmation of all perfections.

The Divine Name al-Wahid: Three Aspects of Oneness
Defines al-Wahid and enumerates three aspects of divine oneness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the comprehensive meaning of al-Wahid and the three aspects of divine oneness.

Core Idea
God's oneness means no partner in essence, attributes, or actions.

This lesson introduces the divine name al-Wahid (the One). Imam Dardir explains that it signifies freedom from any partner in essence, attributes, and actions. The footnote then unpacks three essential aspects of oneness, giving a complete picture of tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how the definition leads into the three aspects.
The footnote is part of the original text.
2
Identify the three aspects
The footnote lists three aspects of oneness. Find each one and notice the phrasing: negation of multiplicity, negation of a peer, and exclusive creation and management.
3

This connects to the learning objective.

Al-Wahid: meaning the one who is free from partner in essence, attributes, and actions (4). So He is free from opponent (ad-didd) and rival (an-nidd).

Commentary
This passage defines al-Wahid (the One). It states that al-Wahid means the one who is free from any partner in His essence, attributes, and actions. The footnote then elaborates on the meaning of oneness (wahdaniyya) for Allah, enumerating three aspects: (1) negation of multiplicity in His essence, meaning He is not composed of parts; (2) negation of any peer or partner in His essence or any attribute; (3) His exclusive sovereignty in bringing into existence, creating, and managing all affairs. The passage concludes that He is free from any opposite (didd) or rival (nidd). The three aspects together give a comprehensive understanding of divine unity.

Allah's Omniscience: The Meaning of al-'Alim
Defines al-'Alim as knowing all that is, is not, and will be.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's attribute al-'Alim means He knows everything: what will happen, what will not happen, and what exists, including all categories of beings.

Core Idea
Allah's knowledge covers even what never happens.

This short but profound passage from Imam Dardir's commentary defines the divine name al-'Alim (the All-Knowing). It reveals that Allah's knowledge is not limited to actual events but extends to all possibilities and impossibilities. Understanding this expands our appreciation of divine omniscience.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly, noting the terms for what will be, what will not be, and what is.
The footnotes (2), (1), (6), (7) are part of the original text.
2
Identify the three categories of knowledge
The passage lists three things Allah knows: what will be (ma yakun), what will not be (ma la yakun), and what is (ma huwa ka'in). Recognize that 'what is' refers to existing things.
3
Understand the footnoted expansions
The passage then clarifies that this knowledge includes impossibles (mustahilat), contingents (ja'izat), and necessary existents (wajibat). This shows Allah's knowledge encompasses every conceivable category.
Impossibles are things that cannot exist, yet Allah knows them as such.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that Allah only knows what actually happens. This passage shows He also knows what does not happen and what cannot happen.

The footnotes clarify that this knowledge includes the categories of impossibles (mustahilat), contingents (ja'izat), and necessary existents (wajibat). In brief, Allah's knowledge encompasses absolutely everything: past, present, future, possibilities, and impossibilities. The definition highlights the comprehensiveness of divine omniscience.

The Names al-Fard, al-Ghani, and al-Majid
Defines al-Fard, al-Ghani, al-Majid and notes the opening's rhetorical beauty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of three divine names—al-Fard, al-Ghani, and al-Majid—and appreciate the rhetorical beauty of the poem's opening.

Core Idea
Divine names reveal Allah's uniqueness, self-sufficiency, and generosity.

This passage from Imam Dardir's commentary defines three key names of Allah mentioned in the poem's opening line: al-Fard, al-Ghani, and al-Majid. Each name highlights a different aspect of Allah's perfection, and the author also notes the rhetorical skill in beginning the poem with these names. Understanding these names deepens our appreciation of Allah's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage includes footnotes that expand on the meanings.
2
Understand each name's definition
Focus on each name separately: al-Fard (the Solitary), al-Ghani (the Self-Sufficient), and al-Majid (the Glorious). Note how each definition emphasizes a distinct aspect of Allah's perfection.
Al-Ghani has a longer explanation because it implies absolute independence.
3
Reflect on the rhetorical beauty
Consider why the commentator remarks on 'bara'at al-istihlal' (opening rhetoric). Think about how beginning a poem with these names is appropriate to the purpose of the text.
This shows the poem's high literary quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الفرد) أي: الواحدِ ذاتا وصفات وأفعالاً.
(الغني) عَنْ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ (۱)، فلا يفتَقِرُ إِلى مَحَلَّ ولا تُخصص (۲) ولا مُعين ولا وزير ولا غَير ذلك، فالغنى المطلَقُ يتضَمَّنُ اتّصافه تعالى بجميع الصفاتِ السلبية والكماليّة.
(الماجد) قيل : معناه الكريمُ الواسِعُ العطاء، وقيل: الشريف العظيم.
ولا يخفى ما في هذا البيت مِنْ بَراعة الاستهلال (۳).
(۱) له تعالى الغنى المطلق فهذا الاسم من الأسماء الجامعة لنفي النقائص وإثبات الكمالات.
(۲) لا يفتقر إلى محل أي ذات تقوم به، لأنه تعالى ذات وليس صفةً، والمراد بالمخصص الفاعل لأنه تعالى لم يخصه غيره بالوجود، فوجوده تعالى لذاته لا لغيره.
(۳) براعة الاستهلال: كون ابتداء الكلام مناسبًا للمقصود، وذلك بأن يأتي المتكلم في طالع كلامه بما يشعر بمقصوده.
Al-Fard: meaning the One in essence, attributes, and actions. Al-Ghani: free of any need of anything (1), so He does not depend on a locus, nor a specifier (2), nor a helper, nor a vizier, nor anything else. So absolute self-sufficiency entails His being described with all attributes of negation and perfection. Al-Majid: it is said its meaning is the generous one of vast giving, and it is said the noble, the great. And it is not hidden what is in this line (of the poem) of opening rhetoric (3). (1) To Him, exalted be He, belongs absolute self-sufficiency; thus this name is from the comprehensive names for negating defects and affirming perfections. (2) He does not depend on a locus, i.e., a substrate in which He subsists, because He is an essence, not an attribute, and what is meant by 'specifier' is the agent, because no other specified Him with existence; His existence is due to Himself, not another. (3) Opening rhetoric (bara'at al-istihlal): that the beginning of speech is appropriate to the intended purpose, such that the speaker brings in the outset of his speech what indicates his purpose.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage defines three divine names: al-Fard, al-Ghani, and al-Majid, and then remarks on the rhetorical beauty of the poem's opening. First, al-Fard (the Solitary) means the One in essence, attributes, and actions, emphasizing uniqueness. Second, al-Ghani (the Self-Sufficient) means He is free of need of anything. The footnote explains that this name is comprehensive, negating defects and affirming perfections. It elaborates that Allah does not depend on a locus (mahall), because He is an essence (dhat), not an attribute that subsists in another. Also, He does not depend on a 'specifier' (mukhassis), i.e., an agent that brings Him into existence, because His existence is due to Himself. He needs no helper, vizier, etc. Third, al-Majid (the Glorious) is interpreted either as the Generous of vast giving or the Noble and Great. Finally, the commentator notes that the opening line of the poem displays 'bara'at al-istihlal' (rhetorical opening), where the beginning of the speech is perfectly suited to the intended purpose, indicating the poem's high literary quality.

الفرد
الغني
الماجد
براعة الاستهلال
المحل
المخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Ghani simply means 'wealthy' or 'not in need of anything' but miss that it implies absolute independence from any locus or cause.
Correction: Al-Ghani is a comprehensive name that negates all defects (like needing a locus or specifier) and affirms all perfections.

Key Takeaway
Al-Fard, al-Ghani, and al-Majid are divine names that emphasize Allah's uniqueness, absolute independence, and nobility, and the poem's opening exemplifies rhetorical beauty.

Allah's Names Al-Fard, Al-Ghani, Al-Majid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of Allah's names Al-Fard, Al-Ghani, and Al-Majid as explained by Imam Dardir, and how the opening line of the poem demonstrates rhetorical excellence (barāʿat al-istihlāl).

Allah's independence (al-Ghani) entails His absolute oneness and perfection.

Imam Dardir begins his commentary by unpacking three pivotal divine names mentioned in the opening line of the poem. These names — Al-Fard, Al-Ghani, and Al-Majid — are not merely titles but encapsulate profound theological truths about Allah's essence, independence, and generosity. Moreover, the author highlights the rhetorical craftsmanship (barāʿat al-istihlāl) of the poem's opening, showing how it beautifully hints at its core subject. This lesson will walk you through these names and their implications.

Guided Path
1

2

4

5

مقدمة الشيخ الدردير (الفرد) أي: الواحدِ ذاتا وصفات وأفعالاً. (الغني) عَنْ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ)، فلا يفتَقِرُ إِلى مَحَلَّ ولا تُخصص (۲) ولا مُعين ولا وزير ولا غَير ذلك، فالغنى المطلَقُ يتضَمَّنُ اتّصافه تعالى بجميع الصفاتِ السلبية والكماليّة. الماجد) قيل : معناه الكريمُ الواسِعُ العطاء، وقيل: الشريف العظيم. ولا يخفى ما في هذا البيت مِنْ بَراعة الاستهلال (۳). له تعالى الغنى المطلق فهذا الاسم من الأسماء الجامعة لنفي النقائص وإثبات الكمالات. لا يفتقر إلى محل أي ذات تقوم به، لأنه تعالى ذات وليس صفةً، والمراد بالمخصص الفاعل لأنه تعالى لم يخصه غيره بالوجود، فوجوده تعالى لذاته لا لغيره. براعة الاستهلال: كون ابتداء الكلام مناسبًا للمقصود، وذلك بأن يأتي المتكلم في طالع كلامه بما يشعر بمقصوده.
Introduction of Shaykh al-Dardir: (Al-Fard) i.e., the One in essence, attributes, and actions. (Al-Ghani) independent of everything, so He is not in need of a locus nor a specifier (2), nor any helper, nor minister, nor anything else. Absolute independence necessarily entails His being described with all negative and positive attributes. (Al-Majid) it is said: its meaning is the Generous, the One of abundant giving, and it is said: the Noble, the Great. And it is not hidden what is in this line of excellent opening (3). He—exalted—has absolute independence, so this name is among the comprehensive names that negate deficiencies and affirm perfections. He is not in need of a locus, i.e., a substrate in which He subsists, because He—exalted—is a substance (dhāt) and not an attribute. What is meant by 'specifier' is the agent, because nothing other than Him specified Him with existence; His existence is by Himself, not by another. Excellent opening: that the beginning of speech is appropriate to the intended purpose, such that the speaker comes in the opening of his speech with what indicates his intended purpose.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the opening line of the poem, explaining three divine names. The author first defines al-Fard as 'the One in essence, attributes, and actions,' emphasizing God's absolute oneness. Then he explains al-Ghani: God is independent of everything—He needs no locus (no substrate in which He subsists) and no specifier (no external agent that brought Him into existence). This absolute independence entails that He possesses all negative attributes (negating any lack) and all positive attributes of perfection. The name al-Majid is explained with two interpretations: the Generous, abundant in giving, or the Noble, the Great. The author then notes the rhetorical excellence (barāʿat al-istihlāl) of the poem's opening, i.e., the first words appropriately hint at the intended subject matter. The passage concludes with added clarifications: that al-Ghani is a comprehensive name negating deficiencies and affirming perfections, and that God's independence from a locus affirms that He is a substance (dhāt), not an attribute subsisting in another. The footnote on barāʿat al-istihlāl defines it as beginning speech with what indicates the intended purpose, thus enhancing the poem's elegance.

?

?
Al-Majid means the Generous, the Noble. Do you truly believe that His generosity is not earned by your deeds? In what areas do you struggle to accept His unmerited favor, still feeling you need to 'deserve' His blessings?

Defining Ma'rifa of Allah as Knowing the Three Categories
Dardeer defines ma'rifa as knowledge of wajib, mustahil, ja'iz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that ma'rifa of Allah is defined as knowledge of what is rationally necessary, impossible, and possible regarding Him, not knowledge of His essence.

Core Idea
Knowing Allah means knowing what is wajib, mustahil, and ja'iz for Him — not His essence.

The author, Imam Dardeer, begins by clarifying what 'knowing Allah' truly means. It is not a direct grasp of His essence — that is impossible and not required. Instead, true ma'rifa consists in knowing the three rational judgments as they apply to Allah: what must be affirmed of Him, what must be denied, and what is merely possible. This passage establishes the proper scope of theological knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the translation. Notice how the author first states what ma'rifa is not, then what it is.
The passage has a negative and positive part.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage presents three rational judgments: wajib (necessary), mustahil (impossible), ja'iz (possible). Find where each is mentioned and note that they apply to Allah's right (haqq).
3
Understand the exclusion of essence
The author explicitly says ma'rifa is not knowledge of the essence. Reflect on why: impossibility and lack of obligation. This sets the limit of what we can know.
This guards against the misconception that we must know Allah's essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كانت معرفة الله تعالى عبارة عن معرفة ما يجب في حقه تعالى وما يستحيل وما يجوز، لا معرفة حقيقة الذات العلية ؛ لعدم إمكان ذلك، و لعدم تكليفنا بالان[؟] فسرَ المَعْرِفةَ بما هو المراد، فقال: تفسير المعرفة تقدير وبيان المراد بها ١ - أي: يعرفُ الواجب والمحالا * مع جائز في حقه تعالى.
And since knowledge (ma'rifa) of Allah Most High is the knowledge of what is necessary (wajib) in His right, what is impossible (mustahil), and what is possible (ja'iz), not knowledge of the true essence of the Self-Sufficient; due to the impossibility of that, and because we are not tasked with that, he interpreted knowledge (al-ma'rifa) as what is intended, and said: The interpretation of knowledge: estimation and clarification of what is intended by it: 1. That is, he knows the necessary and the impossible, along with the possible, in His right, Most High.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage defines what knowledge (ma'rifa) of Allah means. It clarifies that it is not knowledge of Allah's essence in itself, which is impossible and not required of us. Rather, it is knowledge of the three rational judgments as they apply to Allah: what is necessary (wajib) for Him, what is impossible (mustahil) for Him, and what is possible (ja'iz) for Him. The author then presents a couplet that summarizes this: 'He knows the wajib and the mustahil, along with the ja'iz in His right'. This interpretation establishes the proper scope of theological knowledge.

Key Takeaway

?

Why the Author Used 'Thubut' and 'Intifa'' Instead of 'Wujud' and 'Adam'
Dardeer explains his terminological choice and defines states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Dardeer chooses the terms 'thubut' (affirmation) and 'intifa'' (negation) over 'wujud' (existence) and 'adam' (non-existence) and how this decision accommodates the doctrine of states (ahwal) in kalam.

Core Idea
Thubut is broader than wujud—including mental existence—allowing definitions to cover the controversial states.

In this passage, Dardeer explains a terminological choice that might seem minor but has significant theological implications. By using 'thubut' and 'intifa'' instead of the more common 'wujud' and 'adam', he ensures that his definitions are inclusive of the 'ahwal' (states)—a concept debated among theologians. This lesson unpacks that choice and the nature of states.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the author's explicit reason for his choice of terms.
Notice the parenthetical footnote marker (2) indicates an important clarification.
2
Understand 'thubut' vs 'wujud'
The footnote explains that 'thubut' is more general than 'wujud': every existent is affirmed, but not every affirmed is existent (some affirmed things exist only in the mind).
This distinction is crucial for including states like 'being knowing' which are not existent attributes.
3
Recognize the theological debate
The passage mentions different positions on the reality of states (ahwal). Identify the majority view (denial) and the minority view (affirmation by some Ash'aris and Mu'tazila).
This shows that Dardeer's terminological choice is not neutral but aligns with a particular theological school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما عبّرتُ بالثُبوتِ والانتفاء دونَ الوُجودِ والعَدَم؛ لتَشْمَلَ التعاريف الأحوال على القول بها، كَكونه تعالى عالما، فإنها لا تَتَّصِفُ بِالوُجودِ ولا بالعَدَم) ، وهذا مِنْ جُملة الأحسَنيّةِ التي أشرنا لها، فتَدَبَّرْ. (۲) الأحوال أي الأمور التي هي واسطة بين الوجود والعدم، فلا توصف بأنها موجودة ولا معدومة، مثل العالمية والقادرية فإنها ثابتة وليست موجودة ولا معدومة، لأن الثبوت أعم من الوجود فكل موجود ثابت وليس كل ثابت موجودا، فالذوات والمعاني لها وجود في الخارج ويصح أن ترى، وأما الثابت فموجود في الأذهان لأنه لا يصح أن يرى، فالذين يثبتون الأحوال العلونها واسطة بين الوجود والعدم، والذين ينفونها يسمونها اعتبارات أو أمورا اعتبارية، فمثلا العالمية أمر اعتباري أي هو عبارة عن قيام صفة العلم به تعالى وهكذا ، والجمهور على نفي الحال وأنه لا واسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم وقالوا الحق أن لا حال والحال من المحال، بينما ذهب بعض الأشاعرة كالباقلاني وإمام الحرمين في قوله الأول وبعض المعتزلة إلى القول بها.
And I only expressed with 'thubut' (affirmation) and 'intifa'' (negation) instead of 'wujud' (existence) and 'adam' (non-existence) so that the definitions encompass the states (ahwal) according to those who affirm them, like His, exalted, being knowing, for they are not described with existence nor non-existence. And this is among the improvements we have indicated, so reflect. (2) The states (ahwal) are the matters that are intermediate between existence and non-existence, so they are not described as existent nor non-existent, like 'worldliness' (al-ʿālamiyya) and 'ability' (al-qādariyya), for they are affirmed (thābita) and are not existent nor non-existent, because affirmation is more general than existence: every existent is affirmed but not every affirmed is existent. So essences (dhawāt) and meanings (maʿānī) have existence in the external and it is valid that they be seen; while the affirmed exists in the minds because it cannot be seen. So those who affirm the states make them an intermediate between existence and non-existence, while those who deny them call them 'considerations' (iʿtibārāt) or 'considerational matters'. For example, 'worldliness' is a considerational matter, i.e., it is the expression of the attribute of knowledge subsisting in Him, exalted. And the majority (jumbūr) deny the state (ḥāl) and that there is no intermediate between the existent and the non-existent, and they said the truth is that there is no state, and the state is from the impossible (al-muḥāl). While some Ashʿaris like al-Bāqillānī and Imām al-Ḥaramayn in his first opinion, and some Muʿtazila, went to affirm them.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage explains the author's choice to use terms 'ثبوت' and 'انتفاء' instead of the more common 'وجود' and 'عدم'. The reason is to include the 'أحوال' (states) in the definitions, as these states are considered by some theologians to be real but not existent nor non-existent. Example: God's being 'worldly' (i.e., the state of being a knower) is not an existent attribute but a state. The footnote elaborates on the concept of الأحوال: they are intermediate between existence and non-existence, like the state of being knowing or able. The note clarifies the relationship between ثبوت and وجود: all existent things are affirmed, but not all affirmed things are existent (since affirmed can be mental). Those who affirm states see them as real but non-existent; those who deny them consider them mere mental considerations. The footnote also mentions the views of the majority (denial of states) and the minority (affirmation, including some Ash'aris and Mu'tazila). This shows the nuanced theological positions within kalam.

Misconception: One might think that 'thubut' and 'wujud' are synonymous and interchangeable.
Correction: The passage shows that 'thubut' is more general, including both external and mental existence, while 'wujud' is limited to external existence. This allows 'thubut' to cover the states, which are affirmed but not existent.

Key Takeaway
Dardeer's choice of 'thubut' and 'intifa'' is a deliberate theological move to include the concept of states (ahwal) in his definitions, highlighting the nuanced discussions in kalam about what can be affirmed of God.

Defining Ḥudūth and Qidam
Defines ḥudūth and qidam with a refutation of philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct Sunni definitions of ḥudūth (creation) and qidam (eternity) and how they differ from the philosophers' equivocal usage.

Core Idea
Ḥudūth means temporal creation from nothing; qidam belongs to Allah alone.

In this lesson, we explore two foundational terms in Islamic theology: ḥudūth (creation/origination) and qidam (eternity). Imam al-Dardīr defines them clearly and distinguishes the orthodox Sunni view from the equivocating philosophers. The passage establishes that the world came into existence after non-existence, and that only Allah is eternal without beginning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic poem line and its commentary. Notice how the poet defines ḥudūth and qidam as opposites.
The poem line is concise; the commentary expands it.
2
Understand the definition of ḥudūth
The commentary explains that ḥudūth means existence after non-existence. The philosophers disagree but their definition is rejected.
Pay attention to the refutation of the philosophers.
3
Understand the definition of qidam
Qidam is the opposite of ḥudūth, meaning absence of beginning of existence. It belongs only to Allah.
The passage states there is no intermediate between ḥudūth and qidam.
4
Reflect on the consensus
Imam al-Dardīr states that believing in the world's eternity is disbelief by consensus. This highlights the gravity of the issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الأول: الإلهيات لأولي الألباب (۱) أَوَلَمْ يَنْظُرُوا فِي مَلَكُوتِ السَّمَاوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ وَمَا خَلَقَ اللَّهُ مِن إلى غَيْرِ ذلك مِنَ الآياتِ. ١٩ - حُدوثُهُ: وُجوده بعْدَ العَدَمُ * وضده هو المُسَمّى بالقِدَمُ ۱۹ (حدوثُهُ وُجوده بعد العدَم) يعني: أنَّ حدوث العالَم عِبارَةٌ عَنْ وُجودِه بعد عدمه، خلافا للفلاسفة (۳)، فإنهم ذهبوا إلى قدمه، ومع ذلك أطلقوا القَولَ بحدوث ما سوى الله تعالى، لكن بمعنى الاحتياج إلى الغَيرِ، لا بمعنى سبق العدم عليه، ومُعتقد ذلك كافر بإجماع المسلمين. (٤) (وضدُّه) أي: ضِدُّ الحدوثِ، أي: مُقابِلُه، يعني عدَمَ أوليّةِ الوُجودِ (هو المُسَمّى بالقدم) ولا يكونُ إلا الله وحده كما سيأتي، ولا واسطة بَينَ الحدوثِ والقِدَمِ. معنى الحدوث ومعنى القدم (۱) سورة آل عمران: الآية ۱۹۰ (۲) سورة الأعراف: من الآية ١٨٥ (۳) العالم عند الفلاسفة قديم بالزمان بمعنى عدم أوليته وإن كان حادثا بالذات، ومعنى حدوثه بالذات: احتياجه إلى المؤثر ولو بالتعليل عندهم، والقديم بالذات الواجب وحده، وهو ما استغني عن المؤثر، والحادث بالزمن ما سبقه عدم [حاشية الأمير على الجوهرة ص ٢٤] وعلى ذلك فالأقسام أربعة: القدم الذاتي ويقابله الحدوث الذاتي، والقدم الزماني ويقابله الحدوث الزماني، والمراد بالحدوث عند المتكلمين الحدوث الزماني بمعنى سبق العدم، والمراد به عند الفلاسفة الحدوث الذاتي بمعنى الاحتياج إلى المؤثر، فتدبر (٤) الموجودات ثلاثة: أحدها موجود ليس له ابتداء ولا انتهاء وهو الباري، وموجود له ابتداء وله انتهاء وهو العالم الدنيوي والثالث له ابتداء ولا انتهاء له وهو العالم الأخروي [حاشية السباعي، ص ٧٢] ٦٧
The First Section: Divine Matters (al-Ilāhiyyāt). 'For those of understanding' (1) 'Have they not looked at the dominion of the heavens and the earth and what Allah has created of...' and other verses. 19 - Its creation (ḥudūth): its existence after non-existence, and its opposite is called eternity (qidam). 19 (Its creation is its existence after non-existence) meaning: that the creation of the world is an expression of its existence after its non-existence, contrary to the philosophers (3), for they held its eternity, yet they still spoke of the creation of everything other than Allah, but in the sense of need for another, not in the sense of precedence of non-existence over it. And one who believes that is a disbeliever by consensus of the Muslims. (4) (And its opposite) i.e., the opposite of creation, i.e., its counterpart, meaning the absence of priority of existence (is called eternity) and it belongs only to Allah alone as will come, and there is no intermediate between creation and eternity. The meaning of creation and the meaning of eternity. (1) Surah Al 'Imran: verse 190 (2) Surah Al-A'raf: from verse 185. (3) The world according to the philosophers is eternal in time, meaning the absence of its priority, even if it is originated in essence, and the meaning of its essential origination is its need for an effecter even by way of causation according to them. And the eternally self-sufficient is the Necessary alone, which is independent of an effecter, and the temporally originated is that which non-existence preceded. [Hashiyat al-Amir on al-Jawhara, p. 24] And accordingly the divisions are four: essential eternity and its opposite essential origination, temporal eternity and its opposite temporal origination. What is meant by creation according to the theologians (mutakallimūn) is temporal creation in the sense of precedence of non-existence, and what is meant by it according to the philosophers is essential creation in the sense of need for an effecter. So ponder. (4) The existents are three: one existent has no beginning and no end—that is the Creator; an existent has a beginning and an end—that is the worldly world; and the third has a beginning and no end—that is the afterlife world. [Hashiyat al-Sibāʿī, p. 72] 67
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage defines two key terms: ḥudūth (creation/origination) and qidam (eternity). It begins with the section heading 'Divine Matters' (al-Ilāhiyyāt) and cites a Quranic verse about contemplating the heavens and earth. Then it presents the poem line: 'Its creation: its existence after non-existence, and its opposite is called eternity.' The commentary explains that ḥudūth of the world means its existence after non-existence, contrary to the philosophers who claim the world is eternal in time but originated in essence (i.e., dependent on a cause). However, they also use the term 'ḥudūth' to mean need for another, not temporal precedence. The author states that believing in the world's eternity (in the sense of no beginning) is disbelief by consensus. Next, the opposite (ḍidd) of ḥudūth is qidam, meaning the absence of priority of existence, which belongs only to Allah, with no intermediate state between the two. The footnotes clarify the philosophical distinctions: essential vs. temporal eternity and creation. According to the mutakallimūn, ḥudūth means temporal creation (preceded by non-existence), while philosophers mean essential dependence. Another footnote divides existents into three: the Creator (neither beginning nor end), the worldly world (both beginning and end), and the afterlife (beginning but no end). This lesson firmly establishes the creedal definitions and refutes the philosophers' equivocation.

Key Terms
ḥudūth
qidam
ḥādith
qadīm

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'ḥudūth' can mean mere dependence on a cause, as the philosophers claim, allowing the world to be eternal while still calling it 'created'.
Correction: The passage clarifies that ḥudūth in the Sunni view always means existence after non-existence (temporal creation). The philosophers' usage is rejected, and their belief is considered disbelief.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does ḥudūth mean according to the mutakallimūn?
A) Dependence on a cause.
B) Existence after non-existence.
C) Absence of priority.
D) Being contingent.

Key Takeaway
Ḥudūth means existence after non-existence, and qidam means no beginning of existence, exclusive to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that everything you rely on in this world—your health, wealth, family—is created, temporal, and could cease to exist in an instant? How does this awareness shape your attachment to them?
?
If only Allah is qadīm (eternal without beginning), then what does that mean for your pursuit of worldly permanence? Where are you seeking immortality, and how can you redirect that to the only Eternal One?
?
The philosophers' error was to confuse creation with dependence. In your own life, do you ever treat something that is created (like a person or possession) as if it were necessary and eternal? What would change if you saw it truly as ḥādith, here for a limited time?

Existence as a Necessary Attribute and the Rule of Detailed Belief
Dardeer establishes existence as necessary and introduces detailed vs. general belief rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that existence is a necessary attribute of Allah and the principle that only attributes with specific evidence require detailed belief.

Core Idea
Allah's existence is necessary, but only proven attributes need detailed belief.

Dardeer begins by stating that being described with existence is among the necessary things for the Worshipped One. He then explains a crucial methodological point: while Allah's perfections are infinite, we are only required to believe in detail those attributes for which specific evidence has been given. This sets the foundation for the forthcoming list of necessary attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the verse and the explanation.
2
Identify the key distinction
Look for the part where Dardeer distinguishes between general belief in infinite perfections and detailed belief in attributes with proof.
This is the main methodological point.
3
Reflect on the implications
Think about why this distinction matters for theology: it sets limits on what we must affirm in detail.
This helps avoid overextending theological requirements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاعلَمُ بأنَّ الوصْفَ بالوُجودِ * مِنْ واجِباتِ الواحِدِ المعبود
-
إذا علمتَ أنَّه يجب على كُلِّ مُكَلَّف أن يعرِفَ ما يحِبُ وما يستحيلُ وما يجوز له الواجبة تعالى، وعلمت الطريق الموصل إلى المعرفة ..
(فاعلَمُ بأنَّ الوصْف) أي: اتصافه تعالى (بـ) صفة (الوُجودِ)، ويصح أن يُراد أيضًا بالوصفِ الصَّفةُ)، والباء للتصوير والتفسير، أي: بأنَّ الصِّفةَ المُفسّرة بالوجودِ مِنْ واجبات الواحد المعبود) أي: بعضُ الصِّفاتِ الواجبة له تعالى؛ إذ الواجبات له تعالى كثيرة لا تَنْحَصُرُ فيما ذُكِرَ هنا؛ لأنَّ صِفاتِه تعالى الكمالية لا تتناهي. إلا أنه لا يجب علينا تفصيلُ ما لم يقم عليه الدليلُ بالخصوص، بل الواجب أن نعتقد أنَّ كمالاته تعالى لا تتناهى على الإجمال، وأما ما قامَ عليه الدليل بخصوصه فيجب اعتقاده تفصيلا.
‘So know that the attribute of existence is among the necessary things of the Worshipped One.’ – When you know that it is obligatory for every responsible person to know what is necessary, impossible, and permissible for Allah, and you know the path leading to knowledge... (So know that the attribute) i.e., His being described with the attribute of existence, and it is also valid to mean by ‘attribute’ the description itself, and the ba’ is for depiction and explanation, i.e., that the attribute explained as existence is among the necessary things of the Worshipped One, i.e., some of the necessary attributes for Him, for the necessary attributes for Him are many and not limited to what is mentioned here, because His attributes of perfection are infinite. However, it is not obligatory upon us to detail what specific proof has not been established, but rather it is obligatory to believe that His perfections are infinite in general, and as for what specific proof has been established, it is obligatory to believe it in detail.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the necessity of the attribute of existence for Allah. Dardeer starts by quoting the verse: 'So know that the attribute of existence is among the necessary things of the Worshipped One.' He then provides background: every morally responsible person must know what is necessary, impossible, and permissible for Allah, as well as the method to attain this knowledge. He then interprets the verse: 'the attribute' refers to being described with the attribute of existence, and the preposition 'ba' serves to depict or explain. So the meaning is that the attribute of existence is one of the necessary attributes of Allah. Dardeer emphasizes that Allah's necessary attributes are many and not limited to those mentioned in the text, because His perfections are infinite. However, he makes an important distinction: we are not obliged to believe in detail every attribute for which no specific proof has been given; rather, we must believe in the infinite perfections of Allah in general. But for those attributes that are proven specifically by evidence, we must believe in them in detail. This sets the stage for the enumeration of the thirteen attributes that will follow later.

Correction: Dardeer clarifies that only attributes with specific evidence require detailed belief; otherwise, general belief in infinite perfections is sufficient.
