Proving God's Self-Subsistence
Imam Dardeer proves God's self-subsistence via elimination of alternatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that God must be self-subsistent (qiyām bi nafs) and why alternatives—being an accident or a quality—are impossible.

Core Idea
Self-subsistence is necessary because God cannot depend on a substrate or specifier.

Imam Dardeer now proves the attribute of self-subsistence by eliminating every alternative. If God were not self-subsistent, He would have to be either an accident (needing a substrate) or an eternal quality (needing a subject). Both lead to absurdities, especially the impossibility of a quality possessing other qualities. Follow the logical steps in the text and see how the proof is structured as a conditional syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the main text (the matn) first, then the commentary (the sharh) to see the full syllogistic reasoning.
The passage uses technical terms like 'arad' and 'sifa'—note their definitions.
2
Identify the two alternatives to self-subsistence
The text says if not self-subsistent, then either accident (arad) or eternal quality (sifa). Understand why each is impossible for God.
Accidents are dependent and originated; qualities need a subject.
3
Understand the syllogism for 'not a quality'
Follow the second syllogism: if God were a quality, He could not be described with attributes of meanings (like knowledge), but He is, so He is not a quality.
This is based on the principle that a quality does not accept another quality.
4
Reach the conclusion: self-subsistence is necessary
Since both alternatives are false, the opposite—self-subsistence—must be true. This is the desired result.
The proof is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا القِيامُ بِالنَّفْسِ (١) فَلأنَّهُ لَوْ قامَ بِغَيْرِهِ لَكانَ عَرَضًا، وقَدْ تَقَدَّمَ بَيانُ الأَعْراضِ، أو كانَ صِفَةً قَديمَةً قائِمَةً بِمَوْصوفِها (٣)، فَيَلْزَمُ أنْ لا يَتَّصِفَ بِصِفاتِ المَعاني؛ لِمَا مَرَّ (٣)، وهو باطِلٌ.
(١) وأمَّا القِيامُ بِالنَّفْسِ إلخ. تَقْريرُ الدَّليلِ: المولى يَجِبُ لَهُ القِيامُ بِالنَّفْسِ، لأنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَقُمْ بِنَفْسِهِ لَكانَ عَرَضًا أو صِفَةً، وإذا كانَ كَذلِكَ احْتاجَ إلى المَحَلِّ أو إلى المُخَصِّصِ، لكنَّ كَوْنَهُ عَرَضًا أو صِفَةً مُحالٌ فَبَطَلَ مَلْزومُهُ وهو «لَمْ يَقُمْ بِنَفْسِهِ» فَثَبَتَ نَقيضُهُ وهو وُجوبُ القِيامِ بِالنَّفْسِ وهو المَطْلوبُ.
(٢) وهو إشارَةٌ إلى قياسٍ اسْتِثْنائِيٍّ؛ وتَقْريرُهُ: المولى لَيْسَ بِصِفَةٍ، لأنَّهُ لَوْ كانَ صِفَةً لَزِمَ أنْ لا يَتَّصِفَ بِصِفاتِ المَعاني، واللازِمُ وهو أنْ لا يَتَّصِفَ باطِلٌ، وإذا بَطَلَ اللازِمُ بَطَلَ المَلْزومُ وهو كَوْنُهُ صِفَةً، وإذا بَطَلَ المَلْزومُ ثَبَتَ نَقيضُهُ وهو كَوْنُهُ لَيْسَ بِصِفَةٍ، وإذا انْتَفى كَوْنُهُ صِفَةً ثَبَتَ أنَّهُ ذاتٌ وهو المَطْلوبُ.
(٣) «لِمَا مَرَّ» أي مِن أنَّ الصِّفَةَ لا تَقْبَلُ صِفَةً أُخْرى.
As for self-subsistence (1): because if He subsisted through another, He would be an accident, and the explanation of accidents has preceded; or He would be an eternal quality subsisting in its subject (3), then it would necessarily follow that He is not described with the attributes of meanings, due to what has passed (3), and that is false. (1) As for self-subsistence, etc. The formulation of the proof: the Master must have self-subsistence, because if He did not subsist by Himself, He would be an accident or a quality; and if that were the case, He would need a substrate (maḥall) or a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ); but His being an accident or a quality is impossible; therefore its antecedent – 'He did not subsist by Himself' – is false, so its opposite – the necessity of self-subsistence – is established, and that is the desired conclusion. (2) This is a reference to an exceptional syllogism; its formulation: the Master is not a quality, because if He were a quality, it would necessarily follow that He is not described with the attributes of meanings, and the necessary consequence – that He is not described – is false; so when the consequence is false, the antecedent – His being a quality – is false; and when the antecedent is false, its opposite – that He is not a quality – is established; and when His being a quality is negated, it is established that He is a self-subsistent essence (dhāt), and that is the desired conclusion. (3) 'Due to what has passed' means that a quality does not accept another quality.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage proves the attribute of qiyām bi nafs (self-subsistence). The main text argues that if God were not self-subsistent, He would either be an accident (ʿaraḍ) – which subsists in a substrate – or an eternal quality (ṣifa) subsisting in a subject. Both possibilities are impossible for God. The first alternative (accident) is impossible because accidents are originated and dependent; the second alternative (eternal quality) would entail that God cannot be described with the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) – such as knowledge, power, will – because a quality cannot support another quality (as explained in footnote 3). Since God does possess those attributes, He cannot be a quality. Therefore, He must be self-subsistent. The footnotes provide full formal syllogisms: the first (1) is a conditional proof that if not self-subsistent, then accident or quality, both impossible, so self-subsistence is necessary. The second (2) is a separate syllogism proving that God is not a quality at all, using the impossibility of a quality being described with other qualities. The conclusion is that God is a self-subsistent essence (dhāt).

Key Terms
قيام بالنفس
عرض
صفة
محل
مخصص
ذات
صفات المعاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-subsistence merely means God is independent of creation.
Correction: Self-subsistence (qiyām bi nafs) specifically means God does not need a substrate (maḥall) or a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ); it is a technical term in kalām about ontological independence from a locus.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why cannot God be a quality (ṣifa)?
A) Because qualities are temporal
B) Because a quality cannot be described with other qualities (attributes of meanings)
C) Because qualities are multiple
D) Because qualities are dependent on a subject

Key Takeaway
God must be self-subsistent because the alternatives—being an accident or a quality—lead to contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live with the certainty that God needs nothing from you? How does that freedom affect the way you worship and rely on Him?
?
When you feel pressured to prove your worth to others, are you forgetting that God is self-sufficient and needs no validation? How can you shift your focus from human approval to divine acceptance?
?
If God were dependent on a substrate or specifier, He would be limited. Do you ever act as if God needs your praise or obedience for His own benefit? How can you purify your intention to worship only because He is worthy, not because He needs it?

The Core Argument for God's Oneness
Imam Dardeer states the primary premise for proving oneness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the existence of a peer for Allah would necessitate incapability and temporality, which is impossible for the eternal God.

Core Idea
No equal can exist with Allah, else both would be incapable and originated.

Imam Dardeer begins the proof for God's oneness with a simple conditional: if there were a peer, there would be incapability, and every incapable thing is originated. Since Allah is eternal and free from deficiency, this impossibility eliminates any possibility of a partner. This lesson unpacks that powerful premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation carefully. Note the conditional structure: 'if... then...'
The phrase 'due to what has passed' refers to earlier proofs about incapability.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on nazir (peer), 'ajz (incapability), and hadith (originated). Understand why 'ajz implies hadath (temporality).
In kalam, everything originated is preceded by non-existence.
3
Reflect on the implication
If God had a peer, both would be incapable. Since incapability is a deficiency incompatible with divinity, the assumption is false.
This argument is concise yet decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا الوَحْدانِيَّةُ فَلأنَّهُ لَوْ كانَ لَهُ نَظيرٌ في ذاتِهِ أو صِفاتِهِ لِلَزِمَ العَجْزُ، لِمَا مَرَّ ( )، وكُلُّ عاجِزٍ حادِثٌ.
(ه) هذا دَليلُ المُلازَمَةِ، وهذا البُرْهانُ يُسَمَّى بُرْهانَ التَّوارُدِ لِتَوارُدِ قُدْرَتَيْهِما على أثَرٍ واحِدٍ، وتَقْريرُهُ عِنْدَ اتِّفاقِهِما على إيجادِ شَيْءٍ مُعَيَّنٍ: تَوارُدُ قُدْرَتَيْهِما على شَيْءٍ واحِدٍ لِعُمومِ تَعَلُّقِهِما لَوْ تَعَدَّدَ الإلَهُ لَزِمَ بِكُلِّ مُمْكِنٍ، وتَوارُدُهُما يُؤَدِّي إلى عَدَمِ وُجودِهِ، لأنَّهُ إمَّا أنْ يُوجَدَ بِهِما مَعًا فَيَلْزَمُ تَحْصيلُ الحاصِلِ، يُوجَدَ بِهِما مَعًا بأنْ وُجِدَ بِأحَدِهِما فَيَلْزَمُ عَجْزُ الآخَرِ لاِنْعِقادِ الماثِلَةِ بَيْنَهُما فَلا يُمْكِنُ إيجادُهُ بِواحِدَةٍ مِنَ القُدْرَتَيْنِ. وإذا كانَ هذا عِنْدَ الاتِّفاقِ فَعِنْدَ الاختِلافِ مِن بابِ أَوْلى. فالتَّعَدُّدُ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِعَدَمِ إيجادِ الأَشْياءِ عِنْدَ الاتِّفاقِ وعِنْدَ الاختِلافِ، والمُشاهَدُ وُجودُ الأَشْياءِ، فَدَلَّتْ مُشاهَدَةُ وُجودِها على عَدَمِ التَّعَدُّدِ وهو المَطْلوبُ. [السَّباعِي، ص ٣١٧] وهو مُحالٌ، وإمَّا ألا
As for oneness: because if He had an equal in His essence or attributes, incapability would necessarily follow, due to what has passed ( ), and every incapable thing is originated. (5) This is the proof of the implication, and this proof is called the proof of mutual hindrance (burhān al-tawārud) due to the mutual hindrance of their two powers upon one effect. Its formulation when they agree on bringing into existence a specific thing: their two powers converge upon one thing due to the universality of their attachment; if there were multiple gods, it would apply to every possible thing. Their convergence leads to its non-existence, because either it would be brought into existence by both together, which would entail the obtaining of the obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil); or it would be brought into existence by one of them, which would entail the incapability of the other due to the obstacle (māthila) between them, so it could not be brought into existence by either power. If this is the case when they agree, then when they disagree it is even more so. Therefore multiplicity necessitates the non-bringing into existence of things both when they agree and when they disagree. But the existence of things is observed, so the observation of their existence indicates the absence of multiplicity, and that is the desired conclusion. [al-Saba'i, p. 317] And that is impossible, or else...
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage proves the attribute of waḥdāniyya (oneness) – that God is one without partner. The main text argues that if God had a peer (nazīr) in essence or attributes, it would necessitate incapability (ʿajz) in each, and everything incapable is originated, which is impossible for God. The footnote (5) provides the classical 'proof of mutual hindrance' (burhān al-tawārud) often used in kalam. The logic is: if there were two gods, they would either agree or disagree on creating a specific thing. If they agree, both powers would converge on one effect; either both create it together (which is impossible because it would be a duplication of the already existent), or one creates it while the other is incapable, proving its incapability. If they disagree, one wants to create and the other wants to prevent it, leading to conflict and non-existence of the thing. Since we observe the existence of things, the assumption of multiple gods is false. Therefore, God is necessarily one. The phrase 'each incapable is originated' establishes that incapability entails temporality, which cannot apply to the eternal God.

Key Terms
وحدانية
نظير
عجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that multiple gods could coexist if they cooperate.
Correction: The argument shows that even cooperation leads to incapability, because each power would depend on the other, and each would be incapable of independent action.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the existence of a peer for Allah entail?
A) Wisdom
B) Incapability
C) Power
D) Knowledge

Key Takeaway
The possibility of a peer for Allah is negated because it would lead to incapability and temporality, contradicting His eternal perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever, in your heart, treat a person, a possession, or a status as an 'equal' to Allah in the sense that you believe they can bring you benefit or harm independent of Him? How does the logical impossibility of a peer challenge that hidden assumption? This week, when you feel fear or hope tied to something other than Allah, pause and recite the argument from this lesson: 'If there were an equal, incapability would follow.' Will you commit to that practice?
?
The proof shows that any supposed partner would make Allah 'incapable' (عاجز). Where in your life do you act as if Allah's power is limited by circumstances, people, or your own mistakes? How does acknowledging His absolute oneness strip those limitations away? Write down one area of worry and place it under the statement 'لا نظير له' — He has no peer.
?
If you truly internalized that 'every incapable thing is originated' and that Allah is neither incapable nor originated, would your view of your own weaknesses change? Do you see your own temporary limitations as a sign that you are a created being dependent on the One without peer? How does that humility reshape your dua today?

intermediate
5 min
The Proof of Mutual Hindrance (Burhān al-Tawārud)
Imam Dardeer presents the detailed proof of mutual hindrance against multiple gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed logical proof that multiple gods cannot coexist, based on the impossibility of two powers acting on one thing.

Core Idea
If two gods exist, their powers would hinder each other, leading to non-existence of things.

In this lesson, we dive into the classical proof of mutual hindrance (burhān al-tawārud). Imam Dardeer explains that if there were two gods with universal power, their powers would converge on every possible thing. Whether they agree or disagree, the result is that nothing could exist. Since we observe existence, there must be only one God.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
Imagine two gods with equal and universal power. Their powers would apply to every possible thing.
The proof assumes both have complete power over all things.
2
Consider agreement
If they agree on creating a specific thing, either both create it together (impossible duplication) or one creates it while the other is unable (proving incapability).
The term 'taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil' (obtaining the obtained) means something that already exists cannot be brought into existence again.
3
Consider disagreement
If one wants to create and the other wants to prevent, conflict leads to non-existence.
Disagreement is even more problematic than agreement.
4
Apply to observed world
Since we see things exist, the assumption of multiple gods must be false.
This is a proof by contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا الوَحْدانِيَّةُ فَلأنَّهُ لَوْ كانَ لَهُ نَظيرٌ في ذاتِهِ أو صِفاتِهِ لِلَزِمَ العَجْزُ، لِمَا مَرَّ ( )، وكُلُّ عاجِزٍ حادِثٌ.
(ه) هذا دَليلُ المُلازَمَةِ، وهذا البُرْهانُ يُسَمَّى بُرْهانَ التَّوارُدِ لِتَوارُدِ قُدْرَتَيْهِما على أثَرٍ واحِدٍ، وتَقْريرُهُ عِنْدَ اتِّفاقِهِما على إيجادِ شَيْءٍ مُعَيَّنٍ: تَوارُدُ قُدْرَتَيْهِما على شَيْءٍ واحِدٍ لِعُمومِ تَعَلُّقِهِما لَوْ تَعَدَّدَ الإلَهُ لَزِمَ بِكُلِّ مُمْكِنٍ، وتَوارُدُهُما يُؤَدِّي إلى عَدَمِ وُجودِهِ، لأنَّهُ إمَّا أنْ يُوجَدَ بِهِما مَعًا فَيَلْزَمُ تَحْصيلُ الحاصِلِ، يُوجَدَ بِهِما مَعًا بأنْ وُجِدَ بِأحَدِهِما فَيَلْزَمُ عَجْزُ الآخَرِ لاِنْعِقادِ الماثِلَةِ بَيْنَهُما فَلا يُمْكِنُ إيجادُهُ بِواحِدَةٍ مِنَ القُدْرَتَيْنِ. وإذا كانَ هذا عِنْدَ الاتِّفاقِ فَعِنْدَ الاختِلافِ مِن بابِ أَوْلى. فالتَّعَدُّدُ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِعَدَمِ إيجادِ الأَشْياءِ عِنْدَ الاتِّفاقِ وعِنْدَ الاختِلافِ، والمُشاهَدُ وُجودُ الأَشْياءِ، فَدَلَّتْ مُشاهَدَةُ وُجودِها على عَدَمِ التَّعَدُّدِ وهو المَطْلوبُ. [السَّباعِي، ص ٣١٧] وهو مُحالٌ، وإمَّا ألا
As for oneness: because if He had an equal in His essence or attributes, incapability would necessarily follow, due to what has passed ( ), and every incapable thing is originated. (5) This is the proof of the implication, and this proof is called the proof of mutual hindrance (burhān al-tawārud) due to the mutual hindrance of their two powers upon one effect. Its formulation when they agree on bringing into existence a specific thing: their two powers converge upon one thing due to the universality of their attachment; if there were multiple gods, it would apply to every possible thing. Their convergence leads to its non-existence, because either it would be brought into existence by both together, which would entail the obtaining of the obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil); or it would be brought into existence by one of them, which would entail the incapability of the other due to the obstacle (māthila) between them, so it could not be brought into existence by either power. If this is the case when they agree, then when they disagree it is even more so. Therefore multiplicity necessitates the non-bringing into existence of things both when they agree and when they disagree. But the existence of things is observed, so the observation of their existence indicates the absence of multiplicity, and that is the desired conclusion. [al-Saba'i, p. 317] And that is impossible, or else...
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage proves the attribute of waḥdāniyya (oneness) – that God is one without partner. The main text argues that if God had a peer (nazīr) in essence or attributes, it would necessitate incapability (ʿajz) in each, and everything incapable is originated, which is impossible for God. The footnote (5) provides the classical 'proof of mutual hindrance' (burhān al-tawārud) often used in kalam. The logic is: if there were two gods, they would either agree or disagree on creating a specific thing. If they agree, both powers would converge on one effect; either both create it together (which is impossible because it would be a duplication of the already existent), or one creates it while the other is incapable, proving its incapability. If they disagree, one wants to create and the other wants to prevent it, leading to conflict and non-existence of the thing. Since we observe the existence of things, the assumption of multiple gods is false. Therefore, God is necessarily one. The phrase 'each incapable is originated' establishes that incapability entails temporality, which cannot apply to the eternal God.

برهان التوارد
تحصيل الحاصل
ماثلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the proof only works if the gods disagree.
Correction: The proof covers both agreement and disagreement, showing that even agreement leads to impossibility.

Checkpoint
In the proof of mutual hindrance, what does 'taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil' refer to?
A) The creation of something from nothing
B) The duplication of an already existing thing
C) The cooperation of two powers
D) The prevention of an effect

Key Takeaway
The proof of mutual hindrance demonstrates that multiple gods would lead to the non-existence of all things, contradicting the observed existence, thereby proving God's oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that multiple gods would lead to chaos and non-existence, how would that affect your trust in the order of the universe? Do you live as if the universe is sustained by a single God, or do you sometimes act as if chance or multiple forces control your life? Commit to recognizing God's sole power in every moment today.
?
The proof shows that agreement between two gods would lead to impossibility. In your own life, when you try to serve multiple masters or pursue conflicting goals, do you experience a similar 'mutual hindrance'? What is one area where you need to submit solely to Allah's will to find coherence?
?
The proof relies on the observable existence of things. Do you ever take the existence of things for granted? How can you use this observation to strengthen your conviction in tawḥīd? Make a habit of reflecting on the order of creation as a direct sign of God's oneness.

Divine Attributes
beginner

Proof of Divine Self-Subsistence
Dardeer proves God's necessity of self-subsistence using an exceptive syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof for God's self-subsistence (qiyām bi al-nafs) using an exceptive syllogism, and why the possibilities of God being an accident or attribute are impossible.

Core Idea
God must be self-subsistent because He cannot be an accident or attribute.

This lesson presents a concise logical proof from Imam Dardeer that establishes God's self-subsistence. By examining what would follow if God were not self-subsistent, the argument shows that only self-subsistence is possible. The proof uses an exceptive syllogism, a common tool in Islamic theology, to rule out alternatives. Understanding this argument clarifies why Muslims affirm that God does not depend on anything for His existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the English translation. Let the logical flow come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if He subsisted by another, He would be an accident or an attribute.'
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage presents two things God would be if He were not self-subsistent: an accident (ʿaraḍ) or an attribute (ṣifa). Understand why each is impossible for God.
An accident is perishable; an attribute cannot receive another attribute.
3
Follow the exceptive syllogism
The commentary explains the logical structure: if God were an attribute, He could not have attributes of meanings. Since He does have them, He is not an attribute. Therefore He is a self (dhāt) and self-subsistent.
This is a qiyās istithnāʾī—a conditional syllogism that eliminates the antecedent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القيام بالنفس (۱) فلأنه لو قامَ بغيره لكانَ عرَضًا، وقد تقدَّمَ بيانُ - الأعراض، أو كان صفةً قديمة قائمة بموصوفها (٣) ، فيلزَمُ أنْ لا يتصف بصفات المعاني؛ لما مَرَّ (٣)، وهو باطل. (۱) وأما القيام بالنفس إلخ. تقرير الدليل : المولى يجب له القيام بالنفس، لأنه لو لم يقم بنفسه لكان عرضًا أو صفة، وإذا كان كذلك احتاج إلى المحل أو إلى المخصص، لكن كونه عرضًا أو صفة محال فبطل ملزومه وهو «لم يقم بنفسه إلخ » ، فثبت نقيضه وهو وجوب القيام بنفسه وهو المطلوب. (۲) وهو إشارة إلى قياس استثنائي؛ وتقريره : المولى ليس بصفة، لأنه لو كان صفة لزم أن لا يتصف بصفات المعاني، واللازم وهو أن لا يتصف .. باطل، وإذا بطل اللازم بطل الملزوم وهو كونه صفة، وإذا بطل الملزوم ثبت نقيضه وهو كونه ليس بصفة ، وإذا انتفى كونه صفة ثبت أنه ذات وهو المطلوب. [حاشية السباعي، ص ٩٤] (۳) «لما مر » أي من أن الصفة لا تقبل صفة أخرى. [سباعي، ص ٣١٥]
As for self-subsistence (al-qiyām bi al-nafs), because if He subsisted by another, He would be an accident, and the explanation of accidents has preceded, or He would be an eternal attribute subsisting in its described, and then it would necessarily follow that He is not described with attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī), due to what has passed, and that is false. (1) 'As for self-subsistence, etc.' Explanation of the proof: The Master (al-mawlā) must have self-subsistence, because if He were not self-subsistent, He would be an accident or an attribute, and if so, He would need a substrate or a specificator, but His being an accident or attribute is impossible, so its entailer—namely 'He is not self-subsistent, etc.'—is null, thus its opposite is established, which is the obligation of self-subsistence, and that is the required. (2) It is an indication of an exceptive syllogism (qiyās istithnāʾī); its formulation: The Master is not an attribute, because if He were an attribute, it would necessarily follow that He is not described with attributes of meanings, and the consequent—that He is not described—is false, and if the consequent is null, its antecedent—His being an attribute—is null, and if the antecedent is null, its opposite—that He is not an attribute—is established, and if His being an attribute is negated, it is established that He is a self (dhāt), and that is the required. [Ḥāshiyat al-Subāʿī, p.94]. (3) 'Due to what has passed' meaning that an attribute does not admit another attribute. [Sabāʿī, p.315]
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the necessity of divine self-subsistence (qiyām bi al-nafs). It begins with a conditional argument: if God were not self-subsistent, He would either be an accident (ʿaraḍ) or an attribute (ṣifa). Both possibilities are dismissed as impossible for God. The marginal notes expand on the proof: the author presents an exceptive syllogism (qiyās istithnāʾī). The syllogism proceeds as follows: If God were an attribute, He could not be described with attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī), because an attribute cannot receive another attribute. But God is described with such attributes, so He is not an attribute. Therefore, He is a self (dhāt) and must be self-subsistent. The note also references a previous point that an attribute does not admit another attribute.

Key Terms

عرض
صفة
ذات
قياس استثنائي
صفات المعاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes God is an attribute and then tries to prove He is self-subsistent.
Correction: The proof does not assume God is an attribute; it argues from the impossibility of Him being an attribute to establish that He is a self-subsistent essence.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, if God were not self-subsistent, what two possibilities are considered?
A) He would be an accident or an attribute.
B) He would be a body or a substance.
C) He would be a place or time.
D) He would be contingent or impossible.

Key Takeaway
God's self-subsistence is logically necessary because the alternatives of being an accident or an attribute are impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God’s self-subsistence means He depends on nothing, does your life reflect a deep trust in His absolute sufficiency—or do you secretly believe your happiness depends on things He does not need?
?
The proof shows God cannot be an attribute. Do you ever reduce God to a mere concept or attribute in your mind, forgetting that He is a self-subsistent essence who is beyond your categories?
?
You have just seen a logical proof that God must be self-subsistent. How will this truth change the way you pray tonight—not as if you are informing God of your needs, but as one who knows He is completely independent and yet cares for you?

Names Derived from Negative Attributes: al-Jalil, al-Jamil, etc.
Dardeer explains seven divine names derived from negative attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of seven divine names that derive from negative attributes and how they combine transcendence (tanzih) with positive attributes of perfection.

Core Idea
Divine names like al-Jalil and al-Quddus reflect both transcendence and perfection.

In this passage, Imam Dardeer continues his discussion of negative attributes by explaining several divine names that derive from them. Each name highlights a different aspect of Allah's transcendence, yet also implies positive perfections. As you read, pay attention to how the author ties each name back to the idea of being free from imperfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation side by side. This will help you get a sense of the flow and the technical terms used.
Notice the poetic line at the beginning that lists the names.
2
Identify the negative attribute basis for each name
For each name, ask: what negative attribute (like being free from defect) is being emphasized? For example, al-Tahir means 'the Pure' – free from all imperfection.
3
Analyze the explanations of al-Jalil and al-Jamil
These two names receive a more detailed treatment. Note how al-Jalil involves both negative and positive attributes, and al-Jamil includes a list of perfections like knowledge and life.
The author adds a spiritual observation about the effects of majesty and beauty on the gnostics.
4
Understand the spiritual effects mentioned
The passage says that due to His majesty, the knowers are humble in awe, and due to His beauty, they are enraptured in love. Reflect on how these names lead to distinct spiritual states.
5
Memorize the seven names and their core meanings
Use the guided breakdown below to memorize each name and its key attribute. This will help you internalize these important divine names.
Consider making flashcards for each name.

القسم الأول: الإلهيات
-٣- فهو الجليل والجميل والوَلِي * والطاهرُ القُدوسُ والربُّ العَلي
ثُمَّ فَرَّعَ على ما ذكره مِنْ صِفاتِ السُّلوبِ، بعض أسماء وتنزيهاتٍ، فقال: (فهو) سُبحانه وتعالى (الجليل) أي: العظيم الشأن، الذي يخضَعُ الجلاله كُلَّ عظيم، ويُستَحْقَرُ بالنسبة لعظمتِه كُلَّ فخيم، والأظهرُ أَنَّ الجلال يرجع للصَّفاتِ السلبيّة والكماليّة معًا، لا لإحداهما فقط، كما قيلَ بكُلِّ.

ولجلاله ترى العارفين به تعالى مِنْ هَيبته خاشعين، ولجماله تراهُم مِنْ حُبِّه موهين.
(والوَلي) أي: مالكُ الخلائقِ ومُتَولّي أمورهم.
(والطاهر) أي: المُنزَّهُ عنْ كُلِّ ما لا يليقُ به.
(القدوس) مِنَ القُدْسِ وهو الطُّهْرُ، أي: العظيم التنْزيهِ عَنْ كُلِّ نَقْصٍ .
(والرّبُّ) أي: المالِكُ ومُرَبّي الخلائقِ.
(العلي) أي: المرتفعُ القَدْرِ المُبَرأُ مِنْ كُلِّ عَيْبٍ.

The first section: Divine Matters. -3- So He is al-Jalil, al-Jamil, and al-Wali, and al-Tahir, al-Quddus, and al-Rabb, al-Ali. Then he branched off from what he mentioned of negative attributes some names and acts of transcendence, and said: (So He) glorified and exalted (al-Jalil) i.e., the great in status, before whose majesty every great one submits, and every formidable thing is deemed small relative to His greatness. And the more apparent is that majesty refers to both negative and positive attributes, not just one of them, as has been said by all. (And al-Jamil) i.e., the one described with attributes of beauty and perfection, of knowledge, life, power, will, and others, and it is only completed by transcendence from every defect and deficiency that is not fitting for the most glorious and protected station. And that includes gentleness, forbearance, generosity, pardon, and other countless things, for they refer to the will or with power. And due to His majesty, you see the knowers of Him (exalted) humble from His awe, and due to His beauty, you see them enraptured by His love. (And al-Wali) i.e., the owner of creatures and the one who manages their affairs. (And al-Tahir) i.e., the one transcendent from everything that does not befit Him. (Al-Quddus) from al-quds which is purity, i.e., the great in transcendence from every deficiency. (And al-Rabb) i.e., the owner and nurturer of creatures. (Al-Ali) i.e., the elevated in rank, the one free from every defect. (1) i.e., to the will with power.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of several divine names that derive from the negative attributes previously discussed. The author begins by stating the section heading 'The first section: Divine Matters' and then quotes the poetic line: 'So He is al-Jalil, al-Jamil, al-Wali, al-Tahir, al-Quddus, al-Rabb, al-Ali.' Then the commentary explains each name in order. First, 'al-Jalil' means the great in status; before His majesty every great one submits. The author notes that majesty (jalal) pertains to both negative and positive attributes, not one exclusively. Second, 'al-Jamil' means the one described with attributes of beauty and perfection, such as knowledge, life, power, will, etc. These attributes are completed by transcendence from any defect. This includes traits like gentleness, forbearance, generosity, pardon—which ultimately refer to the will or to will combined with power. The author then adds a spiritual observation: due to His majesty, the gnostics are humble in awe; due to His beauty, they are enraptured in love. Third, 'al-Wali' means the owner and manager of all creatures. Fourth, 'al-Tahir' means the one transcendent from everything unbecoming. Fifth, 'al-Quddus' comes from 'quds' (purity), meaning the one greatly transcendent from all deficiency. Sixth, 'al-Rabb' means the owner and nurturer of creatures. Seventh, 'al-Ali' means the elevated in rank, free from all defect. A footnote clarifies that the traits like gentleness refer to the will combined with power.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that majesty (jalal) refers only to negative attributes or only to positive attributes.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the more apparent view is that majesty refers to both negative and positive attributes together.

Checkpoint

A) The elevated in rank

Key Takeaway
The seven divine names—al-Jalil, al-Jamil, al-Wali, al-Tahir, al-Quddus, al-Rabb, and al-Ali—each highlight a different aspect of Allah's transcendence while also implying positive perfections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite or hear the name al-Jalīl (The Majestic), do you feel genuine awe and humility before Him, or has your heart become hard and unmoved? What one change can you make today to cultivate khashyah (awe) in your worship?
?
The text states that due to His beauty (al-Jamīl), the gnostics are enraptured in love. Have you ever tasted that enrapturement? If not, what competing loves in your heart are blocking you from truly loving Allah for His perfect attributes?
?
Al-Quddūs means 'the One greatly transcendent from every deficiency.' In what areas of your life do you still ascribe deficiency to Allah—perhaps through complaining about His decree or doubting His wisdom? Repent and affirm His absolute perfection right now.

The Majestic and the Beautiful: Al-Jalil & Al-Jamil
Dardeer defines Al-Jalil and Al-Jamil and discusses the scope of majesty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of the divine names Al-Jalil (the Majestic) and Al-Jamil (the Beautiful) as explained by Imam Dardeer, including the scope of majesty and the effect of these attributes on the knowers.

Core Idea
Allah's majesty and beauty together inspire awe and love in the knower.

In this lesson, we explore two of the most profound divine names: Al-Jalil and Al-Jamil. Imam Dardeer explains what these names signify and how they relate to Allah's attributes, as well as how they affect those who know Him. The passage presents a theological nuance about majesty encompassing both negative and positive attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Al-Jalil and Al-Jamil
Read the Arabic passage focusing on these two names, along with the translation. Notice how Dardeer defines each name and the additional detail about majesty.
The passage includes a brief description of the effect on knowers at the end.
2
Understand the scope of majesty (al-jalal)

3
Reflect on the effect of these names

فهو الجليل والجميل والوَلِي * والطاهرُ القُدوسُ والربُّ العَلي
ثُمَّ فَرَّعَ على ما ذكره مِنْ صِفاتِ السُّلوبِ، بعض أسماء وتنزيهاتٍ، فقال: (فهو) سُبحانه وتعالى (الجليل) أي: العظيم الشأن، الذي يخضَعُ لجلاله كُلُّ عظيم، ويُستَحْقَرُ بالنسبة لعظمتِه كُلُّ فَخيم، والأظهرُ أَنَّ الجلال يرجع للصَّفاتِ السلبيّة والكماليّة معًا، لا لإحداهما فقط، كما قيلَ بكُلِّي.
(والجميل) أي: المتَّصِفُ بصفاتِ الجمال والكمال، مِنْ عِلْمٍ وحياةٍ وقُدرة وإرادة وغيرها، وإنما تتمُّ بالتنْزيهِ عنْ كُلِّ عَيب ونقص مما لا يليقُ بالجَنابِ الأعزّ الأحمى، ويندرج في ذلك اللطف والحِلْمُ والكَرَمُ والعَفُوُّ وغَيرُ ذلك مما لا يُحصَى، إِذْ هي ترجِعُ للإرادة أو معَ القُدرةِ.
ولجلاله ترى العارفين به تعالى مِنْ هَيبته خاشعين، ولجماله تراهُم مِنْ حُبِّه موهين.
(والوَلي) أي: مالكُ الخلائقِ ومُتَولّي أمورهم.
(والطاهر) أي: المُنزَّهُ عنْ كُلِّ ما لا يليقُ به.
(القدوس) مِنَ القُدْسِ وهو الطُّهْرُ، أي: العظيم التنْزيهِ عَنْ كُلِّ نَقْصٍ .
(والرّبُّ) أي: المالِكُ ومُرَبّي الخلائقِ.
(العلي) أي: المرتفعُ القَدْرِ المُبَرأُ مِنْ كُلِّ عَيْبٍ.
Then He, glorified and exalted, is Al-Jalil (the Majestic), i.e., the great of station, before whose majesty every great one is humbled, and every magnificent thing is considered insignificant in comparison to His greatness. And the more apparent view is that majesty (al-jalal) refers to both the negative and positive attributes together, not to only one of them, as some have said comprehensively. And Al-Jamil (the Beautiful), i.e., the one described with the attributes of beauty and perfection, such as knowledge, life, power, will, and others, and they are only perfected by transcendence from every defect and imperfection that does not befit the most august and inviolable presence. And included in that are gentleness, forbearance, generosity, pardon, and other countless things, as they return to will or will together with power. And by His majesty you see those who know Him, exalted, humbled out of awe of Him, and by His beauty you see them enraptured out of love for Him. And Al-Wali (the Patron), i.e., the owner of creatures and the manager of their affairs. And At-Tahir (the Pure), i.e., the one transcendent from everything that does not befit Him. And Al-Quddus (the Holy), from al-quds which is purity, i.e., the great in transcendence from every deficiency. And Ar-Rabb (the Lord), i.e., the Owner and the Nurturer of creatures. And Al-Ali (the High), i.e., the elevated in rank, cleared from every defect.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line of the poem listing divine names. It first defines Al-Jalil (the Majestic) as the one of great station before whom every great thing is humbled. The author then notes a theological nuance: majesty (al-jalal) encompasses both negative attributes (sifat salbiyya) and positive attributes (sifat kamaliyya) together, not just one set. Then Al-Jamil (the Beautiful) is defined as the one described with attributes of beauty and perfection like knowledge, life, power, and will. These are perfected only through transcendence (tanzih) from every defect. The author adds that many attributes like gentleness and forbearance fall under will or will together with power. He then describes the effect of these attributes on the knowers: they are humble before His majesty and enraptured by His beauty. The remaining names are defined concisely: Al-Wali (the Patron) as owner and manager of creatures; At-Tahir (the Pure) as completely transcendent; Al-Quddus (the Holy) as greatly transcendent from deficiency; Ar-Rabb (the Lord) as owner and nurturer; Al-Ali (the High) as elevated in rank and free from defect. The passage follows the order of the poem and explains each name's meaning and theological import.

Key Terms
الجليل
الجميل
صفات الجلال
صفات الجمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that majestic and beautiful attributes are separate categories, with majesty only referring to negative attributes.
Correction: The author explicitly states that majesty (al-jalal) refers to both negative and positive attributes together, not just one.

Checkpoint
According to Dardeer, what does the divine name 'Al-Jalil' (the Majestic) refer to?
A) Only the negative attributes of Allah.
B) Only the positive attributes of Allah.
C) Both negative and positive attributes together.
D) The attribute of beauty alone.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jalil and Al-Jamil together show that Allah's majesty and beauty inspire awe and love, with majesty encompassing all attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson says that by His majesty you see those who know Him humbled, and by His beauty you see them enraptured. Which of these two responses—awe or love—is more absent in your daily relationship with Allah? What concrete action will you take today to cultivate the missing one?
?
You have just learned that majesty encompasses both negative and positive attributes. Does your awe of Allah include His mercy and generosity, or do you only feel awe when thinking of His power and justice? How can you expand your understanding of His majesty to include His beauty?
?
The author mentions that attributes like gentleness, forbearance, generosity, and pardon are countless under beauty. If you truly believed that Allah's pardon is part of His beauty, would you still hold onto guilt after sincere repentance? What would change in your heart right now if you fully accepted that?

The Patron, Pure, Holy, Lord, High: Al-Wali, At-Tahir, Al-Quddus, Ar-Rabb, Al-Ali
Dardeer defines five divine names emphasizing sovereignty and purity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of five divine names that emphasize Allah's sovereignty, purity, and transcendence: Al-Wali, At-Tahir, Al-Quddus, Ar-Rabb, and Al-Ali.

Core Idea
Allah is the ultimate Patron, Pure, Holy, Lord, and High—each name underscores His unique majesty.

Having learned about majesty and beauty, we now turn to five more names from the poem. These names highlight Allah's absolute ownership, purity, and elevation above all imperfections. Imam Dardeer provides concise definitions for each, grounding them in the concept of transcendence (tanzih).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the remaining five names
Read the Arabic passage for Al-Wali, At-Tahir, Al-Quddus, Ar-Rabb, and Al-Ali, along with the translation. Observe the pattern in their definitions.
2
Note the common theme of transcendence
Notice that At-Tahir and Al-Quddus explicitly mean being free from defects, while Al-Ali implies being cleared from every defect. This reinforces tanzih.
3
Reflect on the role of these names
Consider how these names describe Allah's relationship with creation: He is the owner and nurturer (Ar-Rabb), the manager (Al-Wali), and the one transcendent above all.

Allah is Exalted Above Foolishness (Safah)
Dardeer defines safah and proves its impossibility for Allah from perfect creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of safah (foolishness) and why it is impossible for Allah based on His attributes of perfect management, awareness, and knowledge.

Core Idea
Safah is placing things in the wrong place; Allah’s perfect wisdom excludes all foolishness.

This lesson examines another attribute of Allah’s perfection: His transcendence above safah (foolishness). We will define this term, see how it is negated from Allah by citing His attributes, and reflect on a profound observation from the gnostics about the perfection of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the flow from definition to proof to testimony.
The passage is concise but rich in meaning.
2
Define safah and note its negation
Identify the definition of safah as 'placing a thing in other than its proper place.' Understand that Allah is above this because He is al-Mudīr, al-Khabīr, al-ʿAlīm.
Safah is not just ignorance; it is misplacement.
3
Connect the negation to Allah's attributes
Think about how the attributes of Manager, All-Aware, All-Knowing logically preclude any foolish action. Perfect knowledge and awareness ensure perfect placement.
This is a rational proof from attributes.
4
Reflect on the gnostic's statement
Consider the statement: 'There is not in possibility anything more wonderful than what has come to be.' Discuss what it reveals about the perfection of creation and why it supports the negation of safah.
This is an illustrative evidence from observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكيم. (و) منزهة أيضًا عن (السفه) وهو: وضع الشيء في غير محله، إذ هو المدير الخبير العليم، ولذا قال بعض أهل العرفان لما شاهد من عجيب الإتقان: ليس لي الإمكان أبدع مما كان.
Al-Ḥakīm (The Wise). And He is also transcendent above foolishness (safah), which is: placing a thing in other than its proper place, for He is the Manager, the All-Aware, the All-Knowing. Hence some of the people of spiritual insight, when they witnessed the wonder of perfect order, said: 'There is not in possibility anything more wonderful than what has come to be.'
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of Allah's transcendence above safah (foolishness). The author first defines safah as 'placing a thing in other than its proper place.' He then negates this from Allah by citing His attributes: He is al-Mudīr (the Manager), al-Khabīr (the All-Aware), and al-ʿAlīm (the All-Knowing). Since He perfectly manages and knows all things, He never acts foolishly. As evidence, the author quotes a statement from 'some of the people of spiritual insight' (understood to be Imam al-Ghazali) who, upon observing the exquisite perfection of creation, declared that nothing in the realm of possibility could be more excellent than what Allah has actually brought into existence. This statement underscores that Allah's creation is optimal, leaving no room for foolishness or imperfection. The passage thus combines a clear definition, a rational proof from Allah's attributes, and an illustrative testimony from the gnostics.

Key Terms
السفه
وضع الشيء في غير محله
المدير
الخبير
العليم
الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the gnostic's statement implies that Allah could not create a better world.
Correction: The statement actually affirms that what Allah created is perfectly suited to His wisdom; it does not limit His power but highlights the perfection of His actual creation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of safah?
A) Ignorance of future events.
B) Placing a thing in other than its proper place.
C) Lying and deception.
D) Inability to create.

Key Takeaway
Allah's perfect wisdom and knowledge preclude any foolishness in His actions; everything He does is perfectly placed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the areas of your life that feel chaotic or misplaced, do you truly believe that Allah, al-Mudīr, has arranged them perfectly? What specific thought patterns lead you to doubt His wisdom, and how can you actively replace them with trust?
?
Consider one decision you made recently that you now regret—did you act without full awareness or knowledge? How does knowing that Allah never misplaces anything challenge you to seek His guidance before you act?

Al-Ghazali's Statement and Its Interpretations
Context and four interpretive responses to al-Ghazali's saying

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning and context of al-Ghazali's controversial saying and the four main responses that preserve divine omnipotence.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali's statement is about God's wisdom, not incapability.

Imam al-Ghazali's saying 'There is not in possibility anything more beautiful than what has been' has puzzled scholars for centuries. This lesson unpacks the statement and the four key interpretations that show it does not diminish God's power.

Guided Path
1
Read the context on divine purity
Notice how the author links the statement to God's transcendence above folly (safah).
2
Learn the statement itself
Read the Arabic saying and its translation carefully.
3
Analyze the four responses
Each response explains the statement in a way that avoids implying limit on divine power.
4
Understand the two sides of possibility
Abu Talib al-Makki and al-Ghazali assume possibility on different sides.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) مُنرَّةٌ أيضًا عن (السَّفَه) وهو: وضْعُ الشَّيء في غَيرِ تَحَلَّه، إِذْ هو المدير الخبير العليم، ولذا قال بعضُ أهْلِ العِرفانِ لَما شَاهَدَ مِنْ عجِيبِ الإتقان: ليس لي الإمكان أبدَعُ مما كان (٢). (2) هذا هو قول حجة الإسلام الإمام الغزالي رضي الله عنه ، وقد استشكل أهل العلم هذا القول لإيهامه العجز وهو عليه تعالى محال، وأجابوا بأجوبة منها: 1 . أن المراد إمكان الخلائق، فالمعنى ليس في إمكان الخلائق تغيير ما أراده الله وأبدعه. .٢ ومنها أن المراد إمكانه تعالى باعتبار تعلق علمه أزلا بإيجاد هذا العالم على هذا النظام، وتعلق القدرة التنجيزي لا يكون إلا على وفق ما خصصته الإرادة وسبق به العلم، فالمعنى ليس في الإمكان شيء يقبل الزيادة أو النقص على خلاف ما سبق في العلم. .٣ قال الشيخ الأمير : هذا كلام مشوش عليه أو سرى له من الفلاسفة. ٤. أو أنه محمول على ما تسعه عقولنا كما قال أبو طالب المكي في كتاب التوكل: اعلم يقينا أن الله لو جعل الخلائق كلهم على علم أعلمهم به وعقل أعقلهم وحكمة أحكمهم .. ثم قيل لهم دبروا الملك بما أعطيتكم من العلوم والعقول لما زاد تدبيرهم على ما تراه من تدبيره تعالى جناح بعوضة ٩٦ .اهـ. فعدم الإمكان افترضه أبو طالب في جانب الخلائق والغزالي في جانب الحق سبحانه. وقد شنع على الإمام الغزالي جماعة قالوا: إن هذا فيه نسبة العجز لقدرة الإله، وذكر الشعراني في اليواقيت عن ابن عربي انه كلام في غاية التحقيق، لأنه ما ثم إلا رتبتان قدم وحدوث، فالمخلوق له رتبة الحدوث، فلو خلق الحق تعالى ما خلق ما خرج عن هذه الرتبة فلا يقال: هل يقدر الحق أن يخلق قديما مثله فإنه سؤال مهمل ،لاستحالته ولما سأل صاحب الإبريز شيخه عبد العزيز الدباغ عن مقالة الغزالي لم يصرح له بالإنكار ولا بالتأويل وإنما قال له: «القدرة لا تحصر والرب لا يعجزه شيء، هذا وقد اختلف العلماء في مقالة الغزالي على ثلاثة أقوال: طائفة ردتها، وطائفة أولتها، وطائفة كذبت نسبتها إليه كما سبق الإشارة إلى ذلك وصاحب الإبريز رد جميع التأويلات وقال في آخر كلامه قد حماني الله تعالى من أبي حامد بشيخنا، وذلك أني لما عزمت على رد هذه المسألة وقف الشيخ علي منامًا فملأ قلبي بتعظيم أبي حامد حتى صارت ردودي تتوجه إلى المسألة ولم ينله منها شيء ولم يجر على لساني إلا تعظيمه». اهـ. وأرى أن الحق مع الطائفة التي أولتها لا مع التي كذبتها ولا مع التي ردتها .
And (He is) also purified from (folly) which is: placing a thing in other than its proper place, since He is the Manager, the All-Aware, the All-Knowing. Therefore, some of the people of spiritual knowledge said, when they witnessed the wondrous precision: 'There is not in possibility anything more perfect than what has been' (2). (2) This is the saying of the Proof of Islam, Imam al-Ghazali, may Allah be pleased with him. The scholars have found this saying problematic because it suggests incapability, which is impossible for Him, Exalted is He. They answered with several answers, including: 1. That what is meant is the possibility of creatures, so the meaning is that it is not within the possibility of creatures to change what Allah has willed and created. 2. Another is that what is meant is His possibility, Exalted is He, considering the attachment of His knowledge from eternity to bringing this world into existence in this system, and the actual attachment of power is only in accordance with what the will has specified and what knowledge has preceded, so the meaning is that there is nothing in possibility that admits increase or decrease contrary to what has preceded in knowledge. 3. Al-Shaykh al-Amir said: This is confused speech or it came to him from the philosophers. 4. Or that it is interpreted according to what our intellects can encompass, as Abu Talib al-Makki said in the book al-Tawakkul: 'Know with certainty that if Allah were to make all creatures possess the knowledge with which He knows them, the intellect with which they are most intelligent, and the wisdom with which they are most wise... then they were told to manage the kingdom with what I have given you of sciences and intellects, their management would not add to what you see of His management, Exalted is He, even a gnat's wing.' 96. So the lack of possibility was assumed by Abu Talib on the side of creatures, and by al-Ghazali on the side of the Truth, Exalted is He. Some groups have attacked Imam al-Ghazali, saying that this attributes incapability to the power of Allah. Al-Sharani mentioned in al-Yawaqit from Ibn 'Arabi that it is speech of extreme verification, because there are only two ranks: preeternity and origination. The creature has the rank of origination, so if the Truth, Exalted is He, had created what He created, it would not depart from this rank, so one does not say: 'Can the Truth create something preeternal like Him?' because it is a meaningless question, due to its impossibility. When the author of al-Ibriz asked his shaykh 'Abd al-'Aziz al-Dabbagh about al-Ghazali's statement, he did not explicitly reject it nor interpret it, but rather said to him: 'Power is not limited, and the Lord is not incapacitated by anything. Indeed, the scholars have differed concerning al-Ghazali's statement in three opinions: a group rejected it, a group interpreted it, and a group declared its attribution to him to be false, as has been indicated previously. The author of al-Ibriz rejected all interpretations and said at the end of his speech: 'Allah has protected me from Abu Hamid through our shaykh, for when I resolved to refute this issue, the shaykh appeared to me in a dream and filled my heart with reverence for Abu Hamid, such that my refutations were directed to the issue itself and nothing of it touched him, nor did my tongue utter anything but veneration of him.' End. I see that the truth is with the group that interpreted it, not with those who declared it false nor with those who rejected it.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage addresses the controversial statement attributed to Imam al-Ghazali: 'There is not in possibility anything more perfect than what has been.' The author first places this in the context of discussing divine transcendence above folly (safah). Al-Ghazali's statement seemed to imply that God could not create a more perfect world, which suggests incapability. The text then presents several responses: (1) The statement refers to the possibility of creatures, meaning they cannot alter God's creation. (2) It refers to God's own possibility in light of His eternal knowledge and will—since God willed this specific world, no other world could have been created. (3) Some dismiss it as confused speech influenced by philosophy. (4) Others restrict it to what our minds can comprehend, citing Abu Talib al-Makki's idea that even the wisest creatures cannot improve upon God's creation. The passage then notes the three scholarly positions: rejection, interpretation, or denial of authenticity. The author (the commentator, likely al-Sawi) sides with those who interpret it. The story of al-Dabbagh's dream shows a reverence for al-Ghazali that prevents direct criticism. The discussion illustrates the complexity of theological discourse and the importance of preserving the integrity of great scholars while addressing problematic statements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What would it take for you to surrender that doubt and affirm, as the scholars did, that His knowledge and power are perfect?
?
The scholars provided interpretations to protect the understanding of God's attributes. In your own learning, have you ever clung to a literal reading of a text or a scholar's saying without seeking its proper interpretation? Are you willing to revisit your beliefs and ensure they align with the consensus of Ahl al-Sunnah? Take a moment to identify one area where you need to re-evaluate your understanding.

Scholarly Opinions on al-Ghazali's Statement
Three scholarly opinions and author's conclusion on al-Ghazali's statement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly positions on al-Ghazali's saying and why the author sides with the interpreters.

Core Idea
The disagreement shows how scholars protect both divine power and scholarly legacy.

After presenting the four responses, the passage turns to the broader scholarly debate. Some rejected al-Ghazali's statement outright, others reinterpreted it, and some denied he ever said it. This lesson explores these positions and the author's resolution.

Guided Path

2
Learn the three scholarly opinions
Identify the three groups: rejecters, interpreters, and those who deny attribution.
3

The author of al-Ibriz's dream shows reverence for al-Ghazali while still critiquing the statement.
4

The author explicitly sides with the interpreters.

And (He is) also purified from (folly) which is: placing a thing in other than its proper place, since He is the Manager, the All-Aware, the All-Knowing. Therefore, some of the people of spiritual knowledge said, when they witnessed the wondrous precision: 'There is not in possibility anything more perfect than what has been' (2). (2) This is the saying of the Proof of Islam, Imam al-Ghazali, may Allah be pleased with him. The scholars have found this saying problematic because it suggests incapability, which is impossible for Him, Exalted is He. They answered with several answers, including: 1. That what is meant is the possibility of creatures, so the meaning is that it is not within the possibility of creatures to change what Allah has willed and created. 2. Another is that what is meant is His possibility, Exalted is He, considering the attachment of His knowledge from eternity to bringing this world into existence in this system, and the actual attachment of power is only in accordance with what the will has specified and what knowledge has preceded, so the meaning is that there is nothing in possibility that admits increase or decrease contrary to what has preceded in knowledge. 3. Al-Shaykh al-Amir said: This is confused speech or it came to him from the philosophers. 4. Or that it is interpreted according to what our intellects can encompass, as Abu Talib al-Makki said in the book al-Tawakkul: 'Know with certainty that if Allah were to make all creatures possess the knowledge with which He knows them, the intellect with which they are most intelligent, and the wisdom with which they are most wise... then they were told to manage the kingdom with what I have given you of sciences and intellects, their management would not add to what you see of His management, Exalted is He, even a gnat's wing.' 96. So the lack of possibility was assumed by Abu Talib on the side of creatures, and by al-Ghazali on the side of the Truth, Exalted is He. Some groups have attacked Imam al-Ghazali, saying that this attributes incapability to the power of Allah. Al-Sharani mentioned in al-Yawaqit from Ibn 'Arabi that it is speech of extreme verification, because there are only two ranks: preeternity and origination. End. I see that the truth is with the group that interpreted it, not with those who declared it false nor with those who rejected it.

Divine Attributes

Classification of Divine Attributes into Existence, Negative, and Psychic
Dardeer classifies divine attributes into existence, negative, and psychic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold classification of divine attributes in Ash'ari theology and the logical ordering of purification before adornment.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are classified by existence, absence, and relation to essence.

Now that the negative attributes have been established, Dardeer turns to the positive attributes of God. He introduces the famous distinction between attributes of meanings, negative attributes, and psychic attributes. This classification is essential to understanding the Ash'ari framework for talking about God's attributes without compromising His uniqueness.

Guided Path
1
Understand the order of presentation
Notice that the author places the discussion of negative attributes first, then attributes of meanings. He explains this order as purification (takhliya) before adornment (tahliya).
This reflects the principle that removing imperfections precedes affirming perfections.
2
Define attributes of meanings
Attributes of meanings are existence attributes (wujudiyya)—those that have real existence in themselves, whether eternal or created. Examples include God's knowledge and power, as well as human knowledge and power.
3
Distinguish psychic from meaningful attributes
Psychic attributes (nafsiyya) are necessary for the essence without a cause (e.g., existence for God). Meaningful attributes (ma'nawiyya) are caused by a cause and are relative to the meanings (e.g., being knowing due to knowledge).
This division refines the category of non-existence, non-negative attributes.
4
Apply the classification to created examples
The author includes examples from creation (our knowledge, power, whiteness) to show that 'attributes of meanings' is a broad term applicable to any existence attribute, not exclusively divine.
This prevents the misconception that the term only applies to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الأول: الإلهيات
ولما فرغ مِنَ الكلام على الصفات السلبيّةِ شَرَعَ في بيان صفاتِ المعاني، وقدَّمها لأنها من باب التخلية، والمعاني مِنْ بابِ التحلية، وشأن التخليةِ أنْ تَقَدَّمَ على التحلية
فقال:
ثُمَّ المعاني سبعةُ للرائي * أيْ: عِلْمُهُ المحيط بالأشياء
(ثُمَّ المعاني) أَيْ: ثُمَّ بِعْدَ أَنْ عَرَفتَ ما تقدَّمَ مِنَ النَّفْسِيِّةِ والسلبية، فيجِبُ عليك معرفةُ الصِّفاتِ المُسماة بالمعاني؛ لأنَّ كُلَّ واحِدةٍ منها معنى قائم بذاتِه تعالى.
بيان
ومُرادهم بصفاتِ المعاني الصِّفاتُ الوُجوديّةُ، أي: التي لها وُجودٌ في نفسها، صفات قديمةً كانتْ أو حادثةً، كعلمه وقدرته تعالى، وكعِلْمِنا وقدرتنا، والبياض والسَّوادِ.
والحاصل : أَنَّ الصفات إنْ كانتْ وُجوديّةٌ سُمّيتْ صِفاتِ معان .. وإن لم تكن وجوديّةً ، فإنْ كان مدلولها عدَمَ أمر لا يليقُ سُمّيت سلبية، وإن لم يكُنْ مدلولها عدَما، فإن كانتْ واجبةً للذاتِ - ما دامتِ الذاتُ غَيرَ - سُمّيت صفةً نفسيّةً وحالا نفْسيّةً، كالوُجودِ وكالتحيز للجرْمِ وقبوله للأغراض . وإن كانت معللةً بعلة بأن كانتْ واجبةً للذات ما دامت علتُها سُمّيت معنوية كالعالمية والقادرية، أي: كون الذات المنصفةِ بالعِلْمِ عالمة، وكَونِ الذات النوارية بالقدرة قادرة، نسبة إلى المعاني، وهي (سبعة للرائي) أي: الناظر المتأمل.
The First Section: Divine Matters. And when he finished speaking on the negative attributes, he began explaining the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī), and he presented them because they are in the chapter of purification, and the meanings are in the chapter of adornment, and the way of purification is that it precedes adornment. He said: Then the meanings are seven for the observer: that is, His knowledge encompassing things. (Then the meanings) i.e., then after you have known what preceded of the psychic (al-nafsiyya) and negative (al-salbiyya), it is obligatory upon you to know the attributes called meanings (al-maʿānī), because each one of them is a meaning subsisting by His Essence, exalted is He. Explanation: And their intent by 'attributes of meanings' is the existence attributes (al-ṣifāt al-wujūdiyya), i.e., those that have existence in themselves, attributes whether eternal or originated, like His knowledge and power, and like our knowledge and power, and whiteness and blackness. And the conclusion is: that attributes, if they are existence attributes, they are called attributes of meanings. And if they are not existence attributes, then if their indication is the absence of an unfitting matter, they are called negative. And if their indication is not absence, then if they are necessary for the essence as long as the essence is other, they are called a psychic attribute and a psychic state, like existence and like the body's occupying space and its receiving accidents. And if they are caused by a cause, in that they are necessary for the essence as long as their cause exists, they are called meaningful (maʿnawiyya), like being knowing (al-ʿālimiyya) and being powerful (al-qādariyya), i.e., the essence described with knowledge being knowing, and the essence described with power being powerful, in relation to the meanings, and they are (seven for the observer), i.e., the contemplative observer.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a classification of divine attributes. The author begins by stating that after finishing the discussion of negative attributes, he now turns to the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī). He orders them this way because the removal of imperfections (takhliya) comes before adornment (taḥliya). Then he quotes the poetic line: 'Then the meanings are seven for the observer: that is, His knowledge encompassing things.' He explains that after understanding the psychic and negative attributes, one must know the attributes called meanings (maʿānī), because each is a meaning subsisting in God's essence. He then gives a 'bayān' (clarification): by attributes of meanings they mean existence attributes (wujūdiyya), i.e., attributes that have existence in themselves, whether eternal or created, citing examples like God's knowledge and power, and also our knowledge and power, and whiteness and blackness. This shows that the term 'attribute of meaning' applies broadly to any existence attribute. He then gives the 'conclusion' (al-ḥāṣil): attributes are divided into three categories. First, if they are existence attributes (wujūdiyya), they are called attributes of meanings (ṣifāt maʿānī). Second, if they are not existence attributes, and their indication is the absence of something unfitting, they are called negative attributes (ṣifāt salbiyya). Third, if they are not existence attributes and their indication is not absence, then: (a) if they are necessary for the essence as long as the essence exists (without a cause), they are called psychic attributes (ṣifa nafsiyya) or psychic states, like existence for God or like a body's occupying space and receiving accidents; (b) if they are caused by a cause (muʿallala bi ʿilla), in that they are necessary for the essence as long as the cause exists, they are called meaningful attributes (ṣifāt maʿnawiyya), like being knowing (derived from knowledge) and being powerful (derived from power). These meaningful attributes are relative to the meanings (the existence attributes). Finally, he reiterates that the meanings (attributes of meanings) are seven for the contemplative observer. This classification is foundational for understanding the Ashʿarī framework of attributes.

Key Terms
صفات المعاني
الصفات الوجودية
الصفات السلبية
الصفات النفسية
الصفات المعنوية
التخلية
التحلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attributes of meanings only apply to God and not to creatures.
Correction: The author explicitly includes created examples (our knowledge, power, whiteness) to show that 'attributes of meanings' refers to any existence attribute, whether divine or created.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what distinguishes a psychic attribute (ṣifa nafsiyya) from a meaningful attribute (ṣifa maʿnawiyya)?
A) Psychic attributes are existence attributes, while meaningful attributes are not.
B) Psychic attributes are necessary for the essence without a cause, while meaningful attributes are caused by a cause.
C) Psychic attributes are negative, while meaningful attributes are positive.
D) Psychic attributes are only for God, while meaningful attributes are for creatures.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari classification of divine attributes uses existence, negation, and psychic (necessary/caused) to systematically describe God's perfections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you approach God's attributes as mere intellectual categories, or do you let them transform how you live? For example, knowing that God's knowledge is an existence attribute, do you live with the awareness that He sees everything? What is one action you will change this week to reflect that awareness?
?
The author says purification (removing negatives) comes before adornment (acquiring positives). What is one negative trait—like arrogance or laziness—that you need to remove before you can truly embody a positive attribute like knowledge? Write down one concrete step you will take today to begin that purification.
?
If you truly believed that every existence attribute of God has an effect on your life—His power enables you, His knowledge guides you—would you rely on Him more? What is one area of your life where you are still depending on yourself rather than on His attributes? Commit to turning that area over to Him this week.

Defining Divine Knowledge: Eternal, All-Encompassing, Infallible
Dardeer defines the attribute of divine knowledge as eternal and all-encompassing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of divine knowledge as an eternal attribute that discloses all things (existents and non-existents) with certainty, without any possibility of error.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge is eternal, covers everything, and never errs.

In this passage, Imam Dardeer unpacks the definition of divine knowledge. He clarifies that God's knowledge is not limited to what exists; it encompasses even non-existent possibilities and impossibilities. This establishes the absolute comprehensiveness and infallibility of divine knowledge, distinguishing it from human knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the author's meaning.
Notice the precision in the wording.
2
Note the scope of 'things'
The author clarifies that 'things' includes all three categories: necessary, possible, and impossible. This is broader than the common usage.
3
Identify the core definition
Find the sentence that defines divine knowledge: an eternal attribute that discloses existents and non-existents as they truly are.
Pay attention to 'disclosure that does not admit the opposite'.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why including non-existents and the impossibility of error matters for understanding God's knowledge.
This distinguishes divine knowledge from human opinion and probability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ فَسَّرَها بقوله: (أي: عِلْمُه) وما عُطِفَ عليه (المحيط بالأشياء) كُلها. واجبها وجائزها ومستحيلها، فليسَ مُراده بالأَشْياءِ الموجودات فقط كما هو المتعارفُ عِندَهُم. وهو: صفةٌ أزليّةً تنكشِفُ بها الموجودات والمعدوماتُ على ما هي عليه انكشافا لا يحتَمِلُ النقيض بوَجهِ
Then he interpreted it by his saying: (that is, His knowledge) and what is conjoined to it (encompassing things) all of them: their necessary, possible, and impossible. So his intent by 'things' is not only existents as is customary among them. And it is: an eternal attribute by which existents and non-existents are disclosed as they are, a disclosure that does not admit the opposite in any way.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of divine knowledge. The author is commenting on the phrase 'that is, His knowledge encompassing things' from the poetic line. He explains that 'things' here includes all things: the necessary (wājib), the possible (jāʾiz), and the impossible (mustaḥīl). He clarifies that 'things' is not limited to existents (mawjūdāt) as is common in ordinary usage. Then he gives a precise definition: divine knowledge is an eternal attribute (ṣifa azaliyya) by which existents and non-existents are disclosed as they truly are, with a disclosure that does not admit the opposite in any way. This means it is infallible and certain, unlike opinion (ẓann) or assumption (iʿtiqād). The inclusion of non-existents (maʿdūmāt) emphasizes that God's knowledge covers everything, even what does not exist, in its true state. The term 'disclosure' (inkishāf) is carefully chosen but later commentators note it might imply change; however, here it is understood as timeless manifestation.

Key Terms
العلم
صفة أزلية
الموجودات
المعدومات
انكشاف
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine knowledge only pertains to existing things.
Correction: The passage explicitly includes non-existents (maʿdūmāt) and the categories of impossible and possible, showing that God's knowledge encompasses everything in its true state, whether existent or not.

Checkpoint
What does the author exclude from the meaning of 'things' (al-ashyāʾ) in this definition?
A) Necessary things
B) Possible things
C) Only existents
D) Impossible things

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge is an eternal attribute that infallibly discloses all things, including non-existents, as they truly are.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah’s knowledge encompasses even your most hidden intentions and future possibilities, what does that mean for the excuses you make for your sins? Will you now confess them to Him with full honesty?
?
The passage says divine knowledge covers ‘impossible’ things. How does the reality that Allah knows everything that cannot happen affect your trust in His decree when you face what seems impossible?
?
Knowledge that is infallible and eternal means Allah can never be surprised by your choices. Since He already knows every moment of your life, are you living with the awareness that nothing is hidden? Commit to one area where you will start living more openly before Him.
