B) The possible with one of its permissible aspects like existence, time, place, etc.
C) Only the actions of humans
D) The essence of God

Key Takeaway
The divine Will is the attribute that makes the specific features of everything in existence possible, and without it, creation would lack order and perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage argues that without Will, God would have 'lack of intention.' Where in your daily life do you act without intention, and how does that contrast with the deliberate will of your Creator? Are you comfortable with that contrast?
?
If every detail of your existence—your birthplace, family, abilities—was specified by Allah’s Will, why do you sometimes attribute your successes to your own effort and your failures to luck? What would it take for you to truly internalize that all is from His Will?
?
The world's order is presented as proof of Will. Look around you right now: can you name one specific aspect of creation that you previously took for granted but now see as requiring an intentional choice of its details? How does this change your gratitude or submission?

The Mu'tazila Dispute on the Scope of Divine Will
Dardeer refutes the Mu'tazilite claim and affirms all things occur by Allah's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that everything that occurs is willed by Allah, and how the Mu'tazila objection is refuted by distinguishing between divine will, command, and pleasure.

Core Idea
Allah wills everything, but He does not command or approve of evil.

In this passage, Imam al-Dardeer addresses a key dispute with the Mu'tazila regarding the scope of divine will. He begins by affirming the Ash'ari principle that nothing occurs in God's dominion except what He wills. The Mu'tazila object, claiming that God cannot will evil. The author refutes this by distinguishing between will (irāda) and command (amr), and illustrates the debate with a historical anecdote.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the three parts: principle, objection, refutation.
2
Identify the Ash'ari principle
Locate the statement 'And every thing that occurs... He willed it' and understand that it establishes that nothing happens except by Allah's will.
This is the foundational claim being defended.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila objection
Find where the Mu'tazila claim that God does not will evil because willing evil is ugly, and that will follows command.
This objection is based on a moral analogy.
4
Grasp the refutation
The author says 'Intent (qaṣd) is different from command (amr)' to show that God may will something without commanding it or being pleased with it.
This is the key distinction that refutes the Mu'tazila.
5
Reflect on the anecdote
Read the story of al-Qāḍī ʿAbd al-Jabbār and al-Ustādh al-Isfarāyīnī to see the debate in action.
The Ustādh's reply 'Is our Lord disobeyed by force?' highlights that disobedience occurs by Allah's will but not by coercion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ ذَكَرَ مسألة تتعلَّق بالإرادة، وقَعَ فِيهَا النَّزاعُ بينَنا وَبَينَ الْمُعْتَزِلِةِ بِقَولِهِ: (وكُلُّ كائن) أي: موجودٍ مِنَ الجواهِرِ والأعْراضِ، وهذا مُبتَدَأ، وجُملةً قوله (أرادَه) شَيْءٍ - أي أرادَ وُجودَه - خَبَرُه. فلا يَقَعُ في مُلْكِه تعالى إلا ما يُريدُ)، وهذا إذا كان الكائن. (3) المعتزلة قالوا: إنه تعالى لا يريد المعاصى والشرور والآثام ونحو ذلك لأن إرادة القبيح قبيحة وجعلوا الإرادة تابعة للأمر فلا يأمر تعالى إلا بما يريد، وهو قول باطل رد عليهم الشيخ بقوله: فالقصد غير الأمر إشارة إلى القول الحق أن كل ما أراده تعالى فهو كائن، وكل كائن فهو مراد له تعالى وإن لم يكن مرضيا له تعالى ولا مأمورًا به، وهذا ما اشتهر عن السلف: ما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ أن يكن. (٤) هذا إشارة إلى واقعة حصلت مع القاضي عبد الجبار بن أحمد المعتزلي أنه دخل على الصاحب ابن عباد وعنده الأستاذ أبو أسحق الإسفراييني، فقال عبد الجبار سبحان من تنزه عن الفحشاء - يريد تنزيه الحق تعالى عن إرادة الفحشاء - فقال الأستاذ: سبحان من لا يقع في ملكه إلا ما يشاء، فقال عبد الجبار : أفيريد ربنا أن يُعصى ؟ فقال الأستاذ : أفيعصى ربنا قهرًا، فقال عبد الجبار : أرأيت أساء، وإن منعك ما هو له فهو يختص برحمته من يشاء. اهـ.
Then he mentioned a matter pertaining to Will, concerning which there is disagreement between us and the Mu'tazila by his saying: (And every thing that occurs) i.e., anything existent, whether substances or accidents, this is the subject, and the sentence (He willed it) i.e., He willed its existence, is the predicate. So nothing occurs in His dominion except what He wills. (3) The Mu'tazila said: He, Exalted, does not will disobedience, evils, sins, and the like, because willing the ugly is ugly, and they made will dependent on command; so He does not command except what He wills. This is a false statement refuted by the Shaykh by his saying: 'Intent (al-qaṣd) is different from command (al-amr)' indicating the true doctrine that whatever Allah wills comes to be, and whatever comes to be is willed by Him, even if He is not pleased with it nor commands it. This is what is famous from the Salaf: What Allah wills is, and what He does not will is not. (4) This refers to an incident that occurred with al-Qāḍī ʿAbd al-Jabbār ibn Aḥmad al-Muʿtazilī: he entered upon al-Ṣāḥib ibn ʿAbbād, and with him was al-Ustādh Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāyīnī. ʿAbd al-Jabbār said: 'Glory be to the One who is free from obscenity' meaning to exonerate the Truth from willing obscenity. The Ustādh said: 'Glory be to the One from whose dominion nothing occurs except what He wills.' ʿAbd al-Jabbār said: 'Does our Lord will to be disobeyed?' The Ustādh said: 'Is our Lord disobeyed by force?' ʿAbd al-Jabbār said: 'Do you see? If He prevents you from guidance and decrees upon you ruin, has He done good to you or evil?' Then he recited: 'He dedicates His mercy to whom He wills.' End.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents the dispute between the Ashʿarīs and the Muʿtazila regarding the scope of divine will. The author first quotes the poem line 'And every thing that occurs' meaning every existent substance or accident, and explains that the phrase 'He willed it' is the predicate, so nothing happens in God's dominion except what He wills. The Muʿtazila object, saying God does not will evil or sin because willing evil is evil, and they argue that will follows command—so God only wills what He commands. The author refutes this by stating that intent (qaṣd) is different from command (amr): God may will something without commanding it or being pleased with it. The true doctrine is that whatever Allah wills must occur, and whatever occurs is willed by Allah, even if it is not pleasing or commanded. This is summed up in the statement of the Salaf: 'What Allah wills is, and what He does not will is not.' Then an anecdote illustrates the debate: al-Qāḍī ʿAbd al-Jabbār al-Muʿtazilī tried to argue that God does not will evil, but the Shaykh countered that nothing occurs in His dominion except what He wills. When ʿAbd al-Jabbār asked if God wills to be disobeyed, the Shaykh replied, 'Is our Lord disobeyed by force?' implying that disobedience occurs by God's will, but not by coercion. The anecdote concludes with ʿAbd al-Jabbār's rhetorical question and the Qur'anic verse about mercy.

Key Terms
الإرادة (al-irāda)
المعتزلة (al-Muʿtazila)
القصد (al-qaṣd)
الأمر (al-amr)
ما شاء الله كان (mā shāʾa Llāhu kāna)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God wills sin, then He must approve of it or command it.
Correction: The passage clarifies that divine will (irāda) is distinct from pleasure (riḍā) and command (amr); Allah wills all that occurs, but He only commands good and is pleased with obedience.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction the author makes to refute the Mu'tazila regarding divine will?
A) Will is the same as command
B) Intent (qaṣd) is different from command (amr)
C) God does not will any evil
D) Will is only for good actions

Key Takeaway
Allah wills all that occurs, but His will does not imply His approval or command; what He wills may be different from what He commands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of the evil in the world, do you find it difficult to accept that Allah wills it? How does knowing that His will is distinct from His command help you trust His wisdom?
?
This lesson shows that Allah wills things that He does not command. Is there an area in your life where you are confusing Allah's permission with His approval? Are you assuming that because something happens, it is what Allah wants you to do? How will you change your perspective?
?
The anecdote ends with 'He dedicates His mercy to whom He wills.' In light of this lesson, how does this verse deepen your understanding of Allah's sovereignty and your own dependence on His mercy? What concrete step can you take today to rely on His mercy rather than your own deeds?

Divine Will: An Eternal Attribute of Specification
Definition of divine will and proof of its necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the divine attribute of Will (Irada) means and why it is necessary for the Creator of a perfectly ordered world.

Core Idea
Divine Will specifies how, when, and where each possible thing exists.

Imam Dardeer begins by defining the attribute of Divine Will, distinguishing it from other attributes. He then provides a compelling argument for why the Creator must possess this attribute, based on the perfect order of the universe. This lesson unpacks both the definition and the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of Irada
The passage defines Irada as an eternal attribute that specifies possible things. Note the key term 'takhsis' (specification).
The definition includes what the attribute does, not just what it is.
2
Understand the scope of specification
The specification covers existence/non-existence, amount, time, place, direction. This shows the comprehensiveness of divine will.
3
Follow the proof from opposites
If Allah lacked will, He would have its opposite—lack of intention. The text lists four opposites: ignorance, death, incapacity, lack of intention.
This is an 'if not X then Y' argument.
4
Connect to the world's order
A being with those opposites cannot create a perfectly ordered world. Since the world is perfectly ordered, the Creator must have will.
The argument is from effect to cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و (إرادة) وهي: صفةٌ أزليّةٌ تُخَصَّصُ المُمكِنَ ببعض ما يجوز عليه، من وجود أو عدم، ومقدار وزمان ومكان وجهة، إذ لو لم يتصف بواحدة مِنْ هذه الصفات الأربعة لاتَّصَفَ بأضدادها، مِنْ جَهْلٍ ومَوتٍ وعجز وعدم قصد إلى شيء، والمتصف بأضدادها لا يُمْكِنُه أنْ يخْلُقَ شَيْئًا مِنَ العالم البديع الإتقان، كيف والعالم موجود على أتَمَّ النظام، وسيأتي لهذا مزيد بيان.
And (Will) is an eternal attribute that specifies the possible thing with some of what is permissible for it, from existence or non-existence, amount, time, place, and direction. For if He were not described with one of these four attributes, He would be described with their opposites: ignorance, death, incapacity, and lack of intention towards anything. And one described with their opposites cannot create anything from the world of wondrous perfection. How, while the world exists in the most perfect order? And there will come for this a more detailed explanation.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of Divine Will (al-Irada) and argues for its necessity. It begins by stating that Will is an eternal attribute (sifa azaliyya) whose function is to specify (takhsis) the possible thing (al-mumkin) with one of its permissible alternatives, such as existence vs. non-existence, or specifying a particular measure, time, place, or direction. It then argues for the necessity of this attribute by way of a proof from contingency: if Allah were not described with the four essential attributes of Life, Knowledge, Will, and Power (implied here as the four), then He would be described with their opposites—ignorance, death, incapacity, and lack of intention. But a being described with such deficiencies cannot create a perfect, orderly world. Since the world indeed exists in the most perfect order, it follows that the Creator must possess these attributes, including Will. The author promises further elaboration later.

Key Terms
إرادة
صفة أزلية
تخصيص
الممكن
الصفات الأربعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine will is the same as divine command.
Correction: The text defines will as an eternal attribute that specifies possibilities, not as a command. They are distinct.

Checkpoint
What is the function of the attribute of Divine Will according to the text?
A) To create the world
B) To specify possible things with permissible alternatives
C) To know all things
D) To command good

Key Takeaway
Divine Will is the eternal attribute by which Allah specifies every detail of contingent things, and its necessity is proven by the perfect order of the universe.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your plans fall apart and things happen at times or places you never expected, do you truly believe that every detail was specified by Allah's eternal will? How does that belief confront your frustration today?
?
The perfect order of the universe testifies that Allah has will and power. But what about the disorder in your own life—your sins, your neglect of duties? Does that disorder reveal a lack of submission to His will? What concrete step will you take tonight to align your will with His?
?
You claim that Allah's will specifies even the smallest details. Yet, how often do you act as if your own choices are independent of His will? When you next make a decision, big or small, will you pause to acknowledge that His will is the ultimate decider? Commit to doing that tomorrow.

Everything That Occurs Is Willed by Allah
Imam Dardeer explains the poem's assertion that every occurring thing is willed by Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to Ahl al-Sunna, every occurrence, whether good or evil, is willed by Allah, and that His will is distinct from His command.

Core Idea
Allah wills everything that occurs, but He only commands good.

This lesson tackles a key point of dispute with the Mu'tazila: do evil deeds happen by Allah's will? The passage from Imam Dardeer clarifies the poem's assertion that every occurring thing is willed by Allah, and explains how this applies to both commanded acts like faith and prohibited acts like disbelief. It establishes the crucial distinction between divine will and divine command.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the flow from the dispute to the two cases.
2
Identify the key claim
Find the sentence: 'nothing occurs in His dominion except what He wills.' This is the central assertion.
3
Examine the two cases
The passage gives two examples: faith of Abu Bakr (commanded) and disbelief of Abu Jahl (prohibited). Notice both are said to occur by Allah's will.
The second case explicitly says 'it is intended by Him due to its occurrence.'
4
Understand the will-command distinction
Realize that Allah may will something He does not command. This refutes the Mu'tazila who claim Allah does not will evil.
The poem line 'falsified the intention is not the command' highlights this.
5
Reflect on the refutation
Consider how this argument undermines the Mu'tazilite position: if Allah did not will evil, it could not occur, but it does occur.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ ذَكَرَ مسألة تتعلَّق بالإرادة، وقَعَ فِيهَا النَّزاعُ بينَنا وَبَينَ الْمُعْتَزِلِةِ بِقَولِهِ: (وكُلُّ كائن) أي: موجودٍ مِنَ الجواهِرِ والأعْراضِ، وهذا مُبتَدَأ، وجُملةً قوله (أرادَه) شَيْءٍ - أي أرادَ وُجودَه - خَبَرُه. فلا يَقَعُ في مُلْكِه تعالى إلا ما يُريدُ، وهذا إذا كان الكائن مما أمر الله به، كإيمان أبي بكر رضي الله عنه، وكذا إيمان بقية المؤمنين. قد أمر به. وإن يكن بضده قد أمرا * فالقصد غير الأمر فاطرح المرا بل (وإن يكن بصده) أي بضد ذلك الكائن (قد أمرا) بألف الإطلاق والضمير يعود عليه تعالى أي وإن كان ذلك الكائن قد أمر الله تعالى بضده ككفر أبي جهل لعنه الله وكذا كفر بقية الكافرين فإنه كائن وقد أمر الله بضده وهو الإيمان ونهى عنه ومع ذلك هو مراد له تعالى بدليل وقوعه.
Then he mentioned an issue related to will, concerning which there is dispute between us and the Mu'tazila by His saying: (And every occurring thing) i.e., an existent from substances and accidents—this is the subject, and the sentence His saying (He willed it) i.e., He willed its existence—is its predicate. So nothing occurs in His dominion except what He wills. And this is if the occurring thing is commanded by Allah, like the faith of Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, and likewise the faith of the rest of the believers—He commanded it. And if it is opposite to what He commanded, i.e., opposite to that occurring thing He commanded, and the pronoun refers to Him, i.e., if that occurring thing – Allah commanded its opposite, like the disbelief of Abu Jahl, may Allah curse him, and likewise the disbelief of the rest of the disbelievers – then it occurs and Allah commanded its opposite, which is faith, and prohibited it, yet it is intended by Him due to its occurrence.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage addresses the theological dispute with the Mu'tazila over divine will. It explains the line of the poem: 'And every occurring thing He willed it.' The author clarifies that 'occurring thing' (ka'in) means any existent, whether substance or accident. The verse asserts that nothing occurs in Allah's dominion except what He wills. The author then considers two cases: (1) when the occurring thing is commanded by Allah, like the faith of Abu Bakr—it is both willed and commanded; (2) when the occurring thing is opposite to what Allah commanded, like the disbelief of Abu Jahl—Allah commanded its opposite (faith) and prohibited it, yet it still occurs, proving that it is willed by Allah despite being prohibited. This refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that Allah does not will evil acts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if Allah wills something, He must command it.
Correction: The passage shows that will and command are distinct: Allah may will something (like disbelief) without commanding it.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the relationship between an occurring thing and Allah's will?
A) Only commanded things are willed
B) Every occurring thing is willed, whether commanded or not
C) Only good deeds are willed
D) Allah wills only what He commands

Key Takeaway
Allah's will encompasses all occurrences, while His command only pertains to good; the two are distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a hardship that seems unjust, do you truly believe Allah willed it? Does this belief make you submit in patience, or do you rebel against His decree? What would change in your heart if you fully embraced that nothing in your life is outside His will?
?
The passage shows that Allah wills the disbelief of the disbelievers yet commands them to believe. How does this affect your understanding of your own sins? Do you ever fall into sin while knowing Allah commands against it? Does this awareness move you to repentance, or do you use it as an excuse? What is the difference between acknowledging Allah's will and excusing your disobedience?
?
The Mu'tazila denied that Allah wills evil because they thought it contradicts His justice. But the passage teaches that His will is broader than His command. How do you respond internally to the idea that Allah wills things He does not command? Does it challenge your comfort? Does it increase your awe of His sovereignty? What practical difference does this belief make in how you view the world's evils?

Distinction Between Divine Will and Command
Imam Dardeer distinguishes between divine will and command, with four logical cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine will and command are distinct, the four logical cases of their combination, and why the Mu'tazilite conflation is mistaken.

Core Idea
Will and command are distinct; what Allah wills may not be commanded, and what He commands may not be willed.

This passage from Imam Dardeer's commentary clarifies a crucial distinction in Islamic theology: divine will (irada) is not the same as divine command (amr). Understanding this is key to resolving debates about predestination and God's relationship to evil. The author presents four logical cases and refutes the Mu'tazilite position that conflates them.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the author's style: he starts with the conclusion, then explains.
2
Identify the four logical divisions
The text mentions 'الأقسام أربعة' (four divisions). Although not listed here, conceptually: commanded and willed, commanded and not willed, not commanded and willed, not commanded and not willed.
These cases show the independence of will and command.
3
Understand the distinction
Note the definitions: will is an attribute that specifies the possible, while command belongs to internal speech (kalam nafsi), like prohibition.
This is the key theoretical point.
4
Examine the refutation of Mu'tazila
The Mu'tazila argue that since Allah does not command evil, He does not will it. The author rejects this equation.
The error is conflating two distinct attributes.
5
Apply the concept
Reflect on how this distinction resolves apparent contradictions about God's will and evil.
Example: Abu Jahl's faith was commanded but not willed; disbelievers' disbelief was willed but not commanded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل: أن كل كائن أي واقع فهو مراد له تعالى، سواء أمر به أو لا، ومفهومه أن ما لم يكن فهو غير مراد الوقوع، سواء أمر به كالإيمان من أبي جهل، أو لم يأمر به كالكفر من المؤمنين، فالأقسام أربعة كما يأتي.

وإذا عرفت ذلك فالقصد – يعني الإرادة – غير الأمر بالشيء، بل ولا يستلزمه، كما أنه لا يستلزمها؛ لما علمت أنهما قد يجتمعان في شيء كإيمان أبي بكر، وقد ينفردان، وذلك لأن الإرادة صفة تخصص الممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه، والأمر يرجع للكلام النفسي كالنهي. فاطرح أي اترك المرا وهو الجدال والنزاع الباطل من المعتزلة الذاهبين إلى أنه تعالى يقع في ملكه ما لا يريد؛ بناء على اتحاد الإرادة والأمر، وهو تعالى لا يأمر بالفحشاء، فلا يريد القبائح كالكفر والمعاصي، وإلا لزم أنه يأمر بها، وهو باطل، وحينئذ فهو تعالى لم يرد من الفاسق إلا إيمانه وطاعته لا كفره ومعصيته.
The conclusion: That every occurring thing, i.e., actual, is willed by Him, whether He commanded it or not. And its implication is that what does not occur is not willed to occur, whether He commanded it like the faith of Abu Jahl, or did not command it like the disbelief of the believers. So the divisions are four, as will come.

And if you know that, then the intention (i.e., the will) is other than the command of a thing, rather it does not even entail it, just as it does not entail it; because you have learned that they may combine in one thing like the faith of Abu Bakr, and they may be separate. And that is because will is an attribute that specifies the possible with some of what is permissible for it, and command returns to the internal speech like prohibition. So discard (i.e., leave) disputation, which is the false argumentation from the Mu'tazila who hold that something occurs in His dominion that He does not will, based on the unity of will and command, and that He does not command abomination, so He does not will ugly acts like disbelief and sins, otherwise it would necessitate that He commands them, which is false. And then He did not will from the sinner except his faith and obedience, not his disbelief and disobedience.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage summarizes the relationship between divine will and command, and refutes the Mu'tazilite position. It first states the conclusion: every actual thing is willed by Allah, regardless of whether He commanded it. The logical opposite: what does not occur is not willed to occur. This yields four logical cases: (1) commanded and willed (e.g., faith of believers), (2) commanded but not willed (e.g., faith of Abu Jahl—since he did not believe, it was commanded but not willed to occur from him), (3) not commanded but willed (e.g., disbelief of disbelievers), (4) neither commanded nor willed (e.g., disbelief of believers? Actually not willed). The author then asserts that the intention (qasd), meaning the will, is distinct from command. Will is an eternal attribute that specifies possible things, while command belongs to the category of internal speech (kalam nafsi), like prohibition. The Mu'tazila erroneously conflate the two, arguing that since Allah does not command evil, He does not will it. The author rejects this, noting that will and command can be separate, and that things can occur in Allah's dominion that He does not command but still wills.

Key Terms
القصد
الأمر
الإرادة
الكلام النفسي
الجدال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If Allah wills something, He must also command it; and if He commands something, He must will it.
Correction: Will and command are distinct attributes. Allah may will something without commanding it (e.g., the disbelief of a disbeliever), and command something without willing it (e.g., the faith of Abu Jahl, which He commanded but did not will to occur from him).

Checkpoint
How many logical divisions of will and command does the author mention?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Divine will governs existence; divine command governs obligation; they are independent attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you genuinely accept that Allah willed that sin to happen? Or do you, like the Mu'tazila, try to exempt Him from willing evil to protect His justice? Can you sincerely repent while holding that He willed your sin, and what does that mean for your trust in His wisdom?
?
If Allah wills everything that occurs, including your failures, how does that change your response to them? Will you stop blaming yourself as if you could have avoided what He willed, and instead turn to Him for mercy, acknowledging that He alone is the ultimate cause?
?
The Mu'tazila thought they were honoring Allah by denying He wills evil. Where in your life do you 'protect' Allah by limiting His sovereignty over your circumstances? Are you ready to submit to the full reality that nothing happens outside His will, even if it tests your understanding of His goodness?

The Distinction Between Divine Will and Command
Dardeer defines the distinction between divine will and command using an example and a general principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine will (irada) and command (amr) are distinct attributes, and that everything that occurs is willed by Allah, whether commanded or not.

Core Idea
Will and command are distinct: will particularizes, command speaks.

This passage from Imam Dardeer's commentary clarifies a crucial point in Islamic theology: the difference between what Allah wills and what He commands. Using the example of Abu Jahl's disbelief, the author shows that something can occur by Allah's will even if He has commanded its opposite. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion and refutes the Mu'tazilite position that equates will and command.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice the poem line and the commentary that follows.
The poem line is in red in the original.
2
Understand the example of Abu Jahl
The author uses Abu Jahl's disbelief as an example. Though Allah commanded faith and prohibited disbelief, Abu Jahl's disbelief occurred and is therefore willed by Allah.
This shows that will and command can separate.
3
Grasp the general principle
The author states: everything that occurs is willed; what does not occur is not willed, whether commanded or not. This implies a logical division into four categories.
The four categories are mentioned but not fully listed here.
4
Identify the distinction
The author explicitly says 'al-qasd (will) is other than the command' and explains the nature of each: will particularizes the possible, command is part of internal speech.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
در أمر الله به، كإيمان أبي بكر رَضِيَ الله، وكذا إيمان بقية المؤمنين. قد أَمَرَ القسم الأول: الإلهيات ٣٤ - وإنْ يَكُنْ بضده قدْ أَمَرا * فالقَصْدُ غَيْرُ الأمرِ، فاطرَح المرا بلْ (وإنْ يَكُنْ بصَدِّهِ)، أي: بضد ذلك الكائن ( قد أمرا) - بألف الإطلاق - والضمير يعود عليه تعالى، أي: وإن كان ذلك الكائنُ قَدْ أَمَرَ اللهُ تعالى بضده، ككُفْرِ أَبي جهل لعنه الله ، وكذا كُفْرِ بقيّة الكافرين، فإنه كائن وقدْ أَمَرَ الله بضده، وهو الإيمان، ونهى عنه ومع ذلك هو مُراد له تعالى بدليل وقوعه. والحاصلُ : أَنَّ كُلَّ كائن أي واقع فهو مُراد له تعالى، سواءٌ أَمَرَ به أو لا، ومفهومه أَنَّ مَا لَمْ يَكُنْ فهو غَيْرُ مُرادِ الوُقوع، سواءٌ أَمَرَ به كالإيمانِ مِنْ أبِي جَهْلٍ، أو يأمر به كالكُفْرِ مِنَ المؤمنين، فالأقسام أربعة كما يأتي. وإذا عرفت ذلك فالقصْدُ) -يعني: الإرادة - (غَيرُ الأمر) بالشيء، بل ولا يستلزمه، كما أنه لا يستلزمها؛ لما علمتَ أنهما قد يجتمعان في شَيءٍ كإيمان أبي بكر، وقد ينفردان)، وذلك لأنَّ الإرادة صفةٌ تُخَصِّصُ المُمْكِنَ ببعض ما يجوز عليه، والأمْرَ يرجِعُ للكلامِ النَّفْسِيِّ كَالنَّهْيِ. (فاطرَح) أي: اتركِ، (المرا) وهو الجدالُ والنَّزاعُ الباطلُ مِنَ المُعتَزِلِةِ الذاهبين إلى أنه تعالى يقع في مُلْكِهِ ما لا يُريدُ؛ بناءً على اتحادِ الإرادة والأمر، وهو تعالى لا يأمُرُ بالفحشاء، فلا يُريدُ القبائح كالكُفْرِ والمعاصي، وإلا لَزِمَ أنه يأمُرُ بها، وهو باطل، وحينئذ فهو تعالى لم يُرِدْ مِنَ الفاسِقِ إلا إيمانه وطاعته لا كُفْرَه ومعصيته. (١) كما في كفر أبي جهل فإنه مراد غير مأمور به. . وهو شنبه
He commanded it: like the faith of Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, and likewise the faith of the rest of the believers. The first section: al-ilahiyyat. 34. 'And if He has commanded its opposite, then the intent is other than the command, so abandon the dispute.' Rather 'And if He has commanded its opposite' i.e., the opposite of that which occurs, 'has commanded' - with the alif of absolute form - and the pronoun refers to the Most High. That is: if that which occurs, Allah Most High has commanded its opposite, like the disbelief of Abu Jahl, may Allah curse him, and likewise the disbelief of the rest of the disbelievers, for it occurs and Allah has commanded its opposite, which is faith, and prohibited it, yet it is willed by Him Most High due to the evidence of its occurrence. The conclusion: every occurring thing is willed by Him Most High, whether He commanded it or not, and its implication is that what does not occur is not willed to occur, whether He commanded it like faith from Abu Jahl, or He commands it like disbelief from the believers. So the divisions are four, as will come. And when you know that, then 'the intent' - meaning the will - 'is other than the command' of the thing; rather it does not entail it, just as it does not entail it; because you know that they may both come together in something like the faith of Abu Bakr, and they may separate. This is because the will is an attribute that particularizes the possible among what is permissible for it, while the command pertains to internal speech (al-kalam al-nafsi) like the prohibition. 'So abandon' i.e., leave, 'the dispute' which is the argument and false contention of the Mu'tazila who hold that the Most High may occur in His dominion what He does not will, based on the unity of will and command - and He Most High does not command indecency, so He does not will ugly acts like disbelief and sins, otherwise it would necessitate that He commands them, which is false. Consequently, He Most High did not will from the sinner except his faith and obedience, not his disbelief and disobedience. (1) As in the disbelief of Abu Jahl, for it is willed but not commanded. And it is [؟].
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage defines and argues for the distinction between divine will (irada) and command (amr). The author begins with the line from the poem: 'And if He has commanded its opposite, then the intent is other than the command, so abandon the dispute.' He explains that the pronoun refers to Allah. The example: Abu Jahl's disbelief occurs, but Allah commanded its opposite (faith) and prohibited disbelief; yet the disbelief still occurs and is willed by Allah because it happens. The general principle: everything that occurs is willed by Allah, whether it is commanded or not. Conversely, what does not occur is not willed to occur, even if it is commanded (e.g., faith from Abu Jahl) or not commanded (hypothetically). This implies four divisions (enumerated later). Then the author emphasizes that al-qasd (i.e., al-irada) is other than al-amr (command). They are distinct attributes: irada particularizes the possible, while amr pertains to internal speech (kalam nafsi). The Mu'tazila wrongly equate them, arguing that since Allah does not command evil, He does not will it; thus they deny that evil deeds are willed by Allah. The author refutes this by citing the evidence of occurrence: whatever occurs is willed. The final line (with the unclear 'wa huwa shanabah') likely concludes the refutation.

Key Terms
إرادة
أمر
كائن
مراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between occurrence and divine will?
A) Only commanded things occur.
B) Everything that occurs is willed by Allah.
C) Only good things are willed.
D) Occurrence is independent of will.

Key Takeaway
Divine will and command are distinct; everything that happens does so by Allah's will, regardless of whether He commanded it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah willed Abu Jahl's disbelief even while commanding its opposite, how do you view your own sins? Do you ever blame Allah for your disobedience, or do you take full responsibility while acknowledging His will? This week, when you sin, pause and say: 'Allah willed this, but He commanded its opposite I am accountable.'
?
The lesson shows that Allah's command and will are distinct. Are you living as if Allah's command is the ultimate authority, or do you sometimes assume that what happens is what Allah 'wants' in the sense of command? Reflect on a specific command you find hard to obey. Does the fact that Allah commands it but may not will it for you (as in faith from Abu Jahl) change your motivation? Commit to obeying that command regardless of your feelings.
?
Everything that occurs is willed by Allah. Think of a recent hardship in your life. Can you sincerely say 'This is willed by Allah' without resentment? How does this truth affect your trust in Allah's wisdom? Write a short prayer thanking Him for willing that event, even if it was hard.

Refuting the Mu'tazila: Will and Command Are Not Identical
Dardeer refutes the Mu'tazilite equation of will and command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite claim that divine will and command are identical, and the refutation based on the evidence of occurrence.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila err by equating will and command; the proof of occurrence shows otherwise.

In this passage, Imam Dardeer directly addresses the Mu'tazilite position that Allah cannot will evil because He does not command it. Using the previously established distinction, he refutes them by pointing out that many things occur by Allah's will even though He commanded their opposite. The discussion highlights the importance of distinguishing between divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the refutation. Note the mention of the Mu'tazila.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
The Mu'tazila argue that since Allah does not command indecency, He does not will it; thus evil deeds are not willed by Allah.
This is based on equating will and command.
3
Understand the refutation
The author refutes by citing the evidence of occurrence: things like Abu Jahl's disbelief occur, so they are willed, even though not commanded. The will and command are distinct.
The author calls the Mu'tazilite argument 'false contention'.
4
Appreciate the theological significance
This distinction safeguards the Ash'ari doctrine that Allah's will is comprehensive, encompassing both good and evil, while His command only enjoins good.
This is a key point in qadar discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
در أمر الله به، كإيمان أبي بكر رَضِيَ الله، وكذا إيمان بقية المؤمنين. قد أَمَرَ القسم الأول: الإلهيات ٣٤ - وإنْ يَكُنْ بضده قدْ أَمَرا * فالقَصْدُ غَيْرُ الأمرِ، فاطرَح المرا بلْ (وإنْ يَكُنْ بصَدِّهِ)، أي: بضد ذلك الكائن ( قد أمرا) - بألف الإطلاق - والضمير يعود عليه تعالى، أي: وإن كان ذلك الكائنُ قَدْ أَمَرَ اللهُ تعالى بضده، ككُفْرِ أَبي جهل لعنه الله ، وكذا كُفْرِ بقيّة الكافرين، فإنه كائن وقدْ أَمَرَ الله بضده، وهو الإيمان، ونهى عنه ومع ذلك هو مُراد له تعالى بدليل وقوعه. والحاصلُ : أَنَّ كُلَّ كائن أي واقع فهو مُراد له تعالى، سواءٌ أَمَرَ به أو لا، ومفهومه أَنَّ مَا لَمْ يَكُنْ فهو غَيْرُ مُرادِ الوُقوع، سواءٌ أَمَرَ به كالإيمانِ مِنْ أبِي جَهْلٍ، أو يأمر به كالكُفْرِ مِنَ المؤمنين، فالأقسام أربعة كما يأتي. وإذا عرفت ذلك فالقصْدُ) -يعني: الإرادة - (غَيرُ الأمر) بالشيء، بل ولا يستلزمه، كما أنه لا يستلزمها؛ لما علمتَ أنهما قد يجتمعان في شَيءٍ كإيمان أبي بكر، وقد ينفردان)، وذلك لأنَّ الإرادة صفةٌ تُخَصِّصُ المُمْكِنَ ببعض ما يجوز عليه، والأمْرَ يرجِعُ للكلامِ النَّفْسِيِّ كَالنَّهْيِ. (فاطرَح) أي: اتركِ، (المرا) وهو الجدالُ والنَّزاعُ الباطلُ مِنَ المُعتَزِلِةِ الذاهبين إلى أنه تعالى يقع في مُلْكِهِ ما لا يُريدُ؛ بناءً على اتحادِ الإرادة والأمر، وهو تعالى لا يأمُرُ بالفحشاء، فلا يُريدُ القبائح كالكُفْرِ والمعاصي، وإلا لَزِمَ أنه يأمُرُ بها، وهو باطل، وحينئذ فهو تعالى لم يُرِدْ مِنَ الفاسِقِ إلا إيمانه وطاعته لا كُفْرَه ومعصيته. (١) كما في كفر أبي جهل فإنه مراد غير مأمور به. . وهو شنبه
He commanded it: like the faith of Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, and likewise the faith of the rest of the believers. The first section: al-ilahiyyat. 34. 'And if He has commanded its opposite, then the intent is other than the command, so abandon the dispute.' Rather 'And if He has commanded its opposite' i.e., the opposite of that which occurs, 'has commanded' - with the alif of absolute form - and the pronoun refers to the Most High. That is: if that which occurs, Allah Most High has commanded its opposite, like the disbelief of Abu Jahl, may Allah curse him, and likewise the disbelief of the rest of the disbelievers, for it occurs and Allah has commanded its opposite, which is faith, and prohibited it, yet it is willed by Him Most High due to the evidence of its occurrence. The conclusion: every occurring thing is willed by Him Most High, whether He commanded it or not, and its implication is that what does not occur is not willed to occur, whether He commanded it like faith from Abu Jahl, or He commands it like disbelief from the believers. So the divisions are four, as will come. And when you know that, then 'the intent' - meaning the will - 'is other than the command' of the thing; rather it does not entail it, just as it does not entail it; because you know that they may both come together in something like the faith of Abu Bakr, and they may separate. This is because the will is an attribute that particularizes the possible among what is permissible for it, while the command pertains to internal speech (al-kalam al-nafsi) like the prohibition. 'So abandon' i.e., leave, 'the dispute' which is the argument and false contention of the Mu'tazila who hold that the Most High may occur in His dominion what He does not will, based on the unity of will and command - and He Most High does not command indecency, so He does not will ugly acts like disbelief and sins, otherwise it would necessitate that He commands them, which is false. Consequently, He Most High did not will from the sinner except his faith and obedience, not his disbelief and disobedience. (1) As in the disbelief of Abu Jahl, for it is willed but not commanded. And it is [؟].
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage defines and argues for the distinction between divine will (irada) and command (amr). The author begins with the line from the poem: 'And if He has commanded its opposite, then the intent is other than the command, so abandon the dispute.' He explains that the pronoun refers to Allah. The example: Abu Jahl's disbelief occurs, but Allah commanded its opposite (faith) and prohibited disbelief; yet the disbelief still occurs and is willed by Allah because it happens. The general principle: everything that occurs is willed by Allah, whether it is commanded or not. Conversely, what does not occur is not willed to occur, even if it is commanded (e.g., faith from Abu Jahl) or not commanded (hypothetically). This implies four divisions (enumerated later). Then the author emphasizes that al-qasd (i.e., al-irada) is other than al-amr (command). They are distinct attributes: irada particularizes the possible, while amr pertains to internal speech (kalam nafsi). The Mu'tazila wrongly equate them, arguing that since Allah does not command evil, He does not will it; thus they deny that evil deeds are willed by Allah. The author refutes this by citing the evidence of occurrence: whatever occurs is willed. The final line (with the unclear 'wa huwa shanabah') likely concludes the refutation.

Key Terms
معتزلة
إرادة
أمر
مراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that Allah does not will evil because He does not command it.
Correction: The passage shows that Allah's will is comprehensive; He wills both good and evil, while His command only enjoins good. Occurrence is proof of will.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazilite argument regarding divine will and command?
A) Will and command are identical, so Allah wills whatever He commands.
B) Will and command are distinct, so Allah may will evil.
C) Will and command are opposite.
D) There is no relationship.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite equation of will and command is false; the occurrence of evil proves it is willed by Allah, even though He commands its opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever blamed yourself for a sin as if it happened outside Allah's will? How does knowing that Allah wills even your disobedience change your understanding of His sovereignty and your responsibility?
?
If Allah wills your sins but does not command them, how does that affect your view of His mercy and justice? Are you comfortable with the tension, or does it make you question His goodness?
?
Right now, is there a sin you are struggling with? In light of this lesson, how can you reconcile that Allah willed it to happen, yet you must repent and strive against it? What will you change in your daily spiritual practice to honor both His will and His command?

The Attributes of Speech, Hearing, and Sight
Dardeer defines the divine attributes of Speech, Hearing, and Sight

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of Allah's attributes of Speech, Hearing, and Sight, and how they differ from human attributes and from each other.

Core Idea
Allah's Speech, Hearing, and Sight are eternal and unlike anything in creation.

Dardeer now completes his enumeration of the seven attributes of meanings by defining Speech, Hearing, and Sight. He emphasizes that these attributes are eternal and self-subsisting, not composed of letters or sounds, and that they disclose existents in a way distinct from Knowledge. Read carefully as he clarifies the unique nature of each.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of Dardeer's definitions. Notice the terms used for each attribute.
Pay attention to the metaphorical expression in the line about 'al-ibṣār'.
2
Identify the definitions of each attribute
Locate how Dardeer defines Speech (no letters/sounds, indicating all known things), Hearing, and Sight (eternal attributes disclosing all existents).
3
Understand the distinctions
Note how Dardeer distinguishes disclosure through Hearing and Sight from disclosure through Knowledge, and how the two differ from each other.
This reinforces that each attribute has a unique function, though their exact nature is beyond human comprehension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخامس صفات المعاني كلامه تعالى، وهو صفة أزَليَّةٌ نفسيّة، ليستْ بحَرْفٍ ولا صَوت، تدل على جميع المعلومات. (و) سادسها (السمعُ) وسابعها (الإبصارُ)، يعني: البصر، فقد أطلَقَ اسْمَ الْمُسَبَّبِ وأراد السبب مجازًا، يدلُّ على مُرادِه أَنَّ الكلام في المعاني، وكذا ما يأتي في التعلق. ولو قال: «ثُمَّ البصر» لكانَ أوضحَ. والسمع والبصرُ صِفَتانِ أزليَّتان ينكشِفُ بهما جميعُ الموجوداتِ انكشاف تاما. والانكشاف بهما يغاير الانكشاف بالعِلْم، كما أنَّ الانكشاف بإحداهما يغاير الانكشاف بالأخرى.
And the fifth of the attributes of meanings is His Speech, may He be exalted, and it is an eternal, self-subsisting attribute, not consisting of a letter nor a sound, indicating all known things. And the sixth is Hearing, and the seventh is Sight, meaning vision (al-basar); he used the name of the effect and intended the cause metaphorically, indicating his intent that speech is among the meanings, and likewise what comes in attachment. And if he had said 'then vision', it would have been clearer. And Hearing and Sight are two eternal attributes through which all existents are disclosed with complete disclosure. And disclosure through them differs from disclosure through Knowledge, just as disclosure through one of them differs from disclosure through the other.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of three divine attributes: Speech, Hearing, and Sight. Dardeer first states that divine Speech (al-kalām) is the fifth attribute of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī). It is an eternal, self-subsisting (nafsiyya) attribute, not composed of letters or sounds, and it encompasses all known things. This distinguishes it from human speech. Next, he identifies Hearing (al-samʿ) as the sixth and Sight (al-basar) as the seventh. He notes that the poet used the term 'al-ibṣār' (the effect) to refer to 'al-basar' (the faculty) metaphorically. Dardeer then explains that Hearing and Sight are two eternal attributes by which all existents are fully disclosed. Importantly, the disclosure through Hearing and Sight is different in kind from disclosure through Knowledge (al-ʿilm), and the disclosure through Hearing differs from that through Sight. This underscores that each attribute has a distinct function, though their exact nature is beyond human comprehension.

الكلام
السمع
الإبصار
الصفة الأزلية
الصفة النفسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that divine Speech is composed of letters and sounds like human speech.
Correction: Dardeer clarifies that divine Speech is an eternal attribute without letters or sounds.

Checkpoint
How does the disclosure through Hearing and Sight differ from the disclosure through Knowledge?
A) It is less complete
B) It is a different type of disclosure
C) It is only for temporal things
D) It is through created means

Key Takeaway
Allah's Speech, Hearing, and Sight are eternal, non-corporeal attributes that fully disclose existents in a manner distinct from His Knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that Allah's Speech is not composed of letters or sounds. Do you ever catch yourself imagining God speaking like a human, with a voice? How would your awe of His Speech increase if you truly internalized that it is an eternal, self-subsisting attribute that encompasses all things?
?
The passage says that Hearing and Sight disclose all existents completely, yet differently from each other. In your daily life, do you rely more on your hearing or your sight to perceive Allah's signs? Which attribute of His are you most failing to honor by your actions?
?
The common misunderstanding is to think Allah's attributes resemble creation. Examine your heart: Are there subtle ways you still attribute human-like limitations to Allah, despite knowing better? Write down one specific thought you will correct starting today.

The Eternal Psychic Attribute of Speech
Dardeer defines divine speech as a psychic attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine speech is an eternal psychic attribute, not composed of letters or sounds, and why it cannot be contingent.

Core Idea
Divine speech is an eternal psychic attribute, not letters or sounds.

In this passage, Imam Dardeer introduces the fifth attribute of meanings: speech. He defines it as an eternal psychic attribute that indicates all known things. He then provides a proof that it cannot consist of letters or sounds, because those are contingent. This lesson focuses on that definition and the rational proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic definition carefully. Note the terms 'azaliyya nafsiyya' and that it is not harf nor sawt.
The definition is concise but packed with meaning.
2
Understand the proof
The proof relies on the contingency of letters and sounds. Why does that show that psychic speech is not composed of them?
Think about what would happen if psychic speech were composed of contingent elements.
3
Connect to the learning objective
Reflect on how this establishes the unique nature of divine speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخامس صفات المعاني كلامه تعالى، وهو صفة أزَليَّةٌ نفسيّة، ليستْ بحَرْفٍ ولا صَوت، تدل على جميع المعلومات. (1) لأن الحروف والأصوات لا تكون إلا حادثة، فلو تركب الكلام النفسي منها لكان حادثا، والكلام اللفظي دليل على النفسي، ولا شك أن الدال غير المدلول، لأن المدلول قديم ليس بصوت ولا حرف ولا يوصف بالتقديم والتأخير ولا البداية أو النهاية وليس بمخلوق، بخلاف الدال فهو ألفاظ وحروف ومنه تعقيب وترتيب وتقديم وتأخير وهكذا، وينقسم إلى وعد ووعيد وأمر ونهى وخبر، وأما الصفة القديمة فيستحيل انقسامها. اعلم أن كلام الله تعالى يطلق بالاشتراك على الحسي والنفسي، الذي هو الصفة القديمة، فهو حقيقة عرفية في كل فالحسي ما كان بحرف وصوت ومدلوله بعض مدلول الكلام النفسي القديم القائم بذاته تعالى، فالكتب السماوية دالة على بعض مدلول الكلام النفسي ولا يحيط بكل مدلوله إلا هو، لأن مدلول الكلام النفسي الواجبات والمستحيلات والجائزات تفصيلا، وأما الكتب السماوية فقد دلت على بعض من الواجبات تفصيلا، وكل الواجبات إجمالا، وكذا المستحيلات والجائزات. وتكليم الله لسيدنا موسى على الجبل كان بالكلام النفسي على التحقيق عند الأشاعرة وبعض الماتريدية، خلافا للمعتزلة والبعض الآخر من الماتريدية. اهـ ملخصا.
And the fifth of the attributes of meanings is His speech, exalted is He. It is an eternal psychic attribute, not consisting of a letter nor a sound, and it indicates all known things. (1) Because letters and sounds only occur as contingent, so if the psychic speech were composed of them, it would be contingent. And verbal speech is a proof of psychic speech, and there is no doubt that the proof is other than the proven, because the proven is pre-eternal, not a sound nor a letter, not described with precedence or delay, nor with beginning or end, and it is not created, contrary to the proof which is words and letters, and from it [comes] commentary and ordering and precedence and delay and so on. And it divides into promise and threat, command and prohibition, and report. As for the eternal attribute, its division is impossible. Know that the speech of Allah, exalted is He, is applied by common usage to the sensory and the psychic, which is the eternal attribute. So it is a customary reality in each: the sensory is what is with letter and sound, and its signification is part of the signification of the eternal psychic speech subsisting by His essence, exalted is He. So the heavenly books indicate part of the signification of psychic speech, and none encompasses all its signification except Him, because the signification of psychic speech is the obligatory, the impossible, and the possible in detail. As for the heavenly books, they indicate some of the obligatory in detail, and all the obligatory in summary, and likewise the impossible and the possible. And the speaking of Allah to our master Moses on the mountain was by psychic speech according to the verification of the Ashʿarīs and some Māturīdīs, contrary to the Muʿtazila and the other group of Māturīdīs. End of summary.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of divine speech (kalām) as one of the seven attributes of meanings. The author begins by stating that it is an eternal psychic attribute, not composed of letters or sounds, and that it indicates all known things. He then explains why it cannot be composed of letters or sounds: because those are contingent, and the eternal attribute cannot be contingent. Verbal speech (al-kalām al-lafẓī) is only a proof (dalīl) of the inner psychic speech (al-kalām al-nafsī), and the proof is distinct from the proven. The proven (the psychic speech) is pre-eternal, not a sound or letter, not subject to temporal ordering, and is not created. In contrast, the proof (words and letters) is created and can be divided into categories such as promise, threat, command, prohibition, and report; such division is impossible for the eternal attribute. Next, the author clarifies that the term 'speech of Allah' is used in two senses: sensory (al-ḥissī) and psychic (al-nafsī) — the latter being the eternal attribute. Sensory speech is what consists of letters and sounds and indicates only part of what the psychic speech indicates. The heavenly books contain sensory speech that indicates some of the contents of the psychic speech in detail and all of it in summary. Finally, the author settles the theological dispute over the nature of Allah's speech to Moses: it was psychic speech, not sensory, according to the Ashʿarīs and some Māturīdīs, contrary to the Muʿtazila and other Māturīdīs. This summary is derived from a longer work.

الصفة النفسية
الحروف
الأصوات

Correction: Divine speech is an eternal psychic attribute, not composed of letters or sounds, as letters and sounds are contingent.

The Relationship Between Psychic and Verbal Speech
Dardeer distinguishes psychic from verbal speech and discusses the implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between psychic and verbal speech, and how the heavenly books and prophetic revelation relate to the eternal attribute.

Core Idea
Verbal speech points to psychic speech, which alone is eternal.

After defining divine speech as a psychic attribute, Dardeer explains how it relates to the verbal speech we read in scriptures. He clarifies that verbal speech is a proof of the psychic speech, and that the heavenly books communicate only part of its meaning. He also addresses the famous case of Allah's speech to Moses.

Guided Path
1
Read the distinction
Pay attention to the relationship between the two types of speech: verbal speech is daleel (proof) of psychic speech.
The proof is distinct from the proven.
2
Note the division of verbal speech
Verbal speech divides into promise, threat, command, prohibition, and report. Why can't the eternal attribute be divided?
Think about the nature of a simple eternal attribute.
3
Consider the heavenly books
How do they relate to the psychic speech? They indicate some in detail and all in summary.
4
Examine the Moses incident
What does this incident show about the nature of divine speech?
The Ashʿarī position is that it was psychic speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the fifth of the attributes of meanings is His speech, exalted is He. It is an eternal psychic attribute, not consisting of a letter nor a sound, and it indicates all known things. (1) Because letters and sounds only occur as contingent, so if the psychic speech were composed of them, it would be contingent. And verbal speech is a proof of psychic speech, and there is no doubt that the proof is other than the proven, because the proven is pre-eternal, not a sound nor a letter, not described with precedence or delay, nor with beginning or end, and it is not created, contrary to the proof which is words and letters, and from it [comes] commentary and ordering and precedence and delay and so on. And it divides into promise and threat, command and prohibition, and report. As for the eternal attribute, its division is impossible. Know that the speech of Allah, exalted is He, is applied by common usage to the sensory and the psychic, which is the eternal attribute. So it is a customary reality in each: the sensory is what is with letter and sound, and its signification is part of the signification of the eternal psychic speech subsisting by His essence, exalted is He. So the heavenly books indicate part of the signification of psychic speech, and none encompasses all its signification except Him, because the signification of psychic speech is the obligatory, the impossible, and the possible in detail. As for the heavenly books, they indicate some of the obligatory in detail, and all the obligatory in summary, and likewise the impossible and the possible. And the speaking of Allah to our master Moses on the mountain was by psychic speech according to the verification of the Ashʿarīs and some Māturīdīs, contrary to the Muʿtazila and the other group of Māturīdīs. Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of divine speech (kalām) as one of the seven attributes of meanings. The author begins by stating that it is an eternal psychic attribute, not composed of letters or sounds, and that it indicates all known things. He then explains why it cannot be composed of letters or sounds: because those are contingent, and the eternal attribute cannot be contingent. Verbal speech (al-kalām al-lafẓī) is only a proof (dalīl) of the inner psychic speech (al-kalām al-nafsī), and the proof is distinct from the proven. The proven (the psychic speech) is pre-eternal, not a sound or letter, not subject to temporal ordering, and is not created. In contrast, the proof (words and letters) is created and can be divided into categories such as promise, threat, command, prohibition, and report; such division is impossible for the eternal attribute. Next, the author clarifies that the term 'speech of Allah' is used in two senses: sensory (al-ḥissī) and psychic (al-nafsī) — the latter being the eternal attribute. Sensory speech is what consists of letters and sounds and indicates only part of what the psychic speech indicates. The heavenly books contain sensory speech that indicates some of the contents of the psychic speech in detail and all of it in summary. Finally, the author settles the theological dispute over the nature of Allah's speech to Moses: it was psychic speech, not sensory, according to the Ashʿarīs and some Māturīdīs, contrary to the Muʿtazila and other Māturīdīs. This summary is derived from a longer work.

Key Terms
الكلام النفسي
الكلام الحسي
الكلام اللفظي
الدليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Quran (verbal speech) is identical to the eternal attribute.
Correction: The Quran is verbal speech that indicates the eternal psychic speech; they are distinct, as proof and proven.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the speech of Allah to Moses occur according to the Ashʿarīs?
A) Through letters and sounds
B) Through psychic speech
C) Through an angel
D) Through a vision

Key Takeaway
Verbal speech is a proof of the eternal psychic speech, and the heavenly books convey part of its meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you recognize that the words you utter are only a finite expression pointing to an infinite, eternal speech that no one fully comprehends except Allah? How would reciting with this awareness change your focus and awe?
?
The source tells us that the heavenly books indicate only some of the obligatory in detail but all of it in summary. Are you content with knowing Allah's commands and prohibitions in summary, or do you strive to understand them deeply? What is one verse you have read without seeking its deeper meaning that you will now study carefully?
?
The passage distinguishes between outward verbal speech and inner psychic speech. Consider your own speech: do your words accurately reflect the inner convictions of your heart? Is there any inconsistency you need to address starting today?

Divine Hearing and Sight: Eternal Attributes of Complete Disclosure
Dardeer defines divine hearing and sight as eternal attributes causing complete disclosure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of divine hearing and sight as eternal attributes that cause full disclosure of existents, distinct from knowledge, and the obligation to affirm them without modality.

Core Idea
Allah's hearing and sight bring complete disclosure, distinct from knowledge.

In this passage, Imam Dardeer explains the sixth and seventh attributes of meanings: hearing and sight. He clarifies that these are eternal attributes that cause a complete disclosure of all existents, and that their mode of disclosure is different from that of knowledge. He then emphasizes the obligation of believing in these attributes without attempting to understand how, because Allah is unlike anything.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Dardeer defines hearing and sight as eternal attributes that cause complete disclosure.
Pay attention to the phrasing: 'two eternal attributes by which all existents are disclosed with complete disclosure.'
2
Compare disclosure modes
Notice that Dardeer emphasizes that disclosure by hearing and sight differs from disclosure by knowledge, and that each of them differs from the other. This distinction preserves the uniqueness of each attribute.
The disclosure modes are distinct, even though all are complete.
3
Understand the obligation
The passage concludes with the obligation to affirm the attributes without knowing the modality (kayfiyya). Reflect on why this is necessary: because Allah is like nothing.
This is a universal principle for all attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) سادسها (السمعُ) وسابعها (الإبصارُ)، يعني: البصر، فقد أطلَقَ اسْمَ الْمُسَبَّبِ وأراد السبب مجازًا، يدلُّ على مُرادِه أَنَّ الكلام في المعاني، وكذا ما يأتي في التعلق. ولو قال: «ثُمَّ البصر» لكانَ أوضحَ. والسمع والبصرُ صِفَتانِ أزليَّتان ينكشِفُ بهما جميعُ الموجوداتِ انكشاف تاما. والانكشاف بهما يغاير الانكشاف بالعِلْم، كما أنَّ الانكشاف بإحداهما يغاير الانكشاف بالأخرى. ثُمَّ فَرَّعَ عَلَى صِفاتِ المعاني في الجُمْلة، إذ التفريعُ إنما يَظْهَرُ على الأربعةِ الأُوَلِ. الواجب على المكلف اعتقاد ثبوت صفاته تعالى من غير معرفة الكنه على وجه يخالف صفات المخلوقات لأنه تعالى لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ ﴾ [الشورى: ١١]
(And) the sixth of them is hearing and the seventh is sight, meaning vision. He used the name of the effect and intended the cause metaphorically, indicating his intent that the discussion is about attributes of meanings, and similarly what comes regarding relational attributes. And if he had said 'then vision' it would be clearer. Hearing and sight are two eternal attributes by which all existents are disclosed with complete disclosure. And the disclosure by them differs from disclosure by knowledge, just as the disclosure by one of them differs from disclosure by the other. Then he branched off from the attributes of meanings in general, for the branching only becomes apparent on the first four. It is obligatory upon the responsible person to believe in the establishment of His attributes, exalted is He, without knowing the modality, in a manner that opposes the attributes of created beings, because He, exalted is He, is like nothing.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth and seventh attributes of meanings: divine hearing (samʿ) and sight (baṣar). The author notes that the poet used the name of the effect (al-musabbab) and intended the cause metaphorically, meaning that when he said 'hearing and sight' he referred to the attributes themselves, not their effects. He then states that these are two eternal attributes that bring about a complete disclosure (inkishāf tāmm) of all existents. This disclosure is distinct from the disclosure by knowledge, and each of them (hearing and sight) has a distinct mode of disclosure. Next, the author makes a general remark: the branching off from the attributes of meanings only becomes apparent with the first four attributes (life, knowledge, will, and power). Finally, he adds a general principle: it is obligatory for every responsible person to affirm the attributes of Allah without attempting to understand their modality (al-kayfiyya), because Allah is unlike anything in creation.

البصر

Correction: The passage emphasizes that disclosure by hearing and sight differs from disclosure by knowledge, and each of the two attributes has its own distinct mode of disclosure.

God as the Free Agent: Refuting Necessary Causality
Dardeer refutes the philosophers' claim that God is a necessary cause and proves He is a free agent using the diversity of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the diversity of the world proves that God is a free agent, not a necessary cause, and how this refutes the philosophers' doctrine.

Core Idea
God's free will is proven by the world's diversity.

In this passage, Imam Dardeer refutes the philosophers' claim that God acts by necessity, like a natural or efficient cause. He shows that the variety in creation—animate, inanimate, dark, light, sweet, bitter—cannot come from a single, uniform cause. This leads him to affirm that God is al-Fāʿil al-Mukhtār, the Free Agent, who creates as He wills and is not compelled by any external factor.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the author's direct style and Quranic quotations sink in before analyzing.
Notice the strong language against the philosophers.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim and Dardeer's response
The philosophers say God is a 'doer by cause' (فاعل بالعلة). Dardeer refutes this by affirming God as the Free Agent. Find how he contrasts the two views.
3
Examine the evidence from the diversity of the world
The world contains many different kinds: inanimate/living, dark/luminous, sweet/bitter. Dardeer argues this diversity cannot result from a single necessary cause. Reflect on why that is a strong proof.
He quotes the Quran about fruits watered by the same water but differing in taste.
4
Reflect on the consequences and warning
Dardeer states that the philosophers' view leads to denying bodily resurrection and essential divine attributes. He ends with a stern warning about their deceptive arguments.
The passage shows how false beliefs can lead to further errors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَولُه (فهْوَ الإِلهُ) أي : المعبودُ بحَقِّ، (الفاعل المختارُ) أي: الذي إِنْ شَاءَ فَعَلَ وَإِنْ شاء ترك، وَرَبُّكَ يَخْلُقُ مَا يَشَاءُ وَيَختَارُ، لا أنه فاعل بالطبع أو بالعلة، خلاف للفلاسفة الملعونين، ولذا قالوا بقدَمِ العالم، لأنه يلزَمُ مِنْ قِدَمِ العِلَّةِ قِدَمُ المعلول ونفَوا عَن الله تعالى صفاته الذاتية، وهو مذهَبْ باطلٌ وكُفْرٌ مراح، إلى غير ذلك، كما أشار ومما يدلُّ على بطلانه تنوُّعُ العالم إلى أنواع مختلفة، فبعضه جماد، وبعضُه حيوان وبعضُه ظُلْماني، وبعضُه نوران، وبعضُه حُلْو، وبعضُه له الكِتابُ العزيز في كثير مِنَ الآي. قال تعالى: يُسْقَى بِمَاءِ وَاحِدٍ وَنُفَضِّلُ بَعْضَهَا عَلَى بَعْضٍ فِي الْأُكُل إِنَّ فِي ذَلِكَ لَآيَاتٍ لِّقَوْمٍ يَعْقِلُونَ)، فهذا يشير إلى أنَّ هؤلاء الخاسرين ليسوا بعقلاء، إذْ فِعلُ العِلّة والطبيعة ليس إلا شيئًا واحِدًا غَيْرَ مُختلف أَفَلاَ يَنظُرُونَ إِلَى الإِبْل كَيْفَ خُلِقَتْ وَإِلَى السَّمَاءِ كَيْفَ رُفِعَتْ وَإِلَى الْجِبَالِ كَيْفَ نُصِبَتْ وَإِلَى الْأَرْضَ كَيْفَ سُطِحَتْ ، أَفَلَمْ يَنظُرُوا إِلَى السَّمَاءِ فَوْقَهُمْ كَيْفَ بَنَيْنَاهَا وَزَيَّنَّاهَا وَمَا لَهَا مِن فُرُوج * وَالْأَرْضَ مَدَدْنَاهَا وَأَلْقَيْنَا فِيهَا رَوَاسِيَ وَأَنبَتْنَا فِيهَا مِن كُلِّ زَوْجٍ بَهِيجٍ ولَكِنْ مَنْ يُضْلِ اللَّهُ فما له مِنْ هَادٍ. ومما بنوه على مذهبهم عدَمُ المعاد الجسماني، وقد زخرفوا مذاهبهم بشُبَه ظنّيّةٍ خيالية، كسَراب بقيعة يحسبه الظمآن ماءً حتى إذا جاءه لم يجده شَيئًا، فضلوا وأَضَلُّوا حتى ظَنَّ كثيرٌ مِنَ الناسِ أنَّ هذهِ الزخارفَ عِلْمٌ ، بل فضَّلوا المتمسكين بها على عُلماء الشريعة، كلّا سَوفَ يعلمون، ثُمَّ كَلَّا سَوفَ يعلمون.
His saying (So He is al-Ilah) meaning: the One truly worshipped, (the Free Agent) meaning: He who if He wills He does and if He wills He leaves, and your Lord creates what He wills and chooses. He is not a doer by nature or by cause, contrary to the accursed philosophers, and therefore they said with the pre-eternity of the world, because from the pre-eternity of the cause follows the pre-eternity of the effect, and they denied of Allah, exalted, His essential attributes, and it is a false and blasphemous doctrine, to other things, as he indicated. And among what indicates its falsity is the diversity of the world into different kinds: some of it inanimate, some alive, some dark, some luminous, some sweet, some bitter. The Mighty Book in many verses: He said, exalted: 'Watered with one water, and We make some of them excel others in fruit. Indeed in that are signs for a people who reason.' This indicates that these losers are not intelligent, for the action of cause and nature is only one thing, not diverse. Do they not look at the camels how they are created? And at the sky how it is raised? And at the mountains how they are set up? And at the earth how it is spread out? Have they not looked at the heaven above them, how We built it and adorned it, and it has no fissures? And the earth We spread out, and cast therein firm mountains, and caused to grow therein every beautiful kind? But whoever Allah leaves astray has no guide. And among what they built upon their doctrine is the denial of bodily resurrection. And they adorned their doctrines with speculative imaginary doubts, like a mirage in a desert which the thirsty supposes is water until when he comes to it he finds it nothing. So they went astray and led astray until many people thought that these adornments are knowledge, rather they preferred those who adhered to them over the scholars of Sharia. No! They will know, then no! They will know.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that God is a necessary cause (فاعل بالعلة) or a natural cause (فاعل بالطبع). The author begins by explaining the phrase from the original poem: 'فهو الإله' (So He is al-Ilah), defining al-Ilah as the One truly worshipped, and 'الفاعل المختار' (the Free Agent), meaning that God does as He wills—He acts when He wills and refrains when He wills. This is supported by the Quranic verse: 'Your Lord creates what He wills and chooses.' The key point is that God is not a doer by nature or by cause, which is the position of the accursed philosophers. Because they claim that God is a necessary cause, they are forced to assert the pre-eternity of the world (since a necessary cause entails a necessary effect), and they also deny God's essential attributes. The author declares this doctrine false and blasphemous. Then he provides evidence for its falsity: the diversity of the world. The world consists of many different kinds—inanimate, animate, dark, luminous, sweet, bitter—yet they all come from the same water, as the Quran says: 'Watered with one water, and We make some of them excel others in fruit.' This diversity cannot result from a single cause that produces only one effect; it must be from a free agent who chooses to differentiate. Thus those who deny this are not intelligent. The author then cites more Quranic verses calling for reflection on creation. He also mentions that the philosophers' doctrine leads to the denial of bodily resurrection. Finally, he describes how the philosophers have adorned their false doctrines with deceptive, speculative doubts, like a mirage, leading many people astray even to the point of preferring them over scholars of Sharia. The passage ends with a stern warning: 'No! They will know, then no! They will know.'

Key Terms

فاعل بالطبع
فاعل بالعلة
الفلاسفة الملعونون
قدَم العالم
المعاد الجسماني
صفاته الذاتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's action is necessitated like natural causation, i.e., He cannot do otherwise.
Correction: God is a free agent; He acts by will and choice, as proven by the diversity of creation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what evidence does Dardeer present to refute the philosophers' claim that God is a necessary cause?
A) The pre-eternity of the world
B) The diversity of the world
C) The denial of bodily resurrection
D) The philosophers' own statements

Key Takeaway
The diversity of creation proves that God is a free agent who acts by will, not by nature or necessary cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the diversity in creation—sweet and bitter, light and dark—do you truly see it as a sign that God is a free agent, or do you unconsciously treat the world as governed by impersonal, necessary laws? In what area of your life today has familiarity dulled your wonder at the Creator's free choice? Decide one specific natural diversity to ponder tonight, and let it lead you to praise Allah for His absolute freedom.
?
The philosophers were led astray by their own speculations, preferring a mirage over the clear signs of the Quran. In your own pursuit of knowledge, have you ever prioritized clever arguments or worldly wisdom over the straightforward guidance of revelation? Where might you be trusting your own reasoning more than the words of Allah? Repent and commit to placing scripture above speculation.
?
The passage warns that many people preferred the philosophers over the scholars of Sharia. Is there any area in your life where you value secular expertise or popular opinion more than the teachings of Islam? How can you realign your priorities so that the truth from the Quran and Sunnah becomes your ultimate authority? List one practical step you will take to honor Islamic scholarship over cultural or academic fads.
